YOUR CHOICC
is called into question pretty nearly every day you live, in some matter or another. Tt may be
business or pleasure, a question of eating, drinking, or even smoking. And if the latter event
calls for a decision you will not find it difficult. You will consider that cigarettes are the nicest
and choicest way of enjoying tobacco, and you will remember that one cigarette in particular
provides, without exaggeration, every qualification as to quality that an experienced smoker requires.
Yodr Choice will be a “ CAMEO.”
Won’t it ?
THE RED PAGE .
There is something of the attraction of
battle-literature in the ‘ Australian Medical
Journal’—which falls nothing behind the
‘ British Medical Journal ’ in interest, and
only so far in value as may be expected from
a comparison of the populations dealt with.
A physician is a lighter, overcoming hostile
disease with a Napoleonic combination of
drugs. A surgeon is rather an explorer : he
sees hi 3 Nile of symptoms, and pushes on-
ward to their source in lesion or degenera-
tion. And diagnosis has all the fascination
of detective-work ; it is less a matter of
knowledge than of acumen, intuition, per-
sonal genius ; while its triumphs bring the
intellectual pleasure of an astronomer who
deduces new planets from a mathematical
hypothesis. The reader of medical records
is able to combine a war-correspondent’s
fever with a chess-expert’s enthusiasm ; and
to leaven the lump with that ‘ human inter-
est ’ which comes from knowledge that the
experiments are being made not ‘ in corpore
vili,’ but upon ‘the beauty of the world, the
paragon of animals.’ And if this statement
of the fact be inaccurate and roundabout, the
fact is still indubitable ; medical literature,
even or especially in the shape of quack
advertisements, is most seductive reading for
the million. It is disease-talk in excelsis ;
and the only time many people feel really
well is when they are talking about their
diseases.
» * • *
Most of the contributors to the ‘A.M.J.’
seem men of details, occupied and pre-occu-
pied with the study of cases and phases, and
rarely ascending to the consideration of laws
and principles. But in the June number
Dr. J. Murray-Gibbes had a statement of
‘the philosophy of disease,’ somewhat lack-
ing in theoretical novelty and literary
lucidity, yet full of suggestion and stimulus.
Dr. Murray-Gibbes argued, in effect, that
disease is health ; since, generally and
essentially speaking, disease is a natural re-
action from the effects of noxious agents.
And incidentally he states or re-states many
truths which are little appreciated even by
intelligent people— e.g. : ‘ Those who are
most liable to digestive disturbances, as
dyspepsia, are frequently the least afflicted
with constitutional diseases, for the reactions
of the digestive canal prevent the admission
of the causes producing them. Those, also,
who are liable to constitutional diseases are
frequently free from any great disturbance
of the organs of digestion.’ But affirma-
tions such as this— ‘ No drugs, even includ-
ing stimulants, add to the nourishment of
the body ’ —are altogether generous ; and
the paper throughout must be read eclecti-
cally.
9 $ |i I
An excellent essay of another kind, yet
also connected with a neat generalisation,
was contributed to the July ‘A.M.J.,’ by
Dr. A. Mueller, of Yackanflandah (Vie.),
the apostle of the strychnin hure for snake-
bite. Dr. Mueller now advances perehloride
of mercury as a cure for typhoid fever and
other germ-diseases. His preliminaries seem
solid—
The surgeon carries on a constant war with
disease germs, keeps them at arm’s length from
the wounds he makes or treats, and, if they have
sneaked in despite his vigilance, attacks them
vigorously, and exterminates them. The ptvysician
acts on very different lines when certain symptoms
tell him that certain germs have invaded the
system ; he leaves the germs severely alone, as if
they were on sacred ground, letting them do as
much mischief as they can whilst he puts all his
ingenuity to the task of treating the symptoms ;
trusting to the vis medicatrix natures , supported
by appropriate nourishment, to carry his patient
through the long and serious illness which in-
variably results, and never dreaming that this ill-
ness could have been nipped in the bud, and
deprived of all its dangers, by a vigorous germi-
cide treatment in the first stages.
And Dr. Mueller relates how a posteriori
justifies a priori —
The fact that a strong germicide, such as the
perehloride of mercury, will exert its powerful
action on the germs of certain diseases—notably
those of diphtheria, influenza, and scarlatina—
after they have been introduced into the system
and set up disease, has been known to me for
years, and I have acted on this knowledge with a
uniformly favorable result. In diphtheria I give
the drug in combination with Iron, and dispense
with any looal treatment, except syringing the
fauces with Condy’s fluid as soon as the mem-
branes ootnmence to slough. This takes place
almost always towards the third day of the treat-
ment ; there are no secondary growths, and re-
covery proceeds forthwith. Influenza also yielc s
readily to the Perchlonde given in combinatio
with Liq. Jaborandi every two hours, until profu
perspiration breaks out. When this ceases th
temperature usually goes down to normal, bu
rises again on the following day. The Perchloric e
mixture is then taken again with the same resul
and but rarely a third course of it is required. O
the fourth day the patient, though rather weak,
able to dress and walk about. In scarlatina th
Perchloride does not interfere with the eruption
but rather pushes it out; but the latter is invar
ably of shorter duration, and more quickly followec
by desquamation. The temperature is lowered
all cases, and the troublesome sequelae do no i
occur. The treatment of all three diseases wit
Perchloride, in fact, has beoome a mere matter o: r
routine with me, and they give me no trouble
Sore gums, diarrhoea, etc., are very rarely ob
served, as the highest dose for an adult is one
sixteenth part of a grain, and to children,
course, less in proportion to age and constitution
* « * *
Space fails for following Dr. Mueller int
details of his application of the perchloric!
in typhoid fever—with the result that when
the disease was taken early the patient wa
generally out of bed in a week, and was eat
ing solid food almost at the beginning o :
convalescence ; while when the germs hac
firmer hold the dangerous symptoms were
fewer or absent, and the cure was more
rapid than by the ordinary treatment. Dr
Mueller asserts that when generally adopted
perchloride ‘ will reduce the death-rate froir.
typhoid to a minimum hitherto unknown
and undreamt-of ’ ; and his statement anc
results seem of high scientific importance.
* * * *
An interesting paper in Dr. G-. Y. Poore’s
‘ Nervous Affections of the Hand and Other
Clinical Studies (Lond., 1897) quotes Sir
James Paget’s calculation of the cerebra
impressions and transmissions involved in
playing the piano quickly—
He remembered once hearing Mdlle. Janobha
play a presto by Mendelssohn, and he counted the
notes and the time occupied. She played 5595
notes in 4mins. 3secs. It seemed startling, but
let them look at it in the fair amount of its won-
der. Every one of those notes involved certain
movements of a finger, at least two, and many 6
them involved an additional movement laterally
as well as those up and down. They also in-
volved repeated movements of the wrists, elbows,
and arms, altogether probably not less than one
movement for each pote. Therefore there were
three distinct movements for each note. As there
were 24 notes per second, and each of those notes
involved three distinct musical movements, that
amounted to 72 movements in each aeoond. More-
over, each of those notes was determined by the
will to a chosen place, with a certain force, at a
certain time, and with a certain duration.
Therefore there were four distinct qualities
in each of the 72 movements in each
second. Such were the transmissions outwards.
And all those were conditional on consciousness
of the position of each hand and each finger before
it was moved, and, while moving it, the sound of
each note and the force of each touch. Therefore,
there I ** were three conscious sensations for every
note. There were 72 transmissions per second,
144 to and fro, and those with constant change of
quality. Let them imagine it in telegraph wires.
And then, added to that, all the time the memory
was remembering each note in its due time and
place, and was exercised in the comparison of it
with others that came before. So that it would
be fair to say that there were not less than 200
transmissions of nerve force to and from the brain
outwards and inwards every seoond, and during
the whole of that time judgment was being
exercised as to whether the music was being
played worse or better than before, and the mind
was conscious of some of the emotions which the
music was intended to impress.
It must have occurred to many people who
attended the Hambourg concerts that the
chief—almost the only—interest of the elder
brother’s playing came in his brilliant tech-
nique ; and that the marvel of it was not a
musical marvel, but a cerebral marvel akin
to that which astonished Sir James Paget in
Mdlle. Janotha. Where something of musi-
cal inspiration lay in his notes, young Ham-
bourg usually succeeded in translating it
into sound ; but he added no emotion of his
own, created nothing, repeated as mechani-
cally as a talking parrot. The reason is pro-
bably that he had no emotion to give : he is
physically and cerebrally immature, and pro-
bably more immature than the average youth
of his age, because his cerebral force has been
concentrated upon the acquirement of a ve
difficult technique. His ‘ boyish appearance
was frequently matter for newspaper cor
ment ; and on nearly all questions but mus
he talked like a child ; the interviewers oou
make, nothing of him. Neil Gow’s test o: ;
a fiddler was that he could ‘ gar himse ’
greet ’; but one cannot imagine Mark Han
bourg making himself ‘ greet ’ with his pian
And if you consider of what value th
brightest lad of 18 is in literature or printing
or in any other form of art, or in any sphere
of business, you will have something like
measure of Mark Hambourg’s musical value
Maturity may bring him power ; at presen
he is only a clever mechanic. And piano
people must be held to the same standard
other craftsmentheir artistic method i
practically worthless unless they can get th
inspiring force of artistic results.
* * * *
Dr. Poore’s book is a collection of shor;
■papers on a variety of subjects. Those on
the cramps and neuroses arising from cere
bral trouble are perhaps the most acute ant
valuable. In two or three places Dr. Poor
incidentally discusses the interesting ques
tion whether there is a special brain-centr
for writing, as for sight, hearing, &c.— i.e.
whether the writing faculty has become
sort of sixth sense, to the control of which
special area in the brain is exclusively de
voted. Histologically, this seems likely
enough. If the habit of speech has deveiopec
co-ordinating centres for speech, then the
habit of writing—in acquiring which so large
a part of modern life is spent—may well leave
its impression on the, plastic brain of child
hood ; and whether the impression be directly
inherited or not, there is no doubt that the
constant reiteration of impressions on the
brain of generation after generation tends to
produce a susceptibility to similar impressions
which by degrees may develop into a dis-
tinct localisation of capacity. Some such
graphic localisation is probably in the course
of fixation in the educated race-brain at the
present time ; but in European countries the
practice of writing has not been continues
sufficiently long to permit definite conclu-
sions as to the exact extent of the modifica-
tion of the brain, or as to how far this
modification is handed down from parents to
children. But a series of observations has
shown that the unconscious movements of
the muscles in writing are controlled by a
number of brain-cells situated at or near
what is called Broca’s convolution on the left
side. In dealing with writer’s cramp, Dr.
Poore says :
The frequency of writer’s cramp is due, in the
first place, to the unwholesome life of a clerk who
spends his days in a stuffy office in a crowded
city. Secondly, it is due to the fact that the hold-
ing of a pen involves the continuous contraction of
the muscles of pen-prehension, which get tired
out, just as our arms get tired when we hold them
rigidly extended, and as our legs get tired when
we stand in one position for long periods !
The only remedy for this is to teach the child to
write with both hands, which, I believe, might
easily be done. The writing would have to be
upright instead of slanting, and, with a pen in
both hands, the left half of the line might be
written with the left hand and the right half with
the right. We educate the left hand far too little.
Girls in this respect are better off than boys, for
such exercises as piano-playing and knitting en-
courage a great amount of ambidexterity in girls.
Why should not the clerk use either hand alter-
nately. and so give to each its much-needed rest ?
What effect has the exercise of the periphery
upon the development of the centre ? By con-
stantly using the left hand for written language
might we not possibly educate our right Broca’s
convolution instead of letting it lie idle ?
I once gave utterance to these opinions and
speculations at the bedside, and at. their con-
clusion a student of more than ordinary intelli-
gence asked me whether the extreme fluency of
the female organs of speech might not be due to
the fact that the right side of women’s brains had
been developed by a more constant use of the left
hand than is common in males?
Doubtless the periphery does re-act on the
brain and spinal cord ; every reflex centre is
an expression of such a re-action become
habitual and involuntary. Thus the act of
walking is unconsciously performed because
the repetition of the peripheral movement
has permanently impressed the co-ordinating
centres. It is probable that an infant’s
attempts to walk are more or less voluntary
imitations of the walking of others; and
they are po.ssibly controlled by brain-centres
quite other than those to which, through, the
influence of the periphery, the performance of
the act is finally relegated. Quite likely the
infantile brain inherits no active power of
co-ordinating the walking movements, but
merely a latent capacity, which is developed
and confirmed by habit. In early life, every
step modifies or strengthens a brain-cell.
And so with every other often-repeated act
or idea ; voluntary..becomes involuntary, and
involuntary becomes unconscious. The act
of knitting necessitates at first an imitation
which concentrates the whole power of the
brain ; but by dint of continual repetition it
becomes reflex, and many old ladies knit
like machines, and have their brains avail-
able for other matters. It would probably be
quite correct to say that such persons have
developed a knitting centre in the cerebrum,
which does not permit them to miss a stitch,
although they ‘ never count the stitches.’ In
the same way, an expert piano-player, play-
ing a piece to which he is accustomed, will
often say that he ‘ cannot sound a wrong
note ’ —no matter what the speed or the intri-
cacy. The power of sounding wrong notes is,
in fact, put out of his control by the reflex
centres which the periphery has trained;
unless he violently uses higher centres in
opposition.
* * * •
But the remark of Dr. Poore’s student is
not so acute as it appears to have seemed to
Dr. Poore ; for peripheral habits breed in
the brain only the power of continuing those
habits with less and less voluntary exertion
—not an increased power to perform actions
essentially different. Thus the practice of
using the left hand to play the piano can
have no effect whatever on their power of
speech ; it can only increase the power of
using the left hand to play the piano, or of
performing, other acts in which the muscles
are brought into play in a way more or less
similar. Female loquacity is probably due
chiefly to the greater affectability of the
female brain—which is, as it were, hung
more loosely than the male, and reacts more
easily to evanescent impressions. This
affectability is probably due in part to local
heredity and education ; but chiefly to
the affectability of the whole female
organism, with its comparatively unsteady
life-curve. Not only does the average woman
talk more than the average man ; but the
average woman-who-writes writes more than
the average man-who-wfites—and she needs
less provocation in incident or idea And it
is not in evidence that male piano-players
are particularly talkative, or that women are
less talkative when they have never learned
to play the piano or to knit. Women are
more talkative than men because, in the first
place, they are built that way—a slighter
stimulus suffices to produce the mental re-
action which sets their speech-organs in
motion; and because, in the second place,
their brains, as a rule, are not exhausted by
regular or protracted labor, and they have
consequently plenty of energy to spare for
slight stimuli—which are also more abundant
than graver ones ; and because, in the third
place, talk begets talk— i.e., the child accus-
tomed to utter its emotions on slight provo-
cation tends to need always slighter provoca-
tion—or no provocation at all. Many women
appear to talk as they walk —by something
very like reflex action.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Melb. Jews, greatly troubled over
the apparent fact that proselytising has been
carried on by certain rabbis, have motioned
“ That ministers of the congegation shall not
assist in making converts without the permission
of the majority of the members present at a meet-
ing convened to consider the matter.” What are
Cohen and Isaacstein afraid of ? A rush of com-
petition in money-lending or pawnbroking ? The
House of Israel has for centuries been a close
shindy kit, but it would be one of the oddest
developments of this hothouse-age if a red-headed
Moses O’Finn or Phelim O’Cohen, with a thick
brogue and turned-up nose, should become rabbi
of the congregation.
FRIENDS.
We had boiled our quarts together, by lagoons in
summer weather ;
We had ridden side by side behind the scrub-
bers in the. night.
In all the rounds of station life we two were
linked together,
Till they called us “Dave and Jonathan,” and
surely they were right !
That vyinter when I fell so sick you nursed me
like a brother
Through hours of sore complaining when the
pain was hard to bear ;
You had the light and gentle touch and kindness
of a mother,
And all a wife’s fond thoughtfulness and unre-
mitting care.
She was just a slip of girlhood then—but girls’eyes
can work evil—
Even Samson by a woman fell, and we are not
the first
Who, holding faith through ups and downs and
Fortune’s worst upheaval,
Have put the Best of Friendship by for Love
that was the Worst!
And she’s vanished now and left us with a scent
of dead white roses—
Faint mem’ries of the thrilling touch of wander-
ing finger-tips ;
Mere tantalising, agonising hopes the mind dis-
closes,
But never warmth of woman’s mouth against
the bearded lips.
And somewhere in the world she is—surprising
and undoing;
She is somewhere wrecking friendship’s faith
and breaking human lives ;
And yet in dreams the longing heart those light
steps is pursuing,
And the phantom of remembrance ever onward,
onward drives !
Q.
M. BURKINSHAW.
A WAIL FROM THE WEST.
The first rush at Bayley’s was over; Kurnalpi
had started to boom,
And we’d all got a bit in our pockets, though for
more there was plenty of room ;
We were glad, in a way, and contented—though
our veins held the gold-fever still — .»
That which even the typhoid respected, and not
even blank failure could kill.
I’d picked up a slug in the morning, and I thought
we were in for a haul;
And I’d spent half a thousand in fancy, but
castles have always a fall.
The camp-fire was very nigh dyin’ when Joe he
comes in with a run,
And he shows me a glitterin’ beauty—-’twas at
least fifty ounce to the ton.
He described me the place where he’d found it,
when napping a reef white as snow,
Away round the hill at the back there—just aside
of the ironstone-blow.
He’d put in his pegs ere he left it—prospectors’
good “ twenty-four square ” ;
With the thirty days’ grace to “ develop ” —then
“take up,” if prospects are fair.
With the first streak of dawn I was movin’; I
wanted to see what we’d got,
For I knew, if his tale stood the provin’, we were
in for a nice little pot.
We soon reached the site of our treasure —with
the outcrop of reef clear and bold—
When “ My God ! ” muttered Joe, “ here’s a facer!
Two coves nappin’ stones on my gold ! ”
“You’re certain you pegged, Bill?” I asked him,
looking round for a sign of the sticks.
“Assure as I live,” was the answer; “and we
ain’t standin’ none of these tricks.
“ Do yer know that you’re trespassin’, matey, on a
claim that was pegged out by me ?
You’re a-picking between my four corners, and the
gold in her’s mine, do ye see! ”
The intruders were both young in summers, but in
wickedness older than we,
And the younger, a lad about twenty, wheeled
round, and stared brazen at me.
If there’s one thing that riles me like thunder, it
is seeing a kid ape the man ;
And it sticks in my throat to be answered—l must
take him down if I can !
“ You reckon you pegged,” said the youngster ;
“are you lyin’ or dreamin’, old bloke !
Why, the pegs there are mine and my comrade’s ”
—and he grinned in my face as he spoke.
“ We’ve applied ; and we reckon we’ll get it—the
Warden we’ve got up our sleeve !
You’ve no ghost of a show here, my pippin ! so if
you’re quite through you can leave.”
Joe’s face went the color of crimson ; it was cornin’,
I knew, by his look :
“ You’re a liar ! ” he cried, with a straight-un, and
down went the lad like a book.
But the youngster was game as a pebble; he
fought like a tiger at bay,
And for three or four rounds they were level, until
Joe brought his weight into play.
Then a left made the young fellow wobble, and a
feint for the wind dropped his guard,
When —“ whish ! ” went Joe’s right for the finish
—the battle was won by my pard.
But they tackled us next with the lawyers, and
’twas they that had this time the luck,
And they’re making their pile at Kurnalpi—while
we’re hunting “ weights ” for our “chuck.”
Coolgardie.
FRANK HERBERT.

A BARE SUGGESTION.
As the Indian trip is off why not let the N.S.W. Lancers quench their blood-thirst as above? More exciting and useful than tent-pegging or lemon-cutting, and less
dangerous than spearing Swatis.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or ether Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission
To Amateurs in Jilaclc and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 9, 1897.
The Indian Contingent.
It would be interesting to know the exact
variety of blasphemy which was evoked in
British military circles by the offer of 100
Cornstalk warriors to go and help the
“Empire” in its troubles upon the Indo-
Afghan border. The offer was received with
outward courtesy, and replied to with appar-
ent enthusiasm. Nevertheless, the harried
British functionaries probably felt very much
as Rothschild would do if, in the middle of
a big financial crisis, someone cabled to him
urgently, offering to place gd. with him on
fixed deposit provided the conditions were
satisfactory. The regular British army
apart from the multitudes of miscellaneous
armed niggers who have been enrolled and
drilled in various parts of the empire—is so
small by comparison that it is a standing
joke among other European nations. Still
it musters, on paper, about a-quarter of a
million. At the time when New South
Wales sent along its misguided offer a large,
indefinite trouble was looming in India—a
trouble which might, for all anyone knew to
the contrary, ultimately develope into a holy
war, and an upraising of the green banner of
the Prophet, and that general outbreak of
Islam which, it seems to be agreed, must come
some day, and which, when it comes, will
probably be the largest contract Europe has
had on hand since the days of Solyman the
Magnificent. There were no definite signs
of anything of this size, but still Islam is
an unknown quantity, and for a time the
trouble looked serious. The scarce Tommy
Atkins was being hustled to the front with
all possible celerity. There were signs of
unrest among that vast Hindoo population
whose name is mostly Ram Chunder and
things like that, as well as fierce uprisings in
the mountains among the turbulent Mahom-
medans, who are mainly described as Abdul-
something-or-other. It seemed possible for
a while that the Emir of Afghanistan was
urging on the insurrection, and that the
Sultan at Stamboul was sooling on the Emir,
and that the Tsar was whooping up the move-
ment from the rear. For a moment or two,
Britain was seriously reckoning up the
800,000 or 900,000 trained soldiers—British,
Sikhs, Ghoorkas, Pathans, Houssas, black,
white, and yellow men, half-breeds, mon-
grels and nondescripts—that it might be
possible to lay hands upon at the instant.
And when a large, self-complacent offer of
100 self-satisfied, half-trained men came along
just then the probabilities are that the first
and loudest remark was ‘ ‘ Damn ! ”
• • * *
Thc Bulletin, at all events, feels inclined
to bet that the remark which came first to
hand was “ Damn ! ” The situation called
for something of the kind. If the sudden
rising of half-a-dozen or more lighting tribes
on the Indian borderland meant nothing
more than that these same tribes had a
desire for a casual day out, then there
was no real emergency at all. But the
unusual nature of the offer implied that
an emergency was supposed to exist. And
if there was a genuine trouble behind —the
sort of trouble that might be there —100,000
men would be badly wanted, and the 100
offered weren’t the tenth part of a drop in a
tank. Also there is an idea afloat in many
quarters that the British Government had
almost enough of Cornstalk assistance at the
Soudan time. The Australian, having never
seen real war or real butchery, looks upon
soldiering in somewhat of a holiday spirit,
and is apt, from pure want of experience, to re-
gard it as rather like Chowder on an enlarged
scale. And there are reasons why he should
not be too roughly undeceived as to the
Chowder aspect of things. It is the fashion
just now in England to brag violently about
“the colonies” ; the Little Englander cult
is practically dead, and the Imperial senti-
ment is the fashion, and the “ silken bond ”
disease is in a violent state of development.
Britain is furiously joyful at present about
the vastness of the Jubilee empire, and
kipples concerning it in a good deal of verse.
The present tendency is, therefore, to speak
kindly to “the colonies” and offer them
milk in a saucer on the mat. Consequently,
the offer of a miscroscopic scrap of armament
had —out of deference to the sentiment of
the moment —to be received with studied
outward courtesy, and if the 100 soldiers do
really go, and they have to be sent to the
front—assuming there is any front by that
time to send them to—the person in com-
mand will doubtless have to make a great
effort to look as if he took them seriously.
♦ * * »
All the same, if the trouble developes into
anything really great and dangerous, it will
be very difficult to take 100 men seri-
ously—especially if they are accompanied
by four or five correspondents to telegraph
about their doings and experiences, and are
despatched with the blessing of a whole con-
tinent as expressed in several hundred lead-
ing articles, and come liberally provided, like
the other famous Contingent, with tinned
turkey and potted ox-tongue, to say no-
thing of cases of opoponax and bottled hock.
Supposing something like Armageddon on a
small scale breaks out in the passes, and
things go as they usually do on such
occasions, and the defiles are crowded with
broken-down commissariat arrangements,
and camel-trains, and mule-trains, and bogged
artillery, and everything is one great effort
to shove and curse and hustle the motley
army through. Then the presence of a
small squad of amateurs who are supposed
to be treated with some special considera-
tion because they are a sign of the loyal en-
thusiasm of Australia will probably be a
great comfort to somebody. Most likely
that somebody would gladly exchange them
for as many nigger mercenaries who could be
cursed or have even stronger things done to
them if necessary, without the fact being at
once cabled to the Sydney Morning
Herald, and helping to create disloyalty, or
loosen the silken bonds, or arouse complica-
tions within the empire. In case of a parti-
cularly bad stampede some very unpleasant
things often happen in war to the worst
samples among the stampeders. If the Aus-
tralians stampeded very badly, however, the
only visible thing to do would be to cable
that they had behaved with great valour.
The officer who, by doing something un-
pleasant yet necessary, turned off at the tap
that stream of Australian loyalty which had
just begun to dribble, after Joseph Chamber-
lain had agonised so greatly at the Jubilee
to get the flow started, would probably make
himself considerably disliked for the rest of
his life. Under all the circumstances, if the
real inward soul-observation of the British
war authorities when the offer of 100 men
came to hand was not “ Damn ! ” it was
probably a good deal worse.
* * * *
The proposed contingent would be even a
trifle less useful to Australia than to Britain.
When the famous Soudan crowd went off on
the road to Khartoum 12 years ago, with its
Gladstone bag and its hair-wash and its
supply of bloater-paste and other refresh-
ments, one alleged reason for it was that it
would bring back much valuable military
knowledge for use in case of invasion. It
really brought back only an ass and a goat,
and the memory of much desert, and of
some casual views of the Mahdi’s spearmen.
The new contingent, if it goes at all, will
probably see, at the most, a little flurry
among the mountains with some irregular
tribesmen. If Australia ever is invaded it
will almost certainly be by very different
enemies with a very different mode of fight-
ing. And when the great trouble, which
comes to every country soon or late, does
arrive, and Australia makes a levy en masse
or something like that, and gets together
150,000 motley troops, there may be among
them an odd dozen or so of the Contingent,
the rest being dead or emigrated or super-
annuated. They will he present to stiffen
these raw legions with the experience they
gathered while scrimmaging with half-armed
irregulars among the passes, and to tell fish-
stories around the camp-fire of their one
bygone campaign. The idea of sending 100
men in the hope that they will gain
enough military experience to materially
assist in holding up a whole continent
in time of need is wildly ludicrous. If ex-
perience—really useful experience, that is,
not a mere accumulation of reminiscences—
is what is wanted, then the best way to get
it is to send 2000 men to bear a hand in the
next campaign against a scientific enemy,
and to send them on the ordinary conditions,
without the tinned turkey, or the wine, or
potted prawns, or the war-correspondents,
or special artists, or other glories. And, if
the idea is mere grovel and jingoism, then
one man in uniform is enough to send, with
another man to wheel along the refresh-
ments and luggage and the illuminated
address in a barrow. The proposed 100 men
are too few for a military force and too many
for a deputation. Besides, the war is ap-
parently about over, in any case.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Gentleman Who Is Away.
The motion of Glassey, M.L.A., that the re-
port of the Queensland National Bank Investi-
gation Committee should be presented to
Parliament immediately, instead of being hung
up to await the convenience of the Gentleman
Who Is Away, was lost by only three votes.
Twenty-four members voted for having the
Queensland National stink uncorked im-
mediately, and Horace Tozer only contrived
to muster 27 votes on the other side. It was a
decidedly close thing, and but for the manage-
ment of Tozer the chances are that the Govern-
ment would have been badly beaten. Tozer, as
a rule, does not manage things skilfully, but in
this case there was such an obvious way out
that only a phenomenal blunderer could miss
it. Glassey’s attitude was that the report
should be presented immediately. Acting-Pre-
mier Horace replied that the Ministry had no
desire to conceal anything, or to aid in any
wrong-doing, and that it wouldn’t bolster up
any shady bank for the world, or help to cover
up the misdeeds of anybody, or lend itself to
wrongfully shelter the Gentleman Who Is
Away, or any other gentleman. It was only
the aggressive purity and incredible spot-
lessness of the Government which made
it resist Glassey’s demand. Parliament
had entrusted a certain committee with the
business of overhauling the Q. N. Bank. Its
business was to overhaul it thoroughly at any
cost of time and trouble. When it presented
its report Tozer would lay it on the table right
away, but he objected to hurrying the com-
mittee lest it should thereby be induced to send
in its report before it had dragged everything
into the light, and had quite finished blasting
the character of everybody who deserved to
have his character blasted in the matter. It
was such a nobly patriotic way of putting it
that a small majority stood by Tozer, and saw
him through with difficulty. The one point
which was carefully omitted was this : the
committee stated some time ago that its report
was ready, but that before presenting the docu-
ment it wanted formal protection against any
libel proceedings to which it might give rise.
It hasn’t got the formal protection, and the re-
port hasn’t been presented. If Tozer and co.
obtained from Parliament the protection asked
for they might then sit down and wait for
the report with some chance of its coming
along. Not having done this, Tozer and co.
profess to be anxiously waiting for the re-
port in a pure and noble spirit with a
reasonable certainty that they have done what
they can to prevent it corning at all. Two
things that are very doubtful in the matter,
however, are w'hether Glassey very badly
wanted the report to be presented, and whether
Tozer very badly wanted it to be suppressed.
Glassey’s attack was certainly by no means
strong. And on the other side it is probable
that, while the Government is so much
oppressed by the MTlwraith incubus that it
dare not openly do otherwise than help to
keep things dark, yet if, after having kept
friendly with the incubus by doing everything
possible to keep the report out of sight, the
said report insisted on appearing after all, and
the result was to blot MTlwraith out of
Queensland politics, there are several mem-
bers of the Ministry who would feel all
the better pleased MTlwraith is of no pos-
sible use to the Ministry now, and he would
probably have been thrown overboard long ago
if there was only a chance to do it without
anyone else being dragged along with him.
Kaiser Tozer.
“ A Queensland Citizen ” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin, — The “ in-and-out-running” of Kaiser
Tozer (Q.) is very difficult to “size up.” Endeavor to
stand mentally as a supporter of Tozer, an idolatrous,
blind champion of him, and the peculiarities of his
actions become more mystifying than ever. Here is a
presentment of the aspect from that peephole. I am a
lozer supporter, prepared to circumvent the Opposition
(which is practically the Q. Labor-party) all I know, and
in every way in which the Tozer index-finger points. It
is my particular gag at this juncture to declare that the
Labor-party is impertinently interfering with the smooth
and passionless course of justice by trying to compel the
qJ.JN. Bank Committee to present an incomplete report to
the Government. Labor-leader Glassey gives notice of

If Tozer were the de facto Kaiser, instead of the locum

which the mysteriousness of Kaiser Tozer’s actions ex-

Believe me, if the “gentleman not now in the country '

the snapping turtles of to-day would crawl behind his

who dared to say to him, “What doest thou?" Bully

made to compel him to alter it, he would curtly ami

I’m not going to do that. If you don’t like it, lump it, or

was beloved, and always had a loyal following, because it
was of no use for his backers to talk disloyally; they’d
have to be disloyal, and take the consequences. And the
old lion is now reigniny, in feeble health, at Guvment-
ouse ! I remember how he once made a public appearance
in Rockhampton that would have killed any politician of
less calibre. He displeased his audience* into interjec-

fied toko! And I remember the subsequent attempted
row in the House to try and draw the old badger. He

apology he dreamed of making ; “ Mr. Speaker, I’ve often
addressed this House with far more liquor in me than I
had up at Rockhampton, and I hope to do so often again ! ”
Kaiser Tozer ! verily thou almost persuadest me to be a
rat.—Yours, &c., Zum.

“Let Him Marry the Girl.”
Charles Hall, hanged at Bendigo a week
or two back, was a whole essay on the
futility of that great moral theory, “ Let him
marry the girl.” When two young people are
acquainted, and the acquaintance ends un-
wisely and not too well, and the man
refuses to shield the girl from unpleasant com-
ment by marriage, the general result is “affilia-
tion,” or “ maintenance,” or something of the
sort. Then a fatherly bench frequently urges
them to get married and be respectable ever
afterwards. If the bench doesn’t interfere,
the girl’s relatives usually do their best to
hustle them into matrimony. Considering the
circumstances, there is something wildly
grotesque in the idea of any man trying to
bounce the deceiver of his daughter into
taking possession of her, so that he
may get level with her for ever afterwards,
but orthodox Respectability is built that way.
Charles Hall’s marriage came about in this
fashion ; he refused to do the right thing, as it
is understood in the orthodoxy of Podsnap,
and it was only by hard coercion that he was
induced to permanently take over the girl he
had deceived, and make her an “ honest
woman,” and then jump on her for the rest of
her short life. She became respectable —and
dead. In the view of society, four years’
brutal ill - usage, followed by drowning, was
probably worth going through for the sake
of what respectability could be acquired
by marriage with a coarse, unlettered, and
altogether ruffianly footballer. In the bright
lexicon of Respectability, for a woman to
give her body once for love is so utterly dreadful
that the evil deed should be disguised after-
wards even by selling her body permanently to
an individual who only takes it under compul-
sion. On the face of things, to hand over any
young girl to a man who has deceived her and
then cast her off, and who only accepts her to
save himself from worse consequences, is
dropping her deliberately into hell so that she
may be Respectable and damned. But the
moral doctrine, “Let him marry the girl,
reckons it is worth while being damned to be
Respectable—even to be Respectable in the eyes
of a social circle which comprises one small
street and two alleys.

The Steel Rails Job.
The Reid Government’s steel-rail contract,
which suddenly came to a head last week, has
started the N.S.W. fiscal controversy again
in a violent form. The situation is this .
Steel rails are worth about £4 15s. in Europe,
and the cost of landing them in Sydney
is set down at another 15s. —total importe
cost a shade under £5 10s. Provided one
Mitchell, a special political friend of Premier
Reid, establishes the necessary works in
N.S.W., the Reid Government undertakes to
buy locally-manufactured rails of! him at
per ton, which is 10s. above imported price, am
is equal to 9 per cent. duty. The S.M. Heral
and Daily Telegraph allege with great bitter-
ness that this is not Protection, because ro
tection consists in imposing duties on impor s.
And in that sense it is not Protection , i
only Prohibition—as absolute Prohibition as n
the duty was made six billion per cent. 1
Government is practically the only user o s
rails, and when it refuses to accept the i° rei S
article at any price it literally shuts down
the imports altogether. Also, it is Protec.
in another sense : Protection not only cons
in imposing duties on imports but in g 1 " 1 »
bonuses and similar artificial encouragemen
local producers, and the Reid Governmen , )
giving 10s. per ton more than the present mar
price for 150,000 tons of rails, gives the n


industry a bonus of <£75,000. The S.M. Herald
comes in here with an especially painful ex-
plauat ion :
To tell us that to make such an arrangement is to adopt
Protection is to talk nonsense, and to set at nought all
the facts of the case. When we use the term Protection
in' political language as the designation of a policy we
mean, as the Protectionists know well, something other
than making contracts for the local supply of-materials.
What is meant is, not that the State shall arrange to buy
for its own requirements in the local market, but that the
State by means of a prohibitive fiscal tariff shall compel
the community to do so. Of course our Protectionists
know this quite well, and know also that the fact is
destructive of their statement that the steel rails con-
tract, if adopted, would mean Protection in the sense in
which they use the word every day of their lives. But
they say “ the principle is the same. ’ This statement is
as unfounded as the other. The principle involved in
giving preference to a local contract is dissimilar from
that implied in the exclusion by means of a tariff of im-
ported goods, inasmuch as the former imposes no limita-
tions on the freedom o f the individual or on ths freedom of
trade. It causes no difference in the general purchasing
power of a sovereign.
Yet in this case the State is plainly compelling
the community to buy its steel rails in the local
market—the only steel rails the community
wants to buy are for the railways. And it is
imposing limitations on the freedom of the indi-
vidual ; each individual, as part owner of the
State railways, is compelled to pay more for
his share of the rail supply in order to arti-
ficially coddle the industry of Premier Reid’s
friend Mitchell. And it not only imposes
limitations on the freedom of trade (in steel
rails), but it closes the trade altogether. Like-
wise it causes a difference of nearly 2s. in
the <£ in the “ general purchasing price of a
sovereign” the sovereign that the com-
munity generally had set aside for the pur-
chase of rails. The business is merely
Lyne’s pipe - contract over again—the same
contract which the Herald and Telegraph
have denounced off and on for four years ; with
the difference that Lyne paid an enhanced
price for his pipes to protect the local pipe
industry, because he said he believed in Pro-
tection, and Reid proposes doing the same to
protect the local rail industry because he says
he doesn’t believe in Protection. The only
difference lies in the mean shuffling and the
Ananias involved in the steel-rails business.
And, incidentally, the struggles of the two Syd-
ney morning papers to prove that Protection
isn’t Protection when it is done by G. Reid
show up the newspaper party hack—the indivi-
dual who has to prove that everything is as
pure as the driven snowball when his own
fellow does it, and that everything is blacker
than ink when the other fellow does it—in a
very melancholy light. Next to being a ’bus
horse in a ’bus that goes live miles for 2d. the
lot of the newspaper party-hack looks about the
most soulless on this globe.
Our Colonial Oath.
It is a fashion among Australian Judges to
lament about the “ great increase in perjury”
said to have arisen since the law allowed a
prisoner to give sworn evidence on his own be-
half. Several judicial ornaments even suggest
that this privilege should be abolished because
of the alleged increase of perjury to which it
gives rise. Apropos of this theory, The Bulle-
tin would like to know if the small additional
amount of false-swearing, as compared with
the very large amount which previously exis-
ted, is really a thing of any account. Under
the old system a prisoner almost always said
he was innocent, and when the law found him
guilty his statement was set down as lying.
Now he swears that he is innocent, and when
the law finds him guilty his swear is set down
as perjury. The moral difference lies in the
fact that the prisoner formerly lied without
kissing the microbe-infected volume, whereas
lie now lies with that unhealthy decoration
thrown in, and the alleged increase of perjury
consists wholly in the fact that the same pre-
sumably untrue story is told in a different way.
The amount of untruth, however, remains
exactly the same, and as plain falsehood seems
just as bad as the same thing with a moral
ham - frill on it, and adorned with allu-
sions to a Deity that neither the Judge nor
the accused really believes in, there is no
visible increase in any evil in particular. More-
over, The Bulletin reckons on good evidence
that the kind of Judge who is so much troubled
in his soul about the perjury done by prisoners is
a poor, conventional pretender at the best. When
an accused person swears that he is innocent
and the jury finds him guilty, that, in the eye
of ihe law, means that he has committed per-
jury, and the law is greatly shocked thereat.
In quite a large number of cases the law
emphasises its grief by prosecuting the poor
wretch for perjury, and getting him convicted a
second time, which probably means, in some
cases, that he was wrongly convicted in the
first place, and wrongly convicted for saying he
was innocent in the second. On the other
hand, it happens very often indeed that the
prisoner is acquitted, and that at times is equally
solid evidence that all the Crown witnesses who
swore he was guilty are perjurers. Has any
Judge ever complained about the truly alarm-
ing prevalence of perjury among Crown wit-
nesses? Or has any one of the Judges who
wants to prohibit prisoners from giving
evidence because the jury often decides,
in effect, that their evidence is untrue
—has any one of them ever suggested that
Crown witnesses should be similarly prohibited
because the jury often decides, in effect, that
they are false-swearers and gross perverters of
the truth ? The Bulletin smiles with a smile
that can be heard right out by Johanna Springs
or thereabouts. Going solely by the decisions
of juries—and there is generally nothing else to
go by—about twice or three times as many
Crown witnesses perjure themselves as prisoners,
and the average Judge is grieved in his inmost
soul about the small evil, and isn’t troubled in
the least about the large one. It is a curious
state of things.
The Bulletin’s recent remarks about the state
of things Fijian have moved Sydney Catholic
Press to a despairing eruption of mingled
hysterics and garble. This paper wasn’t
“ pleading the cause of Paganism,” or of any
other “ ism ” ; it was simply recording, for
what they were worth, the specific conclusions
of disinterested observers upon a given issue
with regard to a given people, and the cold,
hard facts of the case cauuot be shifted by any
quantity of illogical shriek about Nero, and
Domitian, and the iniquities, generally, of Im-
perial Rome. The question isn’t what paganism
did for Rome, but what so-called Christianity
has indisputably done for Fiji ; and, even put-
ting aside the extreme probability that the
20th century finds the world merely more
hypocritical than it was in the first —
and no whit inherently better, The Bul-
letin wasn’t advancing any general proposi-
tion when it asserted, as it asserts again,
that the conspicuous failure of soi-disant-
Christian dogma, as a moral and social re-
generator, has, in this particular instance, been
conclusively demonstrated. Whether it would
always and everywhere so ignominiouslv fail,
or whether in this case a better brand of the
article would have produced better results, are
matters with w'hich this paper didn’t concern
itself. It stuck simply to the adamantine, un-
hoi table facts, and to describe it on that account
as the “organ of infidelity,” or the organ of
anything else, is mere wilful perversion and
cheap fanatical howl.
Stump speech by “ Lictor,” a Bulletin corres-
pondent at Kalgoorlie:—
Mr. Finnerty, the Coolgardie R.M., has again been
presiding at Quarter Sessions, de.spite the remonstrances
of press and public and several disagreeable remarks
made bv jurymen at the previous sittings. Mr. Finnerty
had lately been making 60me show of concession to
popular opinion by openly declining in court to sit on
some preliminary examinations because he said he had
overheard jurymen remark that they would not find men
guilty if the R.M. first committed and afterwards
tried them. The R.M. perhaps meant well ; but surely
this was very like a hint to the other magistrates that he
considered there should be committals in the cases re-
ferred to. The policy of W. A. Govt, is that everything
they have to buy shall be with the smallest coin avail-
able, and what they have to sell with the largest.
The Supreme Court Judges, Wardens, and P.M’s are
miserably paid, as are also all the civil servants.
As a natural result, many of these last sell
departmental secrets and blackmail the public ; and some
of the least dishonest embezzle, and, when caught (which
is rarely on account of the cheap scheme of rackety super-
vision), are sent to penal servitude. The Government sell
exemptions on mining leases and charge an exorbitant
price, whilst, in civilised provinces, there is only a small
foe to pay cost of recording. A man cannot leave his
residence area for a holiday without paying, besides the
time he wastes in hanging round the court; and he has
to pay £2O for the freehold of a quarter-acre block on
which to live, in addition to numerous preliminary and
vexatious charges—same price for an area close to town
or miles away. If there is a worse hell anywhere than
W.A. the parsons may yet be shown to have been right
after al!

“ A Melbourne Pressman ” to The Bulletin :
What is very likely a mere outcome of the inhuman
piece-work system prevailing in so many industries is the
recent tragic explosion at the Colonial Ammunition Co.’s
works at Footsoray (Vic). Giving his version of the
catastrophe to the reporters of the daily press, the
manager, Captain Whitney, observed, ingenuously,
“ With a powder magazine and ammunition factory it is
always the unexpected that happens, and those who
work in and are connected with factories of the sort are
always liable to suddenly meet with some tragic end "
and announced that he was at a complete loss to account
for the accident. To persons, neither shareholders nor
directors nor employes of the factory, who have had an
opportunity of inspecting these works, the probable
cause is less obscure, and to them the wonder is that only
one explosion has taken place since the establishment of
the concern 12 years ago, and that only three girls have,
so far, lost their lives. In all, 50 girls are employed, the
majority being in comparative safety making cartridge-
cases, while the remainder pass their lives in deadly peril
filling and finishing. But one rule applies to all—the
iron rule of payment by results. According to the state-
ments published in the local Herald, the three girls who
were ihlled received respectively 10s., Bs., and 7s. per
week—a truly noble wage for which to work at racing-
speed and get blown to pieces in the end. But the girls
who work in the dangerous part of this factory are,
judging by their faces, figures, and dress, in a far more
comfortable position than the girls in the cartridge-
making room. This house is the most heartrending
industrial spectacle I have ever seen. The girls are all
ominously young—or else they are unnaturally stunted—
apparently ranging from 14 to 18. Very few grown
women, including the overseers, are met in the whole
establishment. These youngsters, dressed in most
poverty-stricken fashion, feed machines and feed them at
a pace that suggests the last lap of a cycle race —a
pace which must soon smash the nerves of every
worker. Every now’ and again a tired child will
raise her hands over her head and stretch her arms —
to return to w'ork with renewed speed to make up for the
fractional amount she has lost by leaving off—and here
and there are girls with bad scars on their arms and
hands, the result of some minor accident that never
reaches the press. But all are paid by results, and one of
the results of those results is the fearful deaths of Alice
M'Lkod, Elizabeth Greenham, arid Ettie Fitzpatrick.
Not one of these girls was 20 years old, two were but 17,
hut each had already cast upon her the responsibility of
assisting an aged relative. Knowing, consequently, how
their very living, and the livings of those they loved,
depended upon the number of cartridges put-
through the machine by the time the bell sounded, little
wonder is it that one out of the millions of cartridges
manipulated entered the machine a fraction of an inch
out of place ! Did the Colonial Ammunition Co. pay a
weekly wage, it w'ould probably have an output of 50 per
cent, iess, and very properly so, w’hen its employes now
cram two days’ work into one ; but it would have no big
accidents resulting from the hurry and worry the opera-
tives now endure. The blackened bodies of the three
girls, w'ho should have just been entering the happiest
period of their lives, are simply tributes laid by the High
Priest Piecework on the altar of Capital.
The awful stink that hangs round Victorian
J.P.-dom —especially in the matter of Factory
Act oases, where the cheap and nasty beak
openly sympathises with law - breaking em-
ployers and refuses to enforce the law—lately
moved Vic. Labor-party to enter a formal pro-
test against the administration of law by gratis
fat-men. It got very little satisfaction, how-
ever, Premier Turner merely stating that in
his opinion “ the majority of the members of
the bench did their work honestly and fairly.”
Which is probably quite true —and is certainly
quite beside the case. Nobody protested against
the majority which is honest and fair, but
against the large minority which is dishonest
and unfair, also ignorant, brutal, boorish, cor-
rupt, prejudiced and duuder : headed to boot.
The fact that there is a fair and honest majority
is no reason why the very considerable and
wholly-disgusting minority should pervert the
law without hindrance. There is a very large
majority of people who don’t commit murder,
but that isn’t considered as any reason why the
minority who do commit it shouldn’t be hanged.
Dear Bulletin, —I am not writing this from
“ in the know ; ” it is a mere hazard of deduc-
tion. First fact : Tozer has, all through the
late Q.N. Bank tempest, and also in the Willie
Stephens attack anent the Lands Commission,
artfully slid all the alleged blame upon “ Never-
mind ” Nelson by representing, sometimes
straight out and sometimes by insinuation, that
he (Tozer) was opposed personally to those
things which a majority of the Govt, favored.
Second fact: So pronounced was this method
of defence that watchful observers of the
game of politics as it is played in Leperland
have given the warning—" Look out for a
slump somewhere in the phalanx.” Third
fact: Tozer, ever since he took the head of
the table, has carved off titbits of flattery
to Glassey and has ladled lots of
"gravy out of the joint.” Fourth fact:
Glassey and other Labor members are
now credited with inspiring the politics of
Brisbane (Q.) Echo ; it is even hinted that some
of them own, or partly own, shares in it. At
any rate, the Echo is now booming that section
of the Q. Labor-party which the Worker is
not too sweet with. Fifth fact: The Echo
backed up Glassey and damned the Govt,
through this last Q.N. Bank business —right up
till last week. Sixth fact: The Echo the other
day found excuses for Tozer’s evident desire to
let things stand until Nelson returns, saying
—"He (Nelson) has made this business his
own, and should be called upon to add the final
chapter to his rather questionable work in
1893.” Question: What mean these weather-
signs ? The self-portraiture of Tozer ? The
buttering of Glassey? The Echo’s excusation
of Tozer? When shall these three meet again?
When the debonair "Nevermind” lounges
sleepily among the spring-traps which way
will the explosion go ? —Yours, Ow.

"F.” : "A fine alteration has been made in the
preamble of the 1891 Draft of Federal Consti-
tution by the 1897 Convention. The old pre-
amble commenced thus :
Whereas the Australasian colonies of ( ) have
agreed to unite in one Federal Commonwealth.
The new bill reads this way :
Whereas the people of ( ) have agreed to unite in
one indissoluble Federal Commonwealth.
The ‘ States rights ’ folk could not have noticed
this alteration. It gives away their whole case.
It is not the ‘ colonies ’ but the people who are
federating—and the claim that Tasmania shall
have equal voting power in the Senate with
N.S.W. is tliusly proved to be ‘salted’ and a
‘ duffer.’ If Vandeinonians are not con-
tent to be Australians, each Australian
being politically equal to every other
Australian, then let them stay out of a federa-
tion they will never appreciate. If South
Australians want principally to be South Aus-
tralians, let them, in the name of eternal
Bumble, continue in that parochial state while
N.B. W. and Victoria consummate a Federation
on equal terms. And what are we to do with
the slave State ? Queensland gets the credit of
having a white man’s heart, though the Kanaka
skin is so dusky. The now apparently inevit-
able election of Federal delegates will settle
this question, which is a most momentous one
in Australian affairs. (Mem. Australian
Federation, by Bumble out of Kanaka, a good
flatcatcher.).”

Vic. Minister of Railways Williams, after a
three years spell of astonishing failure, is ap-
parently to be returned unopposed by Eagle-
hawk to the House—but not to the Cabinet.
There has never been a Railways Minister like
him. Old John Woods was faddy, crotchety,
and a failure in big patches. The deplorable
C. E. Jones earned a reputation as an adminis-
trator that lives, in certain quarters, despite
mint-sauce incidents. Dunky Gillies was a
magnificent spendthrift. Wheeler tackled
Speight, and threw up the sponge. Shiels
took the job and threw out Speight, and left
on record a mass of unforgetably florid epistles
to the railway-boss. However, they all had
lucid intervals—but Williams has never at any
time done anything that pleased the Cabinet,
the railway-officials, or tlie public. His speeches
in the House were always the helpless drivellings
of out-and-out failure. He reduced failure to a
science. Premier Turner again and again was
compelled to relieve him of his duties in the
House, again and again had to make speeches
to obliterate the inevitable Williams fatuities.
Duffy’s incompetence in the Post-office has
been made endurable to many people by the
bald fiction that Duffy is the author of one or
two good jokes, though, apart from pretend-
ing to be a statesman, his jokes have always
been of the attenuated type. Moreover, Duffy
has some vague reputation by reason of the
many Post-office scandals, his daring silence in
the presence of shattering criticism being re-
garded as more or less heroic. But Williams
has not even the successful silence of stupidity
to his credit—and the only hope is that he will
attempt to explain his railway impolicy to
his constituents before nominations close.
Thereupon some local Chinese fossicker will be
run against him, and will win.
Listen to Tom Bent, the man who figured as
Speaker of Vic. Assembly before last general
election. Burly Thomas moves in a mysterious
way at Port Fairy, where his " dear old friend
and colleague,” Bryan O’Loghlen, was re-
placed by local candidate Duffus three years
ago. Thomas, at time of writing, couldn’t say
for certain whether he was opposing Duffus on
his own account, or merely waddling around
the district in the interests of dear old Bryan,
but his principles are full of awful significance,
anyhow. The wave of Young Victorian feeling
that swept the Speaker off his legs seems to
have left his principles unimpaired. " In allud-
ing to the various works he had done at Port
Fairy during the time he was Commissioner of
Public Works, &c., Mr. Bent said he had got
the large post-office for them, and all Mr.
Duffus had done was to get a pair of doors
put on, which proved too small for him (Mr.
Bent) to get through.” Vote for Bent, the
getter of the large post-office, and down with
Duffus, who merely added a too-small pair of
doors ! Also remember that it was Bent whom
Vic. Assembly delighted to honor, and clothe
him once again in his official wig and gown, and
place him upon the roof of the P.O, aforesaid,
and sing praises to his name !
The very light fines of 10s. and costs lately
imposed by Brisbane Police-court Bench on
Minister Dickson and other magnates for ille-
gally employing Kanakas contrast significantly
with those visited on farmers for slight breaches
of the Polynesian Act. The writer has seen
farmers in Bundaberg district fined £5, .£lO,
and £2O for illegally employing Kanakas, even
in field work—the illegality consisting in the
fact that a duly-attested agreement had not
been entered into before the Polynesian In-
spector. The Courier sort of blamed certain
Labor Ms.L.A. for sitting on the bench in the
Brisbane cases, insinuating that they went
there for the purpose of scorching the stylisli
defendants, but those in the know significantly
winked the other eye when that view of the
case was presented to them. The fines imposed
were so ridiculously low that it is impossible
for the prosecutor to have recovered the ex-
penses of initiating the proceedings—proceed
ings which were very badly needed indeed.
The decision of the Melb. Lormer-Gaunson
case by the Full Court against the teetotal
panjandrum who claimed the right of adjudicat-
ing in public-house licensing and other cases,
came as a surprise in legal circles, and it is by
no means clear that the ground of their Honor’s
judgment can, or will, be logically worked out.
If it is, much good will incidentally come of it,
for the squatter must cease adjudicating over
his neighbor’s shearers, the country store-
keeper over his rival’s debtors, the Orangeman
over Catholic rioters, the orthodox Christian
Judge must stand down in cases of blasphemous
libel, the believer in flogging in cases of garrot-
ting, every Judge in cases of contempt of court,
and a host of other such “ abusions of the
law ” must pack up their traps and depart
from Victoria. But it is seriously to be feared
that the grounds of this decision will be whittled
down by judicious “ distinguishing ” until it
embraces only those cases in which the otrend-
ing justice is a member of a reform movement
(or a fad movement, if you like), in which
the members of the Full Court for the time
being do not concur. For instance, is it likely
that a bench of Freemason Judges will hold
that a Freemason justice is incompetent to hold
the scales in a case between a Freemason and
an outsider? Hey! Had the Vic. court de-
cided the matter on the particular facts of Cold-
Tea Lokmer’s case alone, possibly they might
have been justified (but only possibly). But
the broad grounds given for their decision,
however desirable they may seem to some
minds, certainly appear to be a serious innova-
tion by the Judges on the principles of the
common law.
“ Public School-teacher ” to The Bulletin :
The “religious instruction” given by teachers in N.S.
Wales public schools is highly amusing to an impartial
onlooker, and the ideas of the children on the subject of
religion must be a most interesting miscellany. They
begin with a very young teacher in the infant class, who
gives them her notions on “The Creation,” and “The
Fall of Man,” impressing the eating of the “apple” by
the unhappy Eve and its consequences with great em-
phasis. The youngsters execrate Eve. Then they are
promoted to the immediate charge of the infant mistress,
who is an older woman and has sympathy with her own
sex or a bad opinion of the other one. She shows up
the glaring faults of Adam, passes lightly over the
templing of Joski'H, and refrains altogether from showing
how Ruth and Esther “hooked in” their husbands.
Young teachers invariably try to explain these matters,
not forgetting the birth of Isaac, and the effort would
make a cat laugh. The irreligious young man assistant
“shirks” his religious instruction, if possible ; if not he
“ sticks to the book,” and is asked some awkward ques-
tions by an occasional pupil who takes some interest in
the subject. The Methodist teacher inculcates the “ In-
spiration ” of the Word, and so attempts the gigantic
task of justifying the character of David, because it
is written he was “a man after God’s own heart”!
What a model for our future manhood ! Read
the dying David’s sanguinary charge to his son,
Solomon. No examinations are undergone by teachers
on religious subjects. Each gives his own ideas
or follows some other teacher’s lead. The result is
a wonderful “ free-and-easy,” and if religious instruction,
as per Scriptures, were any good the present farcical
teaching would be a thousand pities. Actually the moral
training of the school counteracts the immoral effect of
the Scripture-lessons and declares that the ways of the
ancient Jews, if passable in their day, are quite unsuit-
able to ours. Occasionally there is a teacher who sifts
the good from the bad, brings the light of the 19th cen-
tury to bear on the Bible as on any other old book, un-
hesitatingly condemns the hideous old Kings of Israel
and their pals and their doings, and explains “Jonaii
in the Whale” and such matters by saying that people
were so ignorant in those old days as to believe anything,
or else we are ignorant and have lost their meanings.
I’m a teacher of that sort but I abstain from religious in-
struction.
4.
The Victorian Government proposes attempting
to create a great tobacco industry by granting a
bonus of 3d. on every lb. of good tobacco ex-
ported—the cash payable to growers only. The
bonus system is a great grief, to Freetraders,
but there is no doubt it often acts well. The
huge butter-export industry of Victoria was
created by it. So was the beet-sugar industry
of France, Germany, and Austria. .So, in part,
was the shipping of several European countries.
So were most of England’s manufactures ; in
the days of 300 years back, when those primi-
tive industries first began to drift over to Eng-
land from Belgium, France, Italy and other
places, they were largely attracted by various
kinds of State assistance. Even that noble
animal, the English horse, arose from bonuses
and grandmotherly government, and beastly
state interference. The English horse used to
be a stunted animal in a disgraceful state of
disrepair, but Henry VIII. interfered by order-
ing the wholesale slaughter of all undersized
mares and stallions, and by giving bonuses for
animals which could stand inspection. The
bonus system may be a disreputable fraud, as
Freetraders allege, but it has done a lot of good
at different times.
“ Compton ” :
As to Ben. Tillett’s cocksureness. It’s an unfortunate
necessity of the position. An Australian audience is to
be moved only by positive assertion. “ Blanky ’is good,
and a wager is still better, but assertion—loud, vociferous
and undoubting —is the bedrock of it all. Some eight
years’ experience of chin-wagging to every variety
of Australian gathering have borne in strongly
upon me the utter futility of expecting any
allowance for the inevitable limitations of a lecturer’s
knowledge or opportunities. A successful Australian
speaker must know all things. The great god, Sport,
rules here as everywhere. A speaker, in Australian con-
ception, is a man putting up a record, and nothing else.
If there is anything he doesn’t know the effect is as
disastrous as would be ignorance by Fitzsimmons of the
approved method of breaking out of a corner. Come to
think of it, how is it. that Tiie Bulletin flourishes
so well, and is so generally accepted ? It isn’t through
any weakness in the way of expressing hesitation or
misgiving. As to Tillett’s disparaging references to
Australian unionism, as a fairly old unionist I support
him. Much has been done here, as you say. But we have
a present and a future (let us hope)—not a past. We
haven’t got enough margin over early days yet to quote
what has been ; while in England, so far as we" can judg'e,
the cause of the horny-handed is in a fair way. Our
unions are, with a few startling exceptions, moribund.
One policy of Q. Government seems to he
to promise increases of salary to those depart-
mental heads who can best “sweat” their subor-
dinates. “ Take £lO a-year off 20 of your officer.-
and we’ll raise your salary by £5O. ”
