“ At Duty’s Call,” the latest Lyceum pro-
duction, started on Monday night with eclat and
everything else it could lay its hands on. Re-
garded as a literary work it deserves much pro-
found disrespect; also, it lacks historical accuracy,
and the logic of its plot is knocked endways at
the beginning and knocked into a cocked hat at
the end, and the middle of it is chaos. But as a
spectacle it is very high-colored, and as a patriotic
howl it is above par. Haidly three minutes pass
without some enthusiastic remarks about the Flag.
The British soldier tramps through it in hundreds,
and then he gets round the back and tramps
through again till he amounts to thousands.
Alfred Woods, closely pursued by the overwhelm-
ing enemy, plunges from a great height into the
raging tank with the British flag round his waist,
and struggles through, damp but glorious. Maud
Williamson, being a prisoner in the towers of the
Egyptian oppressor and her honor threatened by
A. Boothnian, Esq., throws a torch into the
powder-magazine and blows the towers of the
oppressor in various directions. Alfred Woods
and Brother Charles (Brother Charles being a low
character who has joined the Egyptians) fight a
sanguinary duel on horseback; and when the
former has plunged into the tank with the
British flag Maud Williamson arrives on horse-
back and plugs the Copt army with a re-
volver promiscuously. It is sometimes diffi-
cult to tell exactly what the British soldier
is rushing at, but lie rushes furiously and shoots
“something tremenjous,” and, if his aim isn’t
any too good, his loyalty is a pure and spotless
emotion, mingled with drink, and he has a brass
band in front and another behind, and there are
seldom less than eight officers on horseback
sooling him on, and the explosions which take
place on the premises of the Egyptian are
alarming.
* * * *
The scenery of the new production may
fairly be set down as first-class, and the stage-
management— an important feature in a drama
where fortresses and tanks and entrenchments
are heaped up in great numbers—is very good.
As for plot, it is something about an English
gentleman, whose son proves not to be his son
after all, and an Italian lady, whose daughter is
really somebody else, and a British villain who
hears this from behind a plant in the conservatory.
In consequence of which he buys an estate
from the brother of the alleged mother of the
girl who wasn’t her daughter, and, to make
good his title, he arranges to get rid of the
alleged son, and creates evidence against him which
causes him to be sentenced to death by a colonel
who is really his father, and who is likewise the
lost husband of the lady who has the daughter
that isn’t hers. And it is by reason of these
things that Alfred Woods leads a precarious exist-
ence on the heaving tank, and Maud Williamson
blows up the Egyption fortress, and from the
same cause there is a tableau of 2000 British
soldiers at sunset in the desert illuminated by red
fire. Miss Williamson makes a rather pretty and
clever heroine, and Miss Ethel Grey—remem-
bered some years ago in Sydney as a beauty
actress of a rather wooden description—has de-
veloped into acapable performer, without losingany
of her good looks. About a dozen other people—
notably Alfred Woods, Brother Charles, Booth-
man, King Hedley, Harry Shine, and Miss Edith
Barrow— deserve more space than can be spared
this week, so they are held over in a friendly
spirit. Also Private Abraham Moss is treated
with a certain amount of kindly consideration as
the only Hebrew private on record.
** * *
The “Bunch of Keys” made a fair
start at the Palace, and if it doesn’t live up to it
that fact will be more the fault of the piece than
of the company. The company is excellent, and
the piece is moderately good, but thin. It belongs
to the “Chinatown” order, in which the writer
takes one idea —and a very small idea at that—and
hangs on it songs, dances, free fights, jokes, per-
sons falling downstairs with trunks, calisthenics,
beer, theology, spontaneous combustion, and any-
thing else he can think of. In the beginning it
transpires that old man Keys is dead; he was a tough
character in his life and in death he wasn’t divided
unless he died through a train going over him,
which matter remains in obscurity. He left a
hotel and three nieces, and instructions that his
executor shall keep the hotel going till the first
commercial traveller comes along, when the pro-
perty shall go to the ugliest girl of the three—the
bagman to decide. The act ends with two nieces
in hysterics at the prospect of being adjudged the
ugliest, and the third—the torn tomboy of the
crowd —putting a frog down the executor’s
back. The other two acts represent the
executor, George Lauri, solicitor, running
the hotel, and the three men who hope
to marry the three girls laying plans to
make the expected bagman “run stiff.” These acts
don’t help on the story in the least, but they show
that Lauri can’t run a hotel worth a cent. Among
other things, he puts the pig in the safe along
with the will, to prevent it making a row in the
yard at night; hangs himself unsuccessfully
through blighted affections; gets lamentably
drunk, and has a bad time with the functionary
whose duty it is to fall down stairs with the
guest’s trunk. And after everybody has disguised
himself to no particular purpose as somebody else,
and everybody has sung and danced, and the
stove has exploded, and the pig has got out of the
safe, the three girls agree to divide the property
after all. Also, the small towzled girl with the
frog doesn’t marry the executor.
* • • •
The brightest individual feature in a strong
company is Miss Lottie Moore—the same lady
whose neatly - executed somersault won golden
opinions at the Palace in the early Goatcher days.
She is a perfectly incidental character who has
nothing to do with the attenuated rag of plot,
being merely the kitchen Dolly in the hotel.
But she plays her little part admirably, and
dances marvellously, and somersaults in a
very short frock without anything happening
to cause a blush. George Lauri and Misses
Marietta Nash, Fanny Liddiard, and Addie Con-
yers are other people who do good service. A
new arrival, Willie Freear, falls downstairs credit-
ably with the trunk, though the present writer
. has seen the trunk fallen downstairs with a shade
better. The co. also includes Albert Bellman,
Robert Watson, comedian (who used to be Bobby
Watson in the old, irreverent days of melodrama),
and others. It is a really strong combination
for its size, and, seeing that the thin “ China-
town ” did so well, there seems no reason why the
“ Bunch of Keys ” should not do likewise.
** * *
George Rignold returned to Sydney and
opened his martial show at the Royal under pro-
mising conditions. “In the Ranks,” when com-
pared with some newer military spectacles, is full
of good points, and it is so long since it was last
seen in Sydney that it is almost as new as it ever
was. _ Ig is a military spectacle built on coherent
principles, and it doesn’t run on the familiar
theory that when a man puts on a red coat and
wraps his martial gun around him, he necessarily
becomes a lunatic, and conducts himself for ever
afterwards like an ant violently going nowhere in
great haste, with a load of something that is per-
fectly useless. The average dramatic soldier
is an utterly irresponsible person, whose
great idea is to rush at an impregnable
fortress, carrying his rifle in one hand, and
a dead elephant under his arm, and his
wounded officer on his back, and the Flag in his
other hand, while he sings “ God Save,” and
writes a letter to his aged mother simultaneously.
This sort of thing has happened so often that it is
pleasant to come across a military play which
has a motive to start with, and travels on under-
standable lines, “In the Ranks ” looks as pro-
bable as any four-act melodrama can afford to do ;
it is well-mounted, brightly colored, apd adorned
with a convincing villain named Gideon, a beauti-
ful heroine, and every other convenience. The
heroine is the only person who is absolutely new
to Sydney, and, so far as can be judged on a very
short acquaintance, Lilian Wheeler will be a
favorite with the gods. In addition to being a
clever actress, she looks as nice as her photograph,
which is a rare accomplishment.
** * *
“The French Maid,” after a long and
prosperous career at Her Majesty’s, closes at the
end of next week.
** * *
Alfred Dampier and his family and co.
farewelled at the Lyceum on Saturday, and are
now working their way towards Victoria. After
a brief season in Bendigo and elsewhere, they
further move on to England, partly for a rest and
partly to gather up new dramas, and for various
other reasons. The Lyceum audience parted with
Dampier reluctantly, and he exited in the midst
of a great ovation. He is understood to be due
here again some time next year, and the necessary
fatted calf is already in preparation.
** * *
Sydney Opera House, under the manage-
ment of Mr. W. F. Clitherow, is now open three
nights a week. “East Lynne” did a temporary
weep of one night on Saturday, and “Little Lord
Fauntleroy ” followed for Monday and Tuesday.
Jessie Clitherow, a very promising child actress,
does a good deal to lighten up the gloom of the
Opera House, and otherwise the Clitherow show
is mostly old weepful drama played by a co. in
which nobody is first-rate, and nobody is pic-
turesquely disgraceful. “ Saved From the Deep,”
which is at least more novel than “ East Lynne,”
follows on Saturday.
** * *
Some of the people who used to be at the
Tivoli have come back with great difficulty for a
stiort season only, and some other people have gone
away owing to the fact that an overwhelming
mass of engagements elsewhere made it quite im-
possible for them to remain any longer. Also, one
or two new people have come, and some other
people announce that they will shortly have to
depart. But the show in general goes on as pros-
perously as ever, and once every night the shadow
of G. Reid drifts by in the royal carriage, with a
fixed and glassy eye looking out at the prosperity
of Freetrade England.
** * *
Prof. Norton B. Smith, the horse-taming
expert, opened his show under hard conditions.
Prof. Smith undertakes to tame and mollify the
wildest steed which the public can supply, but no
one sent any wild steed. A number of animals
which seemed to be filled with a mild Christian
spirit were brought in, and wanted to lick the
Professor’s hand in amity. It was necessary to
excite them by ringing bells and banging drums
before there was any material to operate on, and
even then they weren’t vicious—only scared and
covered with perspiration. Probably N. B. Smith
can tame the hardest kicker and biter going, but
if only the patient cab-wreck is forthcoming, there
is no room to display his abilities.
A Royal Divorce,” shown to Australia
, r the first time at Melb. Princess’s, on Saturday
night, is a success of uncertain magnitude. As a
dramatic story it is a slow-goer, leaning heavily
upon the crutch of historical interest, but as a
spectacle, the Firm's latest venture should fetch
even those people who have forgotten all that they
ever knew concerning Napoleon Buonaparte, and
the little things he did previous to the Waterloo
fiasco. For which matter the dramatist took care
not to let anyone be well informed, by history, of
the evils that happen in “A Royal Divorce.”
Hie late W. G. Wills imagined many
vain things re Charles the First, Cromwell, Jane
Shore, and other bygone figures, but when he
came to Napoleon and Josephine he let himself
go a lot further than usual. In this unscrupulous
tale we find an ideal Josephine, a constant sweet-
heart and guiding star of Napoleon, sacrificing her
happiness upon the altar of her husband’s ambi-
tion, and anon clinging to his broken fortunes
with beautiful tenacity. The play suggests that
Josephine, when Napoleon wooed her, was a
maiden madly in love with a mere soldier,
whereas she was a calculating widow who married
him for his growing importance, and because his
duties would keep him safely away from her
flirtations at home. Moreover, thereat Josephine
didn't get a chance to cling to her divorced hus-
band after the sun went down upon his greatness.
She was dead. No matter. Had she remained
she might have clung.
* * • *
Tho dialogue in “A Royal Divorce,”
although not tho best of W. G. Wills’s stage
literature, is pretty strong and dignified for the
most part. Napoleon’s mood} - reference to Hing-
lish bull-dog courage is tho one purely ridiculous
bit of clap-trap in the play, and the good old bull-
dog metaphor succeeds in its mission, anyway.
Four fine tableaux are the mainstays of this hand-
some show. The Retreat of the Napoleonic host
from Moscow with a background of flame effects ;
two clever misrepresentations of the Waterloo
battle-field, the second one depicting Buonaparte
in the act of walking away, at the head of his
army, from the alleged charge of the Life Guards ;
and finally the forlorn captive posed upon the St.
Helena rock,' an impressive reminder of a well-
known picture, despite an unaccountable want of
distance in the seascape. For these valuable con-
tributions George Gordon got several bursts of
enthusiasm, being called and re-called. The Gor-
dons—G. and J. —are tho real heroes of “ A Royal
Divorce ” wherein the local drapers, Messrs.
George and George, also score heavily.
* • * ♦
The bulk of the acting falls to Miss Ada
Ferrar, as Josephine. It is a very large, “fat”
part, and the actress makes much of her oppor-
tunities without achieving a really great perform-
ance. Miss Ferrar never quite gets hold of her
audience as a more magnetic artist might. Julius
Knight’s Napoleon is a well-sustained pictorial
effort, and a suitable rendering of the figure when
it speaks. Miss Elliott Page puts in one or two
glorious appearances as a vixenish Marie Louise,
also Miss Raymond is bright and pleasing in the
character of Josephine’s relative and loyal sup-
porter, Stephanie de Beauharnais. Harry Hill,
woodeny at the outset, plays with spirit in the
fourth act, and Atholwood is a successful inciden-
tal “drunk.” The other people are lords, mar-
shals, princes, and kings, and they don’t display
their historical distinction to advantage. One
tall, juvenile marshal ought to be taken out and
shot before daybreak.
** # *
The veriscopic views of Fitzsimmons and
Corbett flickering around a large square “ring”
were duly submitted to a Melb. public, at the
Theatre Royal, on Saturday, and, as far as the
present writer could bring himself to think
seriously about this weird entertainment, he
thought it fell short of reason aide expectation.
The fight, as it appears indistinctly on the big
sheet, is a tame and rather tattered transaction,
intersected by frequent intervals of five minutes.
Still, it is a genuine attempt to show how Fitz-
simmons punched Corbett, and if it doesn’t suc-
ceed in showing the fastest pace at which the
punchers travelled, tho blame is due to the
machine, and not to the enterprising speculators
who run it.

Frank Thornton, at Melb. Bijou, has dis-
posed of “Miss Brown,” and is now winding up
his three months’ business with “Charley’s
Aunt ” (Thursday), and three last nights of “Pri-
vate Secretary.” It was necessary to revive the
Aunt and the Curate out of respect for the multi-
tudes that couldn’t get seated a few weeks back.

This week’s bill at the Alexandra is “The
Old Forge Mystery,” a not too lurid drama, in
which nobody seems happy until the missing
papers have been discovered. On Saturday the
same competent and persevering co. will put all
its energy into “ A Cruel Wrong.”

The young Majeronis speak English, or Italian, without
a suggestion of foreign accent in either case. They talk
Italian at home, but, outside of the family circle, they are
so habitually Australian as to term Signora Majeroni “the
mater” instead of mamma. For fear of seeming effemin-
ately snobbish (to people who don’t know that mamma is
a word imported from Italy by the English “exquisites”
of last century), they even refrain from calling their
mother by her proper title when they meet her in public.
Journalist: “I have been anatomizing a first-class
church choir, from the inside. It is exactly like a thea-
trical co., except, I will allow, in a more stringent regard
to legal matrimony. The choir has the same oamaraderie
as the players, and regards the w hole church as existing
for its special display. It is a matter of Religion for
every choir to devote prayer-time to looking up the
music. I have not yet seen them at a picnic, but they
keep up a sort of one during Divine Service, and the
whole is a mere performance.”

Barton M'Guckin's throat went wrong entirely after
his first appearance in Melbourne. It was then unfit to
bear the strain he put upon it. Result, a general impres-
sion that the tenor's voice has gone for ever, whereas it
is merely worn a little at the edges through hard work in
opera, and should soon be much improved by the change
of climate and comparatively easy time he will have whilst
touring round with a concert co.

Old friend Snazelle has contributed some rather
astounding “Tales of a Traveller” to Lloyd’s Nrws. For
instance, he commences Chapter V. with the allegation
that when Melbourne “ hurricanes” blow from a certain
quarter delicious mussels are washed upon the shore in
beds two feet deep and thirty feet wide, extending for
hundreds of miles around the coast. The shellfish, being
so plentiful, are naturally despised by the local populace,
although Snazzy found them even richer and more sustain-
ing then the whelks sold in London.
Miss Elsie Stanley Hall, the young Australian pianiste,
sailed from England on 27t,h Sept, for her native land.
Miss Hall brings back the distinction of being the winner
of the Berlin Mendelssohn Scholarship against all the
German competitors, and to her “belongs the honor,”
remarks London Musical Standard, “ of being the first
English girl to gain this important scholarship, and when
her youth is considered it is simply incredible.” She
comes fresh from a brilliant success in London.
Barton M'Guckin tells a yarn against himself of how he
overheard two Irishmen in Dublin discussing one of his
first appearances in opera. They were satisfied with his
voice, which they had heard before, also they passed a
vote of confidence in his acting—but, said Pat No. 1, “ I
can’t get over Barton’s nose.” “ How can ye get over it,
when there’s no bridge,” said the other l’athrick. The
tenor can afford to tell the tale seeing that his nose is not
an unimportant feature in private life. It makes a dis-
tinct beginning and a bold end, although the middle por-
tion leaves room for the introduction of stage-effect.
M'Guckin has a budget of Dublin recollections. One
night when he arrived upon the scene as Tannhauser, in
a boat drawn by a swan, a broguev critic yelled from the
gallery “ Begorra ! here comes Barton, with his duck,
down the Liffey.” No Irishman can hope to be taken
seriously in his own country. They give him love with-
out reverence.
Melb. : “ J.M.F. asks for the name of a tragedian who
appeared at the match-box Princess’s somewhere about
1860 (?). It was Maddocks, who appeared in ’57, as I
reckon. I was then a stage-struck little boy, with a loose
leg, but fortunately destitute of money. My evenings
were spent under the Princess’s piazza, because the music
could be heard from the inside, likewise the actors' voices
when they did a bit of rant. The house was not then
named the Princess's, but the Royal Amphitheatre, to
which the title had been changed from Astle.v’s Amphi-
theatre on the circus-ring being taken out and a pit and
stage made. I think it opened as a theatre with the
‘School for Scandal,’ in which Lambert made his first
Melb. appearance. I remember the first night of Mad-
docks, who was saifl to be a tall man. He opened in
‘ Hamlet,’ with 11. N. Warner as the Ghost. Maddocks
died in Melb. not very long after.”
“This drama contains one expression stamped with
the unmistakeable hall-mark of original genius, ‘Ha I
You here ! ’ ”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The New South Wales revenue for September
A'as £681,067, being £8268 more than that for September,
1896. The revenue for the first three months of the finan-
cial year ( £2,115,173) is £166,258 less than that for the cor-
responding quarter of 1896, while the expenditure
( 1 2,240,560) is £9299 more than for the same three months
last year. The expenditure is already £125,387 ahead of
revenue, which is a good and early beginning for G. H.
Reid’s fourth deficit. That champion explainer of things,
being interviewed by the S.M. Herald re the painful
slump, points out that the biggest part of the decline
is in income-tax, which hasn’t come in yet, and it
“simply means that, taking the year as a whole, the
deficiency this quarter as regards income-tax will be com-
pensated by an equally large surplus in another quarter.”
If he really said this it is a fine evidence of Premier Reid’s
chronic inability to tell the whole, straight, honest truth
about anything. The income-tax is deficient because in
last financial year two whole years’ tax was collected—one
lot at the beginning of the financial year and the other at
the end—so that the income-tax receipts for the 12
months were £305,316. This year it will be only pos-
sible to get one year’s' tax, about £160,000,
and there will be no “equally large surplus”
in any other quarter to make up the present de-
ficit. Unless the Reid Government doubles the in-
come-tax this one item of revenue must show a decline
of at least £140,000 as compared with last year. Last
year’s revenue was £170,000 short of expenditure, and
this year, with diminished revenue and increased expendi-
ture, begins badly enough.
During the same quarter, the N.S.W. expendi-
ture of loan-moneys has been £367,522, which is £86,428
more than that for the same quarter of last year. The
great art and mystery of Premier Reid’s finance lies in
charging all possible expenditure to loans, and evidently
the work has been going on this quarter even a little more
gaily than usual.
* * * •
The sale of N.S.W. 3 per cent, stock has been
resumed, and so the process of piling up the Cornstalk
debt is again in full blast. If the province must borrow
it is undoubtedly a hundred times better to borrow
locally than abroad ; nevertheless, the new system has
one serious disadvantage. When a big loan was floated
all in a heap the public could hardly help opening one
eye_ in its sleep and noticing that the province was still
taking the easy way to get over its liabilities by leaving
them to posterity. But now the loan falls as silently
as the gentle dew from heaven, and not one person in a
dozen knows that N.S.W. i 3 booming along the road to
insolvency in a worse fashion than it ever did before-
worse because the old loans were at least mostly spent on
reproductive works, while of the £3,900,u00' borrowed
money which the Reid Government has spent in two and
a-half years more than half has gone upon works which
return absolutely nothing. Still, as N.S.W. never did
anything when it partially woke up and opened one tired
eye to the situation, it probably doesn’t matter much.
* * » *
Queensland revenue for July, Aug., and Sept,
was £1,069,325, as compared with £1,143,017 for the same
months last year— a decline of £73,692. In 1896 the
Queensland revenue reached top figure, and for the year
ending June, ’97, it fell off by about £30,000. Apparently,
unless it braces up considerably, there will be a still
worse decline by June, ’9B, and the Treasury will be on
the rocks again —which is the place where it is usually to
be found.
* * * •
For the quarter ended September, 1897, South
Australia collected £558,305 revenue, which is a fall of
£20,594 as compared with the corresponding quarter of
last year.
* * * *
The Westralian revenue for September (£240,129)
is £10,948 less than that for September, 1896, and the
revenue for the quarter (£655,383) is £25,821 less than
that for the same quarter last year. This is the first
downward movement since the boom, of blessed memory,
started in 1890, and, coupled with the falling off in immi-
gration, the empty Treasury, the difficulty in raising new
loans, even at the heavy rate of 4 per cent., it looks like
as if the glory had departed. Last year, the surplus was
only £3298. If the revenue keeps on at the present rate,
the year’s income will be £lOO,OOO less than that for
1896-97, and that probably means a heavy deficit.
* * * *
The Victorian revenue for the three months up
to 30th September was £1,583,949—£39,857 more than
the amount realised up to 30th Sept., ’96. Tasmania,
which closes its accounts on 31st Dec., has now got through
nine months of its financial year, and, so far, the revenue
is £24,336 better than for the first nine months of ’96.
So far, Vic. and Tas. alone of the six Australian provinces
show any improvement, while N.S.W. has the biggest
slump, and Queensland comes a good second.
* * * *
Agent-General Playford, of S.A., strongly con-
demns the insane agreement entered into by the King-
ston Government, whereby a certain newspaper man gets
a contract for a number of years to hawk the bonds of the
S.A. State Bank in England. There were so many good
reasons against the scheme that its acceptance by Acting-
Premier Holder looks next to incredible. The bonds were
fetching a good price in Adelaide. There is no guarantee
that they will fetch a better price in London ; the Govett
syndicate is to get them at a figure which is governed by
the selling value of S.A. Government stock, so the
Government may find itself compelled to let the
syndicate have them for two or three years at a
lower price than they would realise in Adelaide.
The syndicate will doubtless stick to the business while it
is a loss to the Government, but apparently there is no
cash guarantee that it won’t drop it just as soon as it
becomes a profit to the Government. Sj’ndicates are
smellful things at all times. This one must give the
Government less than could be obtained in the open
market in order to make a profit for itself —it isn’t doing
the business out of pure philanthropy. The new arrange-
ment deprives the local inhabitant of a good investment,
and makes S.A. more dependent on the eternal John
Bull, and takes more money out of the country annually
in interest, and further postpones the time of financial
independence. Altogether it is difficult to find one visible
good point about the scheme.
» * * *
The Victorian income-tax case of Webb v. Eng-
land is going to the Privy Council. This fine example of
British cheek and assurance has now raged in the Vic.
courts for over a year. Defendant represents a large
amount of British capital invested in Victoria. The in-
surance co. whose capital it is gets a big income out of
the province, but flatly refuses to pay tax upon it, appar-
ently on the moral ground that a British co. is something
too large and imposing to be taxed by the beastly
Government of a common, beastly colony. The legal
point on which the trouble is nominally based looks of
the slenderest. Defendant claims that only persons who
“carry on business” in Victoria are taxable, and he
pleads that investing money through an agent, and
collecting interest, and foreclosing on defaulters and
realising the security, are not “carrying on business,”
and that unless the co. does insurance in Australia it
doesn’t come into the category. To the lay mind it
would seem that investing the funds and getting interest
on them is carrying on a very important branch of the
business, and two Vic. courts have decided against the
British person, but this latter is still full of fight and in-
tends having another run for his money. If he wins, the
Victorian Government will have to hustle round as it
never hustled b. fore, and get the Income Tax Act amended.
The idea of levying a special tax on the absentee has
often been considered, but the idea of taxing the local
investor and letting the absentee go free altogether is a
wild inversion of things.

N.S. Wales has three railways which don’t pay
working expenses, let alone interest on cost - Armidale
to Jennings, Murrumburrah to Blayney, and Sydenham
to Belmore. The last one, in proportion to the capital
involved, is the most hopeless job of the lot.
* * * *
In view of the largely increased Victorian fire
insurance rates, the local Railway Department has decided
to establish its own insurance fund and be its own in-
surer for the future. Sundry steamship co’s and other
large insurers contemplate doing likewise. This was the
inevitable result of the attempt to put up rates. The
trouble was, not that premiums were too low—they were
high enough to pay magnificent dividends under fair con-
ditions—but that office expenses are ruinous and utterly
awful. And the reason why the expenses have reached
such a pitch is that twice as many offices —mostly English
—as the business can support are trying to live on it.
As a fine example of how the industry is overdone in
Australian cities, Coghlan enumerates 40 leading offices
doing fire business in Sydney alone. Sixteen of them have
less than half-a-million of insurances, and nine have less
than £'300,000. Assuming an average rate of 10s. per
cent., this latter figure would mean less than £lsooa year
income out of which to pay a full blown manager, office
staff, agents, rent, taxes, gas, stationery, stamps and
sundries—in addition to fire losses. No business can
stand so much management as that under fair conditions.
* * * *
So deep is the Hebrew’s horror of a bad bargain
that a certain M.L.C., who imported a lot of tobacco 11
years ago, prefers to continue paying storage on same (in
the Queen’s warehouse) to this day. The tohacco is, of
course, almost worthless by this time, and storage has
cost 20 times its original value, arid had the M.L.C. dared
to outrage all the traditions of his race, he might have
sold it, when originally imported, at a loss of not more
than 10 per cent.

According to pearler Clarke there are only six
white divers left now in the Straits. About 80 boats are
either hired or owned by Japanese, and average earnings
per boat, amount to about £2OO. Four or five years at this
sort of thing and the Jap. is a made man.

The N.S. W. Govt. Board of Exports’consign-
ment of oranges to London turned out a failure ; the fruit
arrived in poor condition, having heated. The South
Australian depot oranges, on the other hand, arrived in
good condition and sold well.
* * * •
The pungency of the gas thrown off by the
burning Oregon timber in the B.H. mine was marvellous.
Twelve days after the outbreak, and four days after the
issue of the official report that if the fire was not abso-
lutely extinct it was practically so, the odor was horribly
offensive at a distance of a mile from the mine. From a
spectacular point of view, though, amine fire is a thorough
failure. For an hour or less there was a faint issue of
smoke from one of the shafts ; but after that nothing
but this formless and truly shocking stink. The com-
plaint is made that, though the watchman service
on the Broken Hill Proprietary is all that could be
desired on top, it is inadequate underground. Every
hour of the week, except from midnight on Saturday
until midnight on Sunday, the underground workings are
occupied ; for that 24 hours they are deserted except for
half-a-dozen shift-bosses. Men, fearing to be left behind,
rush out of the faces when the signals sound, and the
danger of fire from candles left burning, &c., is thus
largely added to. Certainly it is curious, if nothing
more, that both fires have broken out early on Sunday
morning ; and, more curious still, the last mail brought
word of a very destructive fire in a lead-mine in the Isle
of Man, which also broke out on a Sunday morning. At
one mine the men have been instructed to go to work on
Sundays as on other days ; but that course is not likely to
be adopted on the bigger mines. It ought to be kicked
against, anyway—and for that matter so ought night-
shift (midnight to 8 a.m.). It is a brutal institution in a
hot climate, where men can’t possibly get decent rest in
8 x 10 iron shanties during the day-time.
* • * *
The fire in the B.H. Prop, has had one good
effect : Other mines are making more provision for filling
up the worked-out stopes, between the timbers, with sur-
face refuse—concentrator tailings, &o. Block 14 has been
best-off in this respect, then Block 10 and the British ; the
Junction has been improving lately, and the South, Cen-
tral, and North are now doing more.
* * * *
The British takes advantage of the rise in lead
to go tearing out the carbonate ores at a greater rate ; and
this has something to do, too, with the call of the miners
to do Sunday work in the mine. The British is now send-
ing fair parcels of concentrates to the Illawarra works.

North Nymagee manager’s first report : —“ Ar-
rived at mine on Sunday last. The Nymagee co. has
worked the lode up to our boundary, so it is evident it
must run in our ground.” Look at that now ! Why did

* * • *
“Every available piece of ground north and
south of the Lake George Mines, N.S.W., is being pegged.
Several leading mining men and experts are here inspect-
ing properties for flotation.” The same old business.
Value established after many years’ work in one mine,
and the surrounding country is pegged for miles. Every
block, of course, will be “on the lode.” The writer
seriously warns everybody to regard each position-block
as Billings did the snake’s hole. The game is played as a
quick means of making money. Mineral leases can be
taken up for ss. an acre, and, if the public only knew how
hard it is to sell a good mine, they would not waste any
money on duffers.
* * * *
As pointed out by Sydney D.T., one thing
which will help to keep the copper-boom cool in N.S.W.
is the fact that most of the best-known properties are
freehold, and in the hands of people who won’t allow
them to be prospected or worked unless they are made
millionaires right away. Copper, when the Mining on
Private Property Act was framed, was not made one of the
metals which might be mined for.
» * * *
Excuse given for the non-publication of returns
from the Lake George Mines, N.S.W.— that none have yet
been made by the new furnaces, which leaked so badly
that the jackets had to be thrown out and new ones
ordered. They are about to be put in blast, and returns
are to be published regularly. To the richer develop-
ments in copper in the deeper levels of this property the
wonderful rise in the value of shares during the last few
months is attributable.

Conqueror mine, Cobar, N.S.W., 204 tons

* * * *
Relations between the Lucknow strikers and
Vic. Miners’ Association are strained. At latest, the sec.
of the Lucknow men had wired to the sec. of the Bendigo
association :—“ Keep scabs back ; only Victorian black-
legs here.” The cause of the Miners’Association's com-
parative indifference is that the Lucknow men hadn’t a
union of their own till they got in the soup ; and, so far
as Bendigo is concerned, the Mines Act —making a living
wage a first charge on the gold won—has rendered the
local tributors’ occupation more precarious than ever.
* * * *
Bendigo and Ballarat are being canvassed for
miners for Lucknow —7s. a day for miners and 6s. 6d. for
laborers, with 12 months guarantee. Local recognised
rates are 7s. 6d. for ordinary places, and Bs. 4d. for wet
and hot workings. But work is irregular, and men are
forced to tributing, at which less than 30s. is averaged ;
it is the 12 months’ guarantee that attracts. And the
Bendigo A.M.A. officers confess that men may go to
Lucknow and remain members of the A.M.A. provided
they continue to pay their dues. This, which reaches
Tub Bulletin on apparently good authority, is indeed a
startler.
• * * *
New Bushman’s, N.S.W.: 400 tons for 1750 z
and yet they refused a big cash offer, and have never had
a div. since. They don’t deserve one.
• » * *
The prospectus of the Lake George South
Mines, capital £lOO,OOO, appears in this issue. This show
is situate at Captain’s Flat, N.S.W., to the south of the
Lake George Mine's, and it is reckoned that the latter’s
lode goes right through the property. If it does, and if
the lode preserves its quality, there should be money in
the new spec., barring bad management,, special ill-luck,
swindle, the Act of God, and the other risks that all
mining flesh is heir to. A notable fact is that Reginald
Murray, late Victorian Government Geologist, is on the
directorate.
« * * •
Hillgrove: “The Garibaldi mine has been re-
ported for non-fulfilment of labor conditions, but the co.
holds the tributors responsible.” Let Bull once lose a
N.S.W. mine by forfeiture, and it, is all up with the other
floats till the law is changed. Insecurity of tenure is the
curse of mining throughout Australasia. It is not on
account of the number of mines lost by jumping, but all
on account of the glaring anomaly that under the Aus-
tralian law the informer, if successful, is the man who
walks away with the property. All very well in the old
digging days, when everyone worked his own claim and
personal possession was everything, but all that has
changed, and it is time the law changed too. It is almost
obsolete anyway, as there is not a Mines Minister in Aus-
tralasia who dare forfeit a Bull-owned mine.

Goodrich, one of the General Gordon pro-
moters, has been greatly shocked in London by the G.G.
crushing. This is sorrowful. Boomsters seldom recover
from shock.
* * * *
J. C. Auldjo has fallen foul of the Coolgardie
press for publishing the horrible truth about several W.A.
alleged gold districts, enumerating a number of mines
sold to Bull at enormous sums of cash, now closed down
after tens of thousands have been spent in vainly attempt-
ing to win gold in a howling wilderness. Two or three of
these places have reverted to the howling-wilderness con-
dition, from which they should never have been disturbed.
Auldjo admits it is not pleasant to wash dirty linen
in public, or to parade ugly ducklings, but, as the
instances before given had been regarded as strikingly
exceptional, he proceeds to enumerate a number of others
—giving the names of mines closed down, and of others
that, in the interests of decency, ought to close.
* * • *
O’Connor, Engineer-in-Chief for W.A., has
returned with a good report from three English engineers
re his gigantic water-scheme for Coolgardie and other
fields. They say it is practicable, can be carried out for
the estimate (2k millions), that a single line of pipes is not
too risky if made of steel, that corrosion can be prevented
by dipping in Trinidad asphaltum, that the pipes may be
covered or left exposed, and generally that there is
nothing to prevent the scheme being worked successfully.
Thestrataare against strikingan artesian supply—one bore
(down 3000 ft.) has hit none, and, though an enormous
amount of water is found at Hannan’s and other places
from 200 ft. to 300 ft., the indication at the lower level is
that, below it, there will be little water. That has been
proved correct in several cases. If this is the general
state of things, the water near the surface will soon
become exhausted, and the water-trouble will begin in
dead earnest.
* * * *
The writer is quite lost in the labyrinth of lodes
found in the Great Boulder mine, as reported to the
Eastern dailies. He has lost all count. There must be at
least 100 lodes, all very rich in gold and tellurides. The
Press is an ass in most things outside of society items and
politics, but in mining it reaches the zenith of asininity.
Bv-and-bv it dawns on the Kalgoorlie wire-hummer, and
then on the public, that, in nine cases out of ten, it is the
same lode which happens to have been intersected at
different places.

A gorgeous boom tale from Tamboorah (W.A.)
is about the finding of a new reef, sft. wide, which goes
70oz. to the dish. It is added that the around already
opened up will yield IOOOoz. That is, it will yield 15
dishes. The reef is so far opened up that it is known to
be sft. wide, and vet there are only 15 dishes of stuff in
sight. Either the dish is large enough to hold a full-
grown rhinoceros or this is almost the worst-constructed
lie on record.

The Pinafore deep level find proves to be a good
deal of a fizzle, and the expected boom at Lefroy is in-
definitely postponed. T9e shoot of gold did not hold
out, and the stone is not too plentiful, but there is en-
couragement for further prospecting. That is the best
that can be said of the tremendous discovery. However,
the mine was pioneered and is run by plucky men who can
be relied upon to get the best out of it.

A warning from the West Coast of Tasmania
“Sections are being pegged in all directions, and it
behoves buyers to use discretion.” The same old busi-
ness ; every boomer wants to put in pegs—it is cheaper
than buying the other fellow’s mine, and it is easier to lie
about your own mine than to work it. Working mines is
one thing, working the public another.

Gov. Brassey’s absence from Victoria (on
a yachting cruise !) at the time when a general
election is in progress and Parliament has been
dissolved, has again raised the query, “Why
have a Governor at all ? ” On this occasion Chief
Justice Madden has had to perform the formal
duties in connection with the dissolution, while
the old man on the Sunbeam is fooling around the
coast in his beloved tub. If a Governor isn’t
wanted when there is no Parliament in existence,
and the very fate of the Government —his “ ad-
visers ” —is at stake, when is he wanted ? And
what is he paid for, anyhow ?
* * * *
The fancy-dress ball to be given by the
Brassey’s in Cup-week is “ all the talk ” in Melb.
social circles. The Governor, it is stated, will
appear as the Old Man of the Sea.
* * * *
In order to keep His Ex. in good health
while Premier Forrest was in the East, the Wes-
tralian Govt, sent Gov. Smith on a tour through
the agricultural districts. All the Westralian
survivors of prehistoric man turned out to wel-
come him, and built jarrah arches and hung
festoons of turnips across the local sidings to im-
press him with the resources of the Sand Patch.
H.E. was bored to death, but he is much chastened
in spirit since he first struck these shores. He
returned to Perth with a large earthy smell, a
rich complexion of mahogany-red, and some rare
specimens of vegetable products for the vice-regal
table. Forty-seven country Mayors and Js.P. are
now expecting K.C.M.G. by the next post.
* •* * »
By synodical resolution the N.S.W. Angli-
can parsonry have determined to entitle them-
selves “rectors.” This action on their part
appears to savor somewhat of arrogance, yet it is
one that will carry another interpretation. For
does not “rector” mean “ruler” and does not
“ ruler ” mean a stick—a term that quite realisti-
cally defines the average Church of England
minister ?
* * * *
The woes of the way-back parson. An
out-West Anglican, a popular man, found it
necessary to move on; and the best the parishioners
could do for him was to present him with an
address and issue a notice (this was in early Sep-
tember) that “ a grand bazaar towards payment
of the outstanding stipend of will be held at
show-time, May, 1898.” A newly-imported man
takes this one’s place. Why do we import our
parsons ? Often because the local man knows too
much to take the vacant billet.
F. : “A prospector returned
from his Papuan adventures, with
shaven head and fever-shaken limbs,
tells me tales that I believe as I listen,
but which would look Munchausens
in print. He travelled 1000 miles up
and down mountain-sides, and got 80
miles inland—as the crow flies. He
stood beside a river which, the natives
assured him, contained much gold;
said river ran at the rate of many
miles an hour, and rolling in the stream
were boulders as big as a bedroom.
The inland natives regard salt very
covetously, will give much trade for
it, and then eqt it ravenously, as if it
were sugar. Tobacco-smoking is one
of their national habits, i.e., not copied
from whites. The tobacco plant that
is native to the country is small and
weedy in appearance, but excellent in
flavor when well cured. The native
tobacco-pipe is more like a drain-pipe
than like a “ cherry wood.” It is a
bamboo 3in. in diameter and a yard
or more long, and, of course, quite
hollow. One end is closed, the other
open. A small hole is bored towards
the closed end ; in it is placed a spool
of tobacco leaves. The native applies
his mouth, or rather his face, to the
large end, and “ draws ” till the drain-
pipe is filled with smoke. The tobac-
co-spool is then withdrawn from the
small hole, and the company is in-
vited to share the contents of the
drain-pipe, each one ‘ drawing ’ out a
mouthful from the small hole. My
traveller nearly turned a camp of na-
tives from friends into enemies by
striking a vesta match. They thought
it was a bad case of lightning. The
jungles are so thick that the pros-
pectors walked in them for days with-
out seeing the sun. The tropical rain
wetted everything, and they had to
live on mouldy rice and flour. The
natives have a system of long-distance
messages, which are shouted from mountain-tops, a
man with a suitable voice being set apart for in-
telligencer, and he gives all the gossip of the day.
His shouted message is heard and understood
miles away.”
* * * *
The B.H. Prop. Co., after thinking over
the matter for a fortnight, decided to award to the
men who behaved so heroically on the day of the
outbreak of the fire £1 each. There were 130 of
them, exclusive of officers. Some of the men,
besides being stupefied and brought senseless to
the temporary hospital at the surface, damaged
their clothes —it was Sunday—hopelessly. Their
one consolation now is that they were not charged
for the oxygen that was pumped into them, or the
brandy that was poured down their throats.
Somebody writes to the local paper suggesting
that, as so many men have been thrown out of
work, but as fortunately, through the benefactions
of the company, most of them are now capitalists,
the present would be an opportune time to estab-
lish the long - talked -of irrigation colony at
Menindie—Broken Hill’s nearest point to the
river Darling.

lie the decay of Silverton, N.S.W. Of
the eight “ principal ” N.S.W. towns mentioned
in Hughes’ “ Elementary Class-book of Modern
Geography ” (’96 edition), used in many local (es-
pecially R.C.) schools, Silverton is one, despite its
present absurdly reduced circumstances. Also
Parramatta is mentioned, but Gonlburn is over-
looked. Most British geographers seem to imagine
that Parramatta is the capital city of Australia.
Eidsvold (Q.) Reporter has
ceased publication, and the Burnett
electorate is now in the unique posi-
tion of having no newspaper printed
within its borders. The M.L.A. for
that district may feel inclined to con-
gratulate himself. Yet this extensive
electorate contains some of the finest
wheat-growing lands in Q., and is
marred simply for the want of railway
facilities. The once-prosperous gold-
fields of Eidsvold and Paradise are now
practically dead, and agriculture is im-
possible until some means be found to
convey produce to market. Gayndah
municipality (within the electorate) is
so decayed that a Chinaman even can-
not now be found willing to be elected
as an alderman, and the rates collec-
ted are insufficient to pay the town-
clerk’s salary. They have had, though,
a Chinese Mayor in that same Gayndah,
in the good old past.
There are still in prison seven men who
were sentenced for offences committed in the
v\ estern District of N.S.W. during the shearers’
strike of 1894 ; and two or three rather lackadaisi-
cal attempts have been made to secure a mitma-
,e sent; ences. Another is now proceeding ;
the Bourke Municipal Council has decided to sup-
port it. The ground relied on is that the ends of
justice have been served already, and that “a
more kindly feeling is likely to be aroused by tiieir
liberation. Certainly the last three seasons have
been depressmgly quiet.

Periodically the Yic. State scholars pester
friends and strangers for contributions to the
Children’s Hospital. Collecting-cards are issued
by the teacher, who is credited with the sums
collected. If Minister Peacock were not so in-
sufferably “ loyal ” he might be persuaded to
abolish this system, which gives little real benefit,
and, while putting temptation in the way of the
youngsters, trains them to become hardened
cadgers.

“ Gemini ” to The Bulletin :
A death occurred under chloroform, at Melb. Hospital
the other day. The doctor who made the post-mortem
stated that in his opinion the cause of death was suffoca-
tion, arising, he believed, either from a swelling near the
epiglottis, or from deceased’s tongue falling back whilst
he was under the influence of the anaesthetic. verdie„.
Deceased died from suffocation whilst under the influence
of chloroform and ether, properly administered. Now, in
the face of this evidence, was the anaesthetic properly
administered ? It should have been one of the first cares
of the medico who gave chloroform to see that the
patient’s breathing was impeded in no avoidable way,
yet the evidence goes to prove that the patients tongue
was, probably',- allowed to fall back, unknown to the
doctor, and suffocate him. And this despite the fact that
such accidents are common under a general anaesthetic
IF ENGLAND FOOTS THE BILL.
“It is seriously proposed to dispatch 100
mounted men to India to help in the frontier
trouble. The men propose to accept the same
rate of pay as their comrades in the British army.
. . . It is expected that the Imperial Govern-
ment will defray the cost of the passages to and
fro. ”
The Cape is proff’ring battle ships,
Our Lady of the Snows
Is giving England tariff-tips,
What shall we do ? —here goes,
Hang all expense—our offering
Is greater, grander still—
A hundred horsemen swift we bring
To join in Afghan slaughtering
(If England foots the bill).
Pause not to calculate the cost,
The Mother Country calls—
So let the angry seas be orost
Ere England’s empire falls.
Twice fifty men we freely lend
To help to burn and kill,
Our generous instincts have no end—
We’ll all our unemployed send—
(If England foots the bill).
Poor Canada dissolves in tears,
The Cape with envy pales
As loudly ring the British cheers
For loyal New South Wales.
Five score of soldiers, lank and tall,
We send to end the mill.
Why, hang it! they can have us all
(To draw our pay) and boast and brawl
(If England foots the bill).

The Cairns (N.Q.) Yoshiwarra aroma is
getting thicker. The local council has actually
decided to draw the attention of the Col. Sec. to
the little brown woman, and to ask him to ask the
police to shunt the whole 37 of her—out of Cairns ?
No; out of “the main thoroughfares.” Public
talk and prints parade certain of the Cairns Cor-
poration as “ patrons ”of the 37. Here is part of
a Mayoral dissertation :
One might suppose that this petition, with its refer
encesto “ a crying evil ” and “the morality of the dis-
trict,” emanated from a Presbyterian Conference. But I
have seen photographs in which some of those whose
names are attached to that petition are represented
alongside naked prostitutes. ... I have been openly
accused of deriving a large income from these houses of
ill-fame. . .‘ . The properties I own in that quarter
are let on extended leases, and if the lessees let the pre-
mises to undesirable tenants, I don't see how I can inter-
fere.' It would make no difference to me were every
Japanese woman in the town driven away to-morrow.
Good Draper, good old boss ! Good Mayor l
Why should you for such scandals care ?
W hat matter tongue ? What matter paper ?
Or, how you drape her ? Or undrape her?

The retiring president of the Christian
Endeavorers remarks, by way of encouraging
statistics, that there are 501 Christian Endeavor
Societies in Victoria, and that, as the result of a
rallying appeal to them on behalf of missions to
the heathen, they had—amongst the lot of them—
subscribed £llOO. That is to say, a little over £2
per society, or supposing, at a small estimate, that
each society numbers 40 members, 6d. per head of
Christian Endeavorer.. It seems very little to
lavish over the salvation of even the soul of a
heathen, but probably it is quite enough. The
name of the retiring president is Rev. D. Daley.
What is he doing that gallery ? But, then, they
run in all shapes.

• * * *
A popular church (or,
rather, “chapel”) institution
in Cornish mining settle-
ments in Australia—Long
Gully (Bendigo), Moonta
(S.A.), and South Broken
Hill, for instance is the
“ pasty supper,” which takes
the place of the elsewhere
conventional “ bun-wrastle.”
The Cornish pasty is fear-
fully and wonderfully made
of a hard crust encasing
chopped meat and “taters,”
with, in good times, a slice
or two of onions and fat
bacon. There was grumb-
ling the other night at a
Long Gully “ supper ” over
the awful predominance of
the taters, whereupon a wide-
awake politician jumped at
the opening with a telling
reference to dear meat, and
then, atone swoop, converted
the whole audience to an
utter detestation of the stock-
tax.

In Westralia last year
there were 15 more male
deaths than births. Which
is partly an eloquent testi-
mony to the germy condi-
tion of the Golden West,
and partly a proof that the
W.A. male has to scratch so
vigorously for a living now
that he hasn’t time to be
born.

Somebody has been “having” Brisbane
(Q.) Courier “on toast.” A Saturdays issue
reported how an unusually interesting ceremony
was to take place in an Anglican church, with the
very-much -boomed Canon Stone-Wig 1 # (J3isnop
virginal, of Papua) as head showman, and a lot of
communicants as the. “dress-circle. It was to
be the re-admission into the church by special
arrangement of prayers, liturgies and whatnots,
invented by Stone-Wigg for the occasion, of an old
lady who had gone over to Home, and was now
repentant. The Courier didn’t seem to think it
odd that a big church should make Such a mortal
fuss over one troublesome old woman who didn’t
know her own wayward mind. Even when the
paragraphist went on to tell how Bishop Stone-
Wigg had sent the lady to the Roman authorities
to let them know her intention to desert back
again to the faith of her girl's pinafores, the
Courier swallowed it as quite a usual thing. Nor
did it strike the newspaper that people join the
Church of England, depart from it, and rejoin it
every week without any special prayers and
flummery having to be invented, and without
R.C. priests or anyone else being officially con-
sulted. Then the paragraph told (in other lan-
guage, of course) how the lady had gone to Father
Fouhy and told him he was a whited sepulchre
and connected with the Scarlet Woman and that

On a bush racecourse. The impromptu lock-up. Drunks handcuffed over chain.
she was once more free from idolatry, wax-candles
and all that sort of thing, and the Courier set it
all down as a passing event worthy of chronicle.
Not even when the par. wound up with the state-

because the re-enfolded lady was in a hurry to go
and see a sick friend at Gympie (!), did the editor
realise that someone was “ pulling his leg,” but
solemnly put the whole narrative into his columns
—not as a contribution, but as a record of events
noticed by the staff.
* * * *
British Medical Journal, describing the
temperance movement as “a distinguishing feature
of the Victorian era,” remarks that it ought not to
be forgotten that, while drunkenness has decreased
among males, it has largely increased among
women.
C.M.Gr. : “ Was shown to-day by a Melb.
fruiterer a fine navel orange with, the perfect im-
print of a shamrock in a green tint in the skin.
Look d like a natural freak, but may have been
the work of an artful orchardist. ‘ Why don t you
show it in your window?’ I asked. What ,
gasped the shopman, ‘and invalidate my in-
surance ? ”
In a group picture of prominent Christian
Endeavourers in a Melbourne weekly, the face of
ex-champion oarsman Trickett is the only one that
shows any trace of spirituality. The others
mostly suggest only gross feeding, as if the first
Christian endeavour was to get away with masses
of meat.
* ♦ * *
Marriages are made in Heaven and paid
for on earth. A member of the C. of B. Assembly
in Melb. last week tried to secure the reduction
of church marriage fees from three guineas to half-
a-sovereign. The mover declared that existing
fees were so high that church members could not
afford to pay, and were forced to go elsewhere to
be married. The motion was lost, it being held
that the Church could not afford to extend its
blessings at less than three guineas a go. So
marriages made in Heaven will continue to pay
big profits to the importers.
T.D. : “A S.M. Herald article re the
gathering-in of stray dogs for destruction, does
not tell the whole truth. Civilians are authorised
by the police to hunt by night for curs at (I think)
half-a-crown apiece. They adopt a familiar
method—responsible for the fact that nearly all
the animals brought in are of the male kind. A
friend of mine recently pummelled a collector
whom he found trying to induce a valuable canine
to quit his kennel. These men lead their cap-
tures to the slaughter in batches of half-a-dozen—
and to-day I find five lost-dogs advertised for in
Sydney D.T.”

Dan’l Cooper’s occupancy of N.S.W.
Agency-General suffused with the ruddy glow of
confidence the countenances of the Cornstalk
braves who pointed guns India-wards and de-
manded to be let off the chain. “He’ll pay half
our expenses out of his own pocket,’’ they
whispered. In the old Crimean days Cooper gave
.£lOOO, with the promise of £5OO per annum while
the war lasted, and no doubt he would bleed in
this case also all the more readily because of the
official position he temporarily fills.
* * * •
The Chinaman does not object to hanging
as an evil per se. It’s only the public exhibition
he demurs to. In proof whereof may be cited the
case of See Chong, lately condemned to death at
Bundaberg, who tore his shirt in strips and
hanged himself in his prison-cell. Several such
cases have occurred in the North.

• * * *
The wife of a high politician created
quite a scene on a recent train journey. The lady
became hysterical, and, rushing out of the carriage
at a stopping - station, made loud accusations
against people in general and her husband in par-
ticular. It is to be feared that the wives of some
eminent persons in this country give them a lot
more trouble than the affairs of State.
* • * *
How the trouble spreads. A mob of cattle
reaches a station on the Belyando, not a hundred
miles from Clermont. The mob is, of course, per-
fectly clean, and is so reported to the authorities.
As night falls, however, the cook has a row with
the drover, and, in the morning, a raging son of
spuds and suet rushes in to the nearest police-
station with a fist-full of cattle-ticks, and avers
that even the horses of aforesaid drover are
swarming with the pest. And so they were. But
for that little row those ticks would soon have
been down on the Darling.
• * « *
From time to time writers have marvelled
at the fact that corruption permeates almost every
Melb. institution, and the theory has been ad-
vanced that the air of the Southern city is fatal to
honesty—that the microbe of integrity cannot live
in the vicinity of the Yarra. This theory was
peculiarly supported at Melb. City-court the
other day, where a man was charged with larceny.
Seventeen years ago, say the police, accused ar-
arived in Melb. from a foreign land, and, though
previously his character had been beyond reproach,
he had not been there a month before he was sen-
tenced to five years for assault and robbery. Com-
pleting his sentence, he left Melbourne for Queens-
land, and, for the past 12 years, has led an exem-
plary life in the North. In an evil moment, how-
ever, he was tempted to revisit the scene of his
undoing, and, after three days’ inhalation of
Melb. air, he once more fell into crime. When
his present sentence of a month has expired, he
ntends, he says, to take the first train away from
he Queen City of the South and see it never again.
A WAR WHOOP.
I’m a gallant Sydney Lanceah, hot and eagah for the
fray,
I want to help Old Hingland in the East;
And, when the Queen is mentioned, my vociferous
Hooray
Sounds worthy of a Fat Man at a feast.
Hot and eager for the fray,
I am fairly on the slay—
“Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay ”
My song;
Hark ! the band begins to play,
And my soul is on its way
To the army at Bombay—
Bing-bong!
Like a beast in search of prey—
Bing-bong !
A curse for “ dam civilians ” if we only get afloat,
Their cackle cannot alter our intent;
Though the War Bill be defeated by an Opposition vote—
Well, at least we shall have slain the Government.
If we only get afloat,
We will swaggah round the boat,
Bound for latitudes remote—
So long!
Ere I cut a Mollah’s throat,
Sprinkle blood upon my coat
From a sacrificial Goat—
Bing-bong!
From a Soudan sort of Goat —
Bing-bong !
Wheeling o’er the whirling earth,
Whirling in the wheeling maze,
Pedalled by thejeet of mirth,
Paced by laughing hours and days.
Where will all the wheeling end
’Mid the whirling fancies’ snare ?
In fields and pastures new for him ;
Perchance—in sweet divorce for her.
Sept., ’97.
H. C. SHAW.
THE LAY OF THE LANCERS.
We thirst for blood,
We’d like a flood
Of gore.
Rude War’s alarms
For us have charms
Galore.
But, sad to say,
The foe we’d slay
Is far :
He has his lair
Long leagues from where
We are.
Six months we crave
The lance and glaive
To grip :
Two months of war,
And three or four
Of trip.
The savage foe
We’d like to go
And meet.
Although we hear
That he’s in clear
Retreat.
Each lance’s tip
We’d like to dip
In red.
We mean it, though
It’s true our foe
Has fled.
If they the spot
Have left, we’re not
To blame.
And so, you bet,
We’re bound to get
Some fame.
SPRING-SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sing’ out and be happy !
The spring is at hand,
The grass green, and sappy
The trees o’ the land.
Sing, for the breeze is
Rustling and silky,
And toys with and teases
Long blossoms and milky.
The root in the juices
Unfrosted drinks deep,
The loving wave sluices
The weeds as they sleep.
Sing out, for the bees in
Their quest of wild honey
Are haunting the trees in
Green places and sunny.
Distant blue reaches
And green hills invite,
Green hills and long beaches
And roads red and white.
Locked waters are calling
With many gold voices,
Where tides gently falling
Make soft liquid noises.
Broad-spreading sun-glamour
Wraps blossom and stream,
Gold-tinting the armour
Of beetles that dream.
On wet rocks and ledges
Are massed starry mosses,
And painting proud ridges
Grow tiny pink roses.
Sing out, and let trouble
Another pursue,
It will burst like a bubble
And vanish for you.
Out, out on old Sorrow,
Who skulks in her sable !
Laugh gaily, and borrow
Gay laughs while you’re able.
If any care rankles —
Away I—and1 —and behold
Pink feet and white ankles
Or beaches of gold !
And surf that runs after,
To kiss clinging dresses,
And white teeth and laughter,
And naughty brown tresses!
RODERIC QUINN.
