A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 5, 1897.
My I>kar Myke—
The naval hop at Garden Island, on Friday
evening, transferred the matter-of-fact depht into
a gorgeous ballroom. Dancing took place up-
stairs in the enormous sail-loft, into which guests
breathlessly arrived up a long Jacob’s-ladder,
made shipshape with tarpaulin and bunting, and
the angels were ascending and descending it all
the time, as many sitting-out nooks were arranged
near the water's edge. The interior fixings were
very bright, and included multifarious drapings of
the flag that braved ; also some highly-polished tor-
pedoes and cutlasses, and other harmless toys.
Bluejackets stood picturesquely about, holding
cardtrays and rope barriers, and everything
smacked of the briny. Fewer brass buttons
were among the throng than might have been
expected, as some of the ships are away. It’s
drawing near Melb. Cup-time, and they are due
at Port Phillip. It was a crush, and there were
bunches of girls left blooming alone round the
walls. Blondes were in the ascendant for gown-
ing and looks. The wife of one of the hosts was
plump and pleasing, bechiffoned in blue. A
matron, who usually scores in her original ideas,
looked one of the best, in a golden robe with vivid
sleeves. An elderly dame, with a silver bang, had
badly-matched back-hair. Head-ornaments were
varied ; stiff tortoiseshell daggers pierced several
knobs ; some had a simple flower skewered in and
tickling the wearer’s neck ; the fashion introduced
at the recent powder-and-patch ball, of having
little black feathers bobbing overhead, is spread*
ing —two or three were thus decorated, like a
miniature hearse-horse. One girl was topped with
a pallid flower-spray, like a wedding-cake. Tho
music was up-to-date, but the band beat loud and
fast, and was a lap ahead of the dancers from start
to finish.
It’s rather a staggerer to find that Mark Ham-
bourg doesn’t like Australia, and has every inten-
tion of continuing to stay away from this be-
nighted land when once he leaves it. He has
been telling anxious inquirers who seek informa
tion that the criticisms of his playing have disap-
pointed him —they were not discriminating enough.
On his farewell appearance here as a lion he
seemed to turn restive under the adoration-shower,
and evidently found the idol-pose a bore. The
brothers lately, under Sydney Moss’s guidance,
went Blue-mountaineering and did the Jenolan
caves, Jakoff very nervously until his brother
heartened him up with promises of water-picnics,
with games of rounders and other infantile
delights.
Gov. Duff’s neglected grave in Waverley Ceme-
tery has been brought into notice, and some
hideously practical person suggests that each
winner of the A..T.C. Duff Memorial Stakes, run
once a year, shall pay <£s towards keeping the
deceased pro-consul’s grave green. It was the
same sort of incongruity of ideas which suggested
a ball in aid of St. Vincent’s Hospice for the
dying, or for the victims of some sickening disas-
ter. The Taramung wreck brought out a sad-
colored festivity in Melb. some years ago ; the
supper-table was adorned with sugar ships wallow-
ing in seas of whipped-cream. The Dance of
Death !
At the S.A.O. concert last week the novelty of
the evening was a well-played orchestral suite by
the fashionable French composeress, Chaminade,
whose ballet music, until now unknown, proves to
be ahead of her over-rated drawing-room ballads.
She wants more room to turn about in than a song
allows her ; were she a press-woman, she’d be a
better leader-writer than a paragraphist. Many
of the honors were feminine ; the soloist was that
well-known songstress, Miss Josephine Deakin,
and the harpist rippled satisfactorily her impor-
tant color-giving trills in the Hungarian music.
The folk who suffer most at a classical sing-song
are the fathers who chaperon strings of musical
daughters. It would add to the attractiveness of
such a show greatly if concert-givers could plan a
waiting-room for fathers —a place furnished
plainly, with some packs of cards, and a little
whisky-and-soda.
The genius selected to annotate the S.A.O. pro-
grammes penned a feverish verbal obligato on
the principal item. In his wild struggles to ex-
plain the meaning of the music, he remarks : “ In
the allegro con brio a tremendous tragedy is
crowded into a few pages. Fate knocking at the
door evidently does not enter the house without a
fearful combat. The movement ends with a
crash.” These are gems culled from the saner
parts of the description, but he lets himself go
with a rush in the scherzo. “The middle of tho
movement is founded on a rapid passage for the
double basses fortissimo, which shakes the orches-
tra to its foundations, and might be intended to
describe the gambols of an elephant—but the
gamesome beast retires by degrees, and the noise
of his antics is gradually lost. The whole dies
away in a beautiful soft passage for the wind.
After this comes the transformation-scene, and
the composer mounts to Heaven in a chariot of
fire.” It is this sort of gushful idiocy that makes
ordinary common-sense persons ashamed to say
they like classical music.
Amy Sherwin’s admirers, though not numerous
enough to crowd the Town Hall on either Satur-
day or Monday evening, yet welcomed the song-
stress back very warmly, and handed up the usual
posies. as a guarantee of good faith. On Monday
Madame warbled from under a wall-paper frock
with billows of skirt and pretty purple sleeves
and fixings; she duetted with tenor Barton
M’Guckin, who came out without his voice. A.
Deane’s best effort was “ Thou’rt passing hence,”
in the Santley manner. A pianist with a super-
fluity of consonants wandered quietly on to the
platform and glided unobtrusively into Chopin.
His name is several sizes too large for him ; one
syllable would fit him better. All the vocalists of
this co. have the latest London style of singing
with a small piece of paper daintily held in front
of the waist; the old method of flattening down
an open music-sheet with a large, white glove full
of hand, is quite extinct among stylish song-birds.
Eight-Hours’ Day brought out some weird and
wonderful garments that are never aired save on
holidays. I saw a patchy girl in as many colors
as a half-finished rainbow working her way Moore
Parkward on her bike; she had a red hat, a shot-
green blouse, a brown skirt, black hose, drab
gaiters, tan shoes, and the bicycle face. At the
Athletic Sports the stands were fairly filled with
girls, many of them clad in the popular style in
which the native hue of gaudy silk is sickbed o’er
with the pale cast of grass-lawn.
Sydney folk have been divided by the departed
delegates into beggars and bounders. “ Sassiety ”
shunted them, and slumites worried them for
coin, and everybody was ready to rush a free feed.
The keen-eyed inspection of invitation pasteboards
going on ever since the Hawkesbury crush flurries
local pleasurers, but their complaints of discour-
tesy only made old Melb. stagers smile. In the
Victorian capital a rigorous examination of tickets
at a public shivoo has operated for years, and no-
body minds.
A forthcoming wedding is the great topic of
Bathurst just now. Alderman Simmonds and the
matron of the hospital will shortly become one’;
their united ages will tot up to considerably over
a century and ten. Also, in Sydney, a Minister
of the Crown is going to marry again. The bride-
elect, taller than her prospective husband, has not
yet entered her 20th year.
Lily Dam pier’s benefit at the Lyceum on Friday
afternoon was droopsome. Scattered parties of
girls decorated the d.c. and munched chocolates.
Some clapping and some flowers were evoked, but
nobody enthused. Mr. Dampier and his daughter
gave a fine reading of the trial-scene from “The
Merchant.” Lily is a good Shakespearian when
she likes : and has, but not recently, smiled and
strutted her way through Arden most brightly as
Rosalind. Now she has to take to bushranging
and the baekblocks instead, to help to keep the
family-pot boiling.
The “ Arts and Crafts ” exhibition opened with
a members’ day on Saturday. The sensation
picture of the show is undoubtedly F. P.
Mahony’s “Struggling Centaurs,” especially for
hopeless gazers who don’t know what it means.
Its chief defect, to my mind, is the underdone girl
in the corner, who ought to be put back in the
oven fora light browning. Julian Ashton's most
remarkable contribution is a pastel picture of the
sort of nightmare-woman you would probably
dream of after a pork supper and a visit to
the hospital for consumptives. “ Lovely
Woman ” is treated by most exhibitors in her
aged and decrepid stage : Alice Muskett’s
sad-faced mourner has already been hung in
Paris; an elderly working-woman with a past,
and the toothache, sits pondering “ Shall
I have it out ? ” The poster show repre-
sents the newest freak of artistic fashion
—the lengthy lancer at the lamp-post and the
beefy bull and some airy girl-figures look like
popular hoarding-adorners. Hall Thorpe shows
some etchings of local subjects ; the glimpse of
the synagogue from the corner opposite has an
Oriental effect, and in another the Centennial
Park griffin smiles Sphinx-like over hand-bills.
The brass-work is a bright feature, and mostly
done by women with whom hammering and tap-
ping ornamentally on brass trays and cups is a
fashionable occupation. There are some cabinets
of old china. Vice-regal cutlery has clattered on
the Worcester set shown by Mrs. Baynton, which
is older than N.S.W. and of a beautiful S&vres-
like blue,
A local suburban church uses music as a helpful
ecclesiastical side-show. The very Nicest sheep
pasture in that fold, and the authorities, to keep
them in the straight, narrow path, have hired a
silvery tenor and an expensive baritone, who,
dressed up in cassock and surplice, adorn the choir
every Sunday and swell thecollection. Thechurch
“as bylaw established ” is making great efforts
to reach the masses. It can’t do that with its
shovel-hat and gaiters on, and so has determined
to employ lay help, especially women’s work. The
evangelist is a common, uneducated person em-
ployed as buffer between the high and mighty
dignitary and the vulgar masses.
The high priestess of a temple of fashion in the
city tells me that this season has been uncommonly
brisk. Orders have poured in liberally for race
and ( garden-party toggery. The Nicest people
don t buy the most expensive clothes. Sydney
smart women rarely go beyond three-and-a-hal'f
guineas for a hat, and they get their money’s worth
in advts. in the “ Social Columns,” which faith-
fully describe every rag and flower-petal adorning
a leader of sassiety. But at every race-season fair
unknowns purchase elaborate and costly head-
gear, flaunt their bravery abroad for a brief
season, and haven’t even the satisfaction of read-
ing a description in print in hybrid French-
English : “ Mrs. X. looked charming in a sweetly
pretty plisse silk with floral chapeau en suite?’
The six-guinea millinery is worn by flaring persons
whose names Respectable sassiety-column writers
would shudder to pen, or else by well-gilt country
dames who, their town season ended, bury the
costly creations in a baekblocks band-box.
A high-toned couple, who went honeymooning
a short time back, have rather flabbergasted
tradespeople by showing that, like Mrs. Gilpin,
though on pleasure they were bent, they had a
frugal mind. They had stores sent on ahead to
the remote spot which they had selected for their
bridal retirement, and, at the waning of the moon,
they carefully went through the remaining odds
and ends, and wrote to the providers asking them
to check off all the surplus sugar, tea, ham-
knuckles and heel-taps in the oilman’s stores, as
per invoice, and credit their account with the
same.
A popular teacher of singing got married the
other day, The night of his return from a brief
honeymoon-trip, a few of his pupils determined to
give him a “surprise” serenade. Betaking them-
selves to his house, which is a short distance out
of town, they ranged up under his front windows
and opened fire. But, unfortunately, influenza
had deprived them of their only two “bassos,”
and the deficiency soon made itself noticeable.
This was too much for the artistic ear of the
'* serenadee. ” Throwing open the door, he gravely
took his place among the singers, interpolated the
bass at the proper place without moving a
feature, and, the part-song concluded, returned
indoors as abruptly as he came out !
Emulation of the boys in brown who marched
on to glory at ’Ome is now burning in many
breasts. The spread of militarism throughout the
country has developed martial leanings in a pro-
fessional man practising in a coastal town (gossips
a correspondent of mine). The hero, a short man
with a moustache like an angry tooth-brush, drills
his family every night; his poor, meek wife, two
tall daughters, and four small, short-legged sons
are marched round till they are ready to drop
from fatigue—when their commanding officer pre-
scribes and administers two spoonfuls of cod-liver
oil each and orders the regiment off to bed. When
this nightly exercise goes on an admiring crowd
of neighbors jostle each other on the front path
and hang on to the railings to get a glimpse of
the fun.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Probably the most brilliant first-night audience
ever gathered together in this blighted capital
greeted ‘A Royal Divorce’ on Saturday night.
Every seat in the stalls back to the last three rows
were reserved, and the d.c. had a fringe two or
three deep long before the curtain rose. When it
did rise it displayed Hawtrey as Talleyrand in a
high state of satin breeches and lace ruffles, with
the celebrated sneer (as pourtrayed by Ward in
the National Gallery) in fine working order. The
sneer, by the way, is the only convincing trait
about the princely cynic ; according to Wills, his
cynicism was as biting as skim-milk.
‘Josephine’ Ferrar enters early, wearing that
much-beparagraphed white-and-gold court gown.
»She sacrifices personal appearance nobly to
historical accuracy throughout the play and
wore her hair in dangling little ‘ bob ’
curls surmounted by a coronet like a frying-pan.
In Act 3 she displays a further zeal for arclueolo-
gical precision by sporting a weird hat like to a
square box adorned with blue plumes. Miss
Page’s appearance suffers, too, from the fact that
the Archduchess Marie Louise had brick-colored
tresses, and wore them in ‘ barrel curls’ over her
brow like piles of small carrots. An eau-de-nil
satin dress worn with an emerald-green velvet
train and a fascinating bonnet with blue flowers
and white plumes, however, recalled the fact that
Miss E. P. is a most elegantly-built woman and
knows well how to wear fine clothes. A
flourish of trumpets, a couple of bars of
‘ Partant pour La Syrie,’ four glittering
officers and a band of black-legged soldiers mark
the entrance of Julius Nap. every time. Julius
has very little to do when he does enter, but
stands round in characteristic Napoleonic atti-
tudes, and he stands round for all he is worth.
His frown alone must be a heavy item to carry
about, to say nothing of the stuffed chest and
fierce-looking top-boots. His really fine voice
and splendidly-repressed method, however, get
one show to assert themselves in the scene in the
inn at Gemappes. Harry Hill, in the curly-
brimmed hat sacred to comic opera, whirls his
passionate words of love so wildly at the Empress
that he calls Julius a ‘ corsed Cursican ’ for a
‘ cursed Corsican ’ every time. Linda Raymond
makes Stephanie a lovable little French girl, with
an Austrylian accent, who calls the cynic ‘ Museer
1 ally-wrong,’ as though he were a shipping-clerk.
“ In one Thornton play, a heavy mother in side-
curls and 1830 bonnet sent shivers through the
spectators as a sampleof sartorial sorrows in store.
Without an impressive profile, a woman in a ‘ coal-
scuttle ’ is as dead to the world as Diogenes in his
tub. The piquante snub-nosed belle, all fluffy
fringe and dimples, will assuredly topple off her
perch if the early Victorian fashion, now very
visible on the Block, fairly catches on. Pos-
sessors of high noses, smooth dome foreheads,
and sleek abundant hair will, in that case, have it
all their own way. Some people predict even
more radical changes as a result of the threatened
casting-back to the styles of V.R.’s dowdy girl-
hood. Curate-cult and Sunday-school teaching
and all the demure airs and graces pertaining
thereto, are already emerging from disuse and
dried rose-leaves. Only ‘ a fine figure of a woman ’
can carry off the ponderous garments affected by
our grandmothers, and a fine figure’ implies arms,

“ Amy Sherwin’s costumes at her three Melb.
concerts thoroughly * fetched ’ the women. At

“ One of Melbourne’s most comical sights is to
watch the scores of girls and women, note book in
hand, sketching any original ideas exhibited in
the window of a leading Collins-street costumier.
I he latter is furious, and the real fun of the whole
thing is to see him raving inside as the opposi-
tionists steal his designs. Everyday he threatens
to adopt the London fashion of frosted windows
but his better sense prevails against cutting oflf his
nose to spite his face.
There is still romance in marriage—in Africa.
Some months ago a Melb. girl went to S. Africa
to wed her sweetheart. Hubby met her at the
port and married her at once, and, beirur pncrnrrpd
on a contract, had to start for home right away,
first by rail, then by Cape cart. On the way one
horse went dead lame, and the result was a camp
under the cart. The couch \was comfortable, and
a blazing fire, and the hobbled horses munching
grass, and the myriad voices of the night quite
charmed the bride. Presently a horrid hyaena or
something came along and proceeded to make a
meal off the lame horse. The husband awoke and
emptied his magazine rifle at everything in general;
later on, he found that he had bagged both his own
horses, but not the wild beast. Next morning
started a 25-mile walk to the nearest ‘ town.’
When they reached their final destination the
delay had lost them the house in which they were
to have dwelt, and they had to pay .£1 per week
for a one-roomed, mud-floored shanty that made
the bride weep. Fortunately, his wages are 30s.
per day, and now she writes: ‘Dear Charlie is
building a house of our own.’

“George Robertson and Co. have published
in book form Rita’s articles on Cottage Cookery,
lately* running their course through the columns
of Melbourne ’Erald. The business eye of
G, R. and Co. perceived money in these whole-
some kitchen hints and criticisms as soon as they
began to appear, and the volume might have been
predicted by the forthcoming authoress quite a
long time ago. Rita doesn’t actually teach many
things to the experienced ‘middle-class’ house-
wife, but she fills a long-felt want in the way of
information for most Melbourne missuses and
gals, whose culinary thoughts are generally con-
centrated upon the frying-pan when not turned to
the manufacture of indigestible scones and un-
necessary cakelets

At Charters Towers last week some men
who were being lowered down the Day Dawn p.c.,
No. 3 shaft, in the bucket, were suddenly reversed
in direction, going up instead of down, were
crashed through folding hardwood doors, which
their heads and shoulders threw open, and had to
balance themselves and hold on like grim death
1000 ft. deep. Further, one of them,
xl. Millican, got squeezed between the bow of the
uj if* e ' t * ler P u hey or a cross-beam, and
had his right arm cut clean off near the shoulder ;
yet he managed to hold on in his place until slowly
lowered back to the brace—all the time balancing
himself on one side of the bucket with a cramped
left-hand clutching the chain, and a separated
right-arm inside the bucket between the legs of
two of his mates ! A few hours later, in the hos-
pital, Millican, with one arm gone, and his re-
maining hand lacerated and fractured in several
places, was chatting cheerily with his father and
guessing that he d have to learn to shoot left-
handed—for he was a crack rifle-shot.
The unanimity of that so-called public
* iti - State - schools question) meeting at
Melb., last week, was a lovely travesty to those in
the know, and the method by which that unani-
mity was obtained was an eloquent criticism on
the honesty of the religious crowd that ran it.
Ihe front doors of the Town Hall were kept reli-
gious y locked until the advertised opening time,
7 p.m. ; and when they were cast open then, the
general public of mixed opinions found every seat
in the hall filled with a white-faced, anaemic
crowdl which grinned in triumph at how it had
dished the other side by sneaking in per side door.
On the platform were the Elect; the speakers and
the Examples of Piety held upas a glaringcontrast
to the young men who had been educated by
the present godless system.” Among these
examples were a man who draws a big annual
income from houses of ill-fame ; another who
chipped the name of his dead child off its
tombstone after a boom-lawsuit with its god-
a- ■ r ii a , n< * , afc j * eas * * wo people who have been
officially bowled out m unpardonable breaches of
the r ood Adulteration Act. The keynote of the
meeting was struck by the sleek-haired Arch-
deacon Langley who said “lam willing to leave
the question to the parents of the colony to decide
but never will I consent to its being settled on the
votes of the young men already educated under
the secular system.” That is to say—“ Let us
pick the voters and we’ll leave it to the popular
voice.” Booh!

The recent after-dinner observation of a
prominent Australian Q.C. in referring to the
wife of the restaurateur as “ petroleuse,” cost him
(1) a pulled nose in the presence of a large and in-
terested assemblage, (2) a neatly-written apology
which now hangs in the lady’s private office, and
(3) a cheque which clients, during the next few
months, will have to make good.

you know her?” “Yes, sir; the scholar is con-
temporary with all time.”

If a recent paragraph in London Truth
means anything, there’s some ugly connection
between the facts that, during the German Em-
peror’s recent yachting cruise, His Majesty
received a thumping black eye, and that, the fol-
lowing day, one of the vessel’s officers went ashore
and mysteriously evaporated—the official yarn
being that his bicycle had run him into the sea
and drowned him.

B. E. Minns, The Bulletin artist now in
London, and making a big mark in black-and-
white, was, at latest, engaged in illustrating half-
a-dozen ghost-sj;ories for Pearson’s Magazine.
Arthur Streeton had a studio in Chelsea, near
Minns’, and was just starting to paint. He had
some nice little things, Cairo figure - subjects
chiefly, but he was not well when in Egypt, and
produced little for the time he spent there.
Streeton seems to have dropped the idea of holding
a London show.
Gov. Lamington (Q.) either reads his
speeches, or gets them off by rote, and recites
them like a schoolboy “ speaking a piece.” When
H. Ex. (they call him “ Lambing ” now) gave his
public Jubilee dinner to all local M’sP., the Bris-
bane papers reported a very flowery oration about
Queen Victoria. It read well, and placed the
Governor as a top-form orator, so to speak. But
the delivery of the speech was quite another affair.
H.E. read it. At some more than usually loyal
aspiration, one of the guests interjected, or was it
a mere hiccough? That threw the orator’s eyes
off his MS., and it took him 2min. 33 l-ssec. to
find “the place” again.

Deai- Bulletin, —The remark about a man
having once been fined in Australia for calling
another man a Christian, reminds me that B. D.
Morehead, then a giddy wit of much demerit,
was once, in the eighties, called to order in Q.
Assembly for alluding to Aland, M.L. A., as “the
pious and holy member.” Morehead promptly
withdrew the remark and called Aland “ the im-
pious and unholy member ” instead. He wasn’t
called to order that time, which is interesting, as
showing that holiness was a thing that nobody
liked to be suspected of up Brisbane way in those
days of rum and boodle. - D.
At 20 yards.—Size of real target reduced to one-half.
At 80 yards.—Size of target reduced to one-half.
At 50 yards.—Size of real target reduced to one
fourth.
On examining the targets used in revol-
ver-shooting by Jack Hamilton, M.L.A., the
committee of the Queensland Rifle Association
declared that the 30yds. target also counted as 7
bulls ; so that the total made by Hamilton was 123
(and not 122) points out of a possible 126 : —At
20yds., 39; at 30yds., 42; at 50yds., 42. At 20
and 30yds. distances the target had bulls 2in. in
diameter ; at 50yds., the bull was 4in. in diameter
—the same size as in use at Bisley (Eng.), where,
however, there is no 30yds. distance. One differ-
ence between Queensland and English matches is
that in Queensland the 18 shots must be consecu-
tively fired, whereas in England the aggregate
of different distances is taken. Distances are
thus alternated at pleasure, which is a great advant-
age over consecutive shots. The feat of getting
two “ possibles ” in 12 consecutive shots is a world-
record of which Australia may be proud.
A story of Tillett. He wanted some
Birmingham strikers to follow a certain course,
and talked to them for two hours ; they applauded
him enthusiastically. He therefore felt perfectly
safe ; the whole crowd was with him. A ballot,
besides looking well, was quite safe. So one was
taken. Result—For Tillett (say), 1; against (say),
999. Tillett was astounded. One of them, how-
ever, explained things. “You see, we knew the
little beggar, and how he’d talk us into doing any-
thing he liked, so we just pledged ourselves before-
hand.”
Re the approaching visit to Australia of
Baron Ardilaun (another Guinness), the family
may thank Daniel O’Connell for their success in
the “ pig-brand ” trade. At an election in the
early thirties “old” Arthur Guinness promised
his support to O’Connell and broke that promise.
“Dan ” then advised all his supporters to punish
Guinness by boycotting his stout. They took his
advice, and, as the Dublin publicans were to a
man supporters of O’Connell, Guinness had to look
for a foreign market : hence the immense export
trade. Ardilaun is first-cousin to Chief Justice
Darley, who may have somethin ' to do with in-
ducing the ex-stout man to visit .his country,
A surprising fact is the number of silent
plutocrats contained in every community and
practically unheard of until their deaths make
known how the State has profited in probate. The
other day there died in Collingwood (Vic.) a local
butcher, John Pritchard, whose burly dungaree-
clad form has figured in his Smith-street shop
since 1856. Up to the time of his last illness he
had never missed a single day from work ; he was
on the spot at 5 a.m. daily to see that his men
were on time, and was the last to leave on Satur-
day night. Yet Pritchard left about ,£300,000,
and a few years back he was certainly worth
half-a-million. Ten years ago, when the local
Council thought of borrowing money, Pritchard
took its breath away by calmly offering to lend
,£lOO,OOO “on his own.’’ His stipulation for G! 2
per cent., however, ended negotiations. As the
owner of a couple of Riverine stations, Pritchard
seldom went to the saleyards, but whenever he did
he was noted for one peculiarity. If any other
butcher invited the crowd “across the road,”
Pritchard’s drink was always schnapps (sixpence),
but whenever he was the host he gave the guests
no time to talk but demanded “ Four beers ! ” (3d.
each) as soon as his foot was on the front step. Of
such often is the Kingdom of Cash.
Ihe Broughton Club, opened by Speaker
Abbott, last week, in Sydney, and intended as a
rendezvous for the cloth, commemorates an Angli-
can bishop who almost created civil war in
N.S.W. to prevent the head of the R.C. church,
or an v church but his own, being called Bishop.
Broug.iton was originally a Treasury clerk, and all
through life displayed a fine faculty for discerning
the drift of coin. “If the designs of Bishop
Broughton had been carried out,” eulogised
Speaker Abbott, “ this would have been one of
the best-equipped churches on this side of the
Equator.” That is, it would now have been self-
supporting and even rich from the rents of school-
lands.
Otto Brandon to The Bulletin from
Shepparton, Vic. :
About time some of the friends of Mrs. Barney Barnato
contradicted the untruthful and damaging paragraphs
which constantly appear in print about her. She is not
a sister of Alice Holbrook, she was not a barmaid, and
she did not leave Melbourne because she had made it too
hot to hold her. The lady who is now Mrs. Barnato was
one of two sisters—Misses Emily and Affy Bevan—at one
time engaged by Williamson, Garner and Musgrove, and
who afterwards (in 1889) went to South Africa with Emma
Chambers and Albert Marsh. Affy (who was of great
personal attractions, and was at one time much photo-
graphed by Falk) became Mrs. Barnato, and it would
much surprise any member of the Chambers co. (of which
the present writer was one) to hear that she was other
than a well-conducted, highly-respected girl.
Premier Seddon, since his return to Maori-
land, has developed some quite-English little ways,
writes one of his numerous enemies. His ola
bluff style of speech has been much modified, and
he now speaks deliberately and with careful
pauses ; also, he stops and addresses one and all,
even the understrappers who bring him docu-
ments, as “ sir,” “no, sir,” “ yes, sir.” Before he
went to England it was “ l)ick,” “Tom,’’ and
“Harry,” or “Brown,” “Jones,” and “Robin-
son,” with an “old man” or two thrown in.
However, when the Right Hon. gent meets one
or two of his old West Coast diggers and calls them
“sir ” there will be sanguinary language, and all
the Chamberlain veneer will peel in one act.
One of Queen Victoria’s sweet womanly
characteristics is her never-failing objection to
tobacco-sinoke. Nobody—including her late, very
late, husband and her nicotined eldest son—
was ever allowed to blow a cloud in any room
where she was likely to smell it. John Brown
was an al fresco exception to the royal rule against
smoking, but even that “faithful gillie’’ didn’t
smoke before her. When he was following V.R.’s
pony chaise in its slow and stately procession
around Windsor Castle or Balmoral district, John
invariably gave himself permission to light his
pipe. If, perchance, he came forward to swear at
the intractable pony Brown’s briar was smuggled
away somewhere in his Hielan’ costume —pro-
bably the pocket of his kilt.

Q. Meteorologist Wragge is not satisfied
with having definitely nicknamed the Almighty
the “Infinite Dynamo.” After dealing out
various smaller baptismal certificates, he has now
re-christened Him (or It ?)“ the great Ego Sum.”
This is retracking on the trail of the Hebrew
Why not break out into new fields ? The “ Great
Go Forth,” the “Pervasive Übi,” the “Infinite
Inertia,” the “Logocosm,” the “Sempivibrant ”
the “ Whatnot Is,” or beautiful appellations of
that sort ? All the same, Wragge had better look
out that the Almighty, or someone doesn’t re-
christen him, for playing chuekstones with the
names of the variant Joss. “ For yet they are not
three Josses but one Joss,” and a patent trade-
label is badly needed, to have force throughout the
world. How would “ Chiffon ” suit Wragge as
an alter-nomen ? “ Clement Wragge, the Eminent
Chiffon!” The meteorological ramasseur ! The
Remnant !


The Eye-sight measure per post by the in-
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraelough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection—
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraelough-*

Business, Bobbery , etc .
Apropos of the Lucknow dynamite-bomb busi-
ness and of explosions at ammunition-factories, the agent
of a ’Frisco dynamite-factory (the man who is selling
dynamite in Australia at a shilling a pound or so to the
great disgust of the other dynamite crowd) says: “We
employ many Chinese at our works, but whenever there
is an explosion scores of fresh Chows are ready to fill the
breach. By George Washington, a dynamite explosion is
real business. When the mixing-house last went-up ”
[“went-up” is good]—“ seven or eight Chinamen went
with it, and the only trace we ever found of any one of
those Chows was the heel of one india-rubber boot. No
bones, blood, hair, or other horrors —only that one poor
little boot-heel.”
* * * *
The quietest Parliamentary enquiry on record is
that now being made into the circumstances surround-
ing the over-ruling of the decision of a certain mining
warden by a certain Minister of Mines. It appears that a
careless Government survey of a certain important mining
field left open a wedge of auriferous land between the
leases held by a certain very prosperous and well-managed
mining co. The local warden, recognising that the omis-
sion of this bit of land in the mining co.’s lease was due
to careless survey, refused to grant aleaseof the wedge to a
certain speculator, but the latter got the warden’s decision
over-ruled by the Minister, and the speculator now leaves
it to the co. to (Ist) either pay him an enormous sum
(£26,000) for the lease, which practically cuts their lode,
or (2nd) to force the Minister to back-down—hideous
would be the consequences thereof ! —or (3rd) get his
decision upheld by the Cabinet. A very, very interesting
case.
« * • •
Nearly two years ago, a shrewd Melbourne
solicitor lost from hissafe £2OO worth of diamond jewellery.
Instead of advertising his loss he lay low. He was
approached recently, by the thief’s go-between, to
“ arrange a recovery.” Price asked, £75. Gent One
astonished the go-between by pretending to have almost
forgotten the loss ! “ The stones were only paste, and
very poor paste at that,” but he wouldn’t mind springing
a “ fiver ” for their return, just for the sake of the
settings. It’s hard to believe, but the thief and his pal
actually fell in, and the lawyer got back his real diamond
jewellery for a fortieth of its actual value.

A fifteen - year - old Melb. draper’s assistant
accounted to his mother for the sappiness of his boots
when he returned home one fine evening recently by
explaining that he had been hosing and walking on the
bulk of “the firm’s ” last season’s stock in their back yard
in anticipation of their next day’s clearing-sale of “ new
and fashionable goods just arrived ex. So-and-So, dam-
aged by fire and water, and to be cleared at a huge ioss.”

A Bulletin correspondent on ‘ ‘ More Mercan-
tile Morality ” Tub Bulletin has written of a moral
merchant who runs six people on three 1 non-transferable '
annual railway tickets bv adopting the simple device of
having the tickets posted from place to place as required.
Thereis an Australian firm which works a much bigger
swindle than this. It deals considerably in (amongst
other lines) oils. Many of its shipments figure as ‘ kero-
sene ’on the bills-of-lading. Sometimes almost a whole
cargo is invoiced as ‘ kerosene.’ The cases, of course, are
kerosene-cases. The tins resemble kerosene-tins, and
have a little mineral oil on the outside. If you turn the
taps of most of them kerosene will run out—a little. But
just half the tins do not contain oil of any kind (except
fusel), nevertheless. They hold nothing but German
potato-spirit, on which (as alcohol) the duty is (say) 14s.
per gal. The kerosene duty was never over 6d. per gal. By
paying duty as kerosene there is a clear gain of £2l4s. per
case. And cases have from time to time arrived by the
hundred, so judge ofthe profit to the merchant. Thescheme
has been cleverly worked. The spirit is not sent alone
but mixed with genuine kerosene—tin for tin in each case.
The tins of spirits used to have metal tubes containing
kerosene attached to the screw-tops. This precaution is
now more or less abandoned. There is no longer much
use for it because a prominent Customs official knows all
about the little game. The profit does not stop at evading

« « * *
A Dunedin (M.L.) soft-goodsman, possessing
excellent gifts as an advertiser, but whose relations with
his employes are characterised by a certain want of
bountifulness, had a disagreement with his carter re-
cently, during which the latter expressed his feelings in
an undertone. ‘ ‘ Speak out,” said the boss, ‘‘ I don’t like to
hear people mumbling in that way.” “Yes, and get the
run I ’* retorted the carter. “No, I promise you you
shan’t,” replied the dealer. “ Well, then, I never in all
my life met such a rotten blankety blank as you
I” The soft-goodsman was stiffened for a moment,
and, despite his promise, sacked the candid carter on the
spot. Next day, however, when the latter returned to
fetch away some personal belongings, the boss relented
and told him to resume work.
* * * •
A new dodge to defraud the revenue has lately
come under the notice of the Sydney Stamp Office —viz.,
the giving of receipts marked “ Interim ” (with a view of
avoiding the necessity of affixing a 2d. stamp), and at no
later period issuing duly stamped receipts in lieu. An
impression also prevails in certain commercial circles
that a receipt “ on account ” need not be stamped, which
is wrong, the only exceptions allowed being money re-
ceived by Govt., money received for the purchase of
stocks and shares in banking and public co’s, laborers’
receipts for wages, and depositors’ hank-receipts.
* • *
Ed. Bulletin, — Re cyanide, .although you have
clearly stated the position of the Australian Gold-Recovery
Go. with regard to the application for amendment in
N.S.W., Victoria, and M.L., nothing has, so far, been said
as to the position in Westralia. Letters of registration
were granted here to the Cassel Gold-Extracting
Co., as assignees of the inventors, such letters having
the same force and effect as letters patent.
The Comptroller having allowed the amendment
in the English specification, leave to amend was
granted immediately on application at W.A. Patent-office,
it being held that the W.A. letters were solely dependent
on the Eng. grant. According to the variable Sep. Burt.
Attorney-General, this opinion is an erroneous one.
Further still, greater complications are looming. No
opportunity was given to the public to oppose the applica-
tion for amendment, and, as the provisions of the Act
apply in the same way mutatis mutandis to letters of
registration as to letters patent, the amendment cannot
be considered valid. Two or three g.m. co’s in the direc-
tion of Kaltroorlie refuse to pay royalty for the use of the
process, holding that the amendment is invalid. The
patentees are in a quandary.— Patent Agent, W.A.
* * * *
Nemesis has pursued nearly all the Victorian
coal co’s. which were floated and exploited by the land-
boom crowds. Eleven of them, with more or less shady
directorates,"have either closed down or gone into an in-
definite sort of liquidation. The four or five co's. still
afloat are almost the only ones whose directorates are
free from the ancient fish-like odor of the land-boom.
Rare instances of honesty being its own reward.
* * * *
A very smart personage, a marvellous salesman,
who had already sold to two different parties, fcr a sub-
stantial consideration in each case, the “sole” local
rights of his wonderful invention, essayed to trade
the same “sole” rights off for the third time in
the same city 1 But the game leaked out somehow, and
on the day he was to have signed up and received his
cheque the two first victims “ got the office” from the
intended victim, and whilst a detective (rigged up as a
clerk) was chatting with the swindler in the merchant’s
office, the merchant popped out, and returned—not with
the anticipated cheque, but with the other two sole
agents. Threatened with prosecution for obtaining
money under false pretences, he disgorged handsomely,
and is now seeking fresh fields.
» * * *
Possibly the game worked with the most profit
during the land-boom was that of the influential director
of many companies, who would shift all the cronk assets
of the institution with which he stood best, on to the
others in which he held only a small interest. An enter-
prising and pious Victorian has just discovered the appli-
cability of the principle to other commodities. His firm
was loaded up with a line of, say, bungholes which, being
of an obsolete pattern and practically worthless, were
quite unsaleable, so he recently sold them at cost price
to another shaky concern which he directs, but in which
the poor public hold most of the shares. If the latter
goes down the good man only loses a few hundreds as a
small shareholder, and meanwhile he saves a few thou-
sands by getting rid of his valueless stock.
A blackmailer (in the guise of a share-
broker) has just made three hauls from Custom
House officers who, he found out, had, contrary to
regulations, taken commission on the sale of shares
in a mine which they were floating svb-rosa.
♦ • * »
The latest Melb. swindle is only workable
by confederates. A nautical, weather-beaten-look-
ing chap offers —in strict secrecy —say 51b. of any
well-known brand of imported tobacco, duly labelled
and quite genuine, for half its value. Those who
are not above taking a bit out of the revenue, and
buy the presumably smuggled weed, are visited
next day by an official-looking gentleman, who in-
forms the victim that so-and-so has been caught
smuggling tobacco, and has given his (the victim’s)
name amongst others as a receiver. The victim,
believing No. 2 to be a Custom House official, is
only too glad to save a threatened expose by pay-
ing sometimes ten times the value of the tobacco
“to square it,” and the precious pair whack up a
handsome profit between them as the result. They
(the swindlers) buy their tobacco in the ordinary
way, and Tub Bulletin imagines that all their
victims will prefer to do likewise in future.
* * •
D. : “ A man whom I know finds it of
material importance, on shifting his residence, to
always move to a terrace, where he uses his utmost
endeavours to get his rent reduced below what the
other tenants are paying; having no family he
generally succeeds. After a few months he moves
again, rent six or seven weeks in arrears. Land-
lord generally lets him go scot-free under the cun-
ning threat of his tenant that, if stopped, he
will let the other tenants (he generally chooses
big terraces) know that he has been paying, say, 13s.
a week while they are paying 16s. The landlord
mostly always sees the force of the argument.”
* • * *
A result of the additional beer-tax in
Queensland is an agitation to let down the brewers
easily by lowering the import-duty on malt. The
brewers ! The brewers don’t care a rap about the
tax ; they are all right. They have raised the price
per hhd. on the publicans, and their troubles ! The
reduction of import-duties on malt would be a
mistake, since it is unmistakable that good
malting-barley can be grown in Q., a-nd local agriculture
needs all encouragement.
The brewer is top-dog always, beer-excise-tax or
no beer-excise. M. has £5OO, and is searched out by a
brewery and offered a pub. The incoming price is £750.
M. pays down his £5OO, and gives a p.n. for £250. If tha,t
p.n. is met, well and good ; if M. can keep up the periodi-
cal interest on it, it may be well and good. But the
moment he is in trouble, Brewery X. gets him to sign a
transfer of the license to , leaving a blank for any
name to be inserted. Then Brewery X. hunts around for
another man with £5OO, or more or less. Immediately
the latter is found, the blanks in the transfer are filled in,
and the legal “ notice of transfer” is at once advertised in
the local newspapers, &c., often without advising the cur-
rent licensee of the fact! Even if he denies to the Licens-
ing Court that he wants to transfer, the bench is with
Brewery X., for there is the publican’s signature to the
transfer, and—out he goes !

* * * *
“Beautifully white diamonds have been found
at Inverell, N.S. Wales, and, from the rich yield of the
mine and the white color of the stones, great things were
expected. A parcel of many hundred carats came to
England, when it was found that they were so hard as to
be practically unworkable as gems, and I believe they
were ultimately sold for rock-boring purposes.”— Prof.
Crookes to London Royol Institution.
* • * *
The Perth Mint building proceeds deviously on
its way to incompletion. Again and again the work has
been stopped for the most flimsy of reasons. Meantime
the gold-yield is being merrily exploited by the banks
and shipping co.’s. At present not less than 2s. per oz.
of gold has to be paid to get it to Melbourne or London
for sale. The bank charges commission, the shipping co.’s
charge freight, the insurance co.’s “have a cut,” and the
railways aren’t quite out of it. Two shillings per
ounce on 300,0000 z. amounts to 130,000, and that is just
about the yearly rate that the Westralian gold-getters are
paying for having their gold coined at someone else’s
Mint. If the Mint has to be opened in Perth, there is
every reason to get it in work soon. Of course, the 2s.
per ounce is divided up amongst fat companies, and that
consideration, perhaps, excuses Forrest a good deal. Wes-
tralia is now exporting more or less gold, and is import-
ing sovereigns, thus paying shipping, insurance, and
banking companies twice over, when no expense is neces-
sary at all.

The Westralian Mines Minister is between devil
and deep sea, as he must either offend the capitalist by
refusing exemption, or increase the number of unein-
ploj'ed by granting it. So far, the capitalist has the best
of it.
On June 30th there were 6070 leases current in
Westralia, with an area of 103,801 acres, employing 17,500
men, being about one man to six acres as required by the
regulations. As many leases have from 20 to 200 men em-
ployed on thenq by onlyallowin -five men each to3sooleases
-—a ridiculously low average— .here would remain 3470 on
which no men are employed at all. The return also shows
that for the half-year ended June 30th 265,3290 z. were
produced, equal to £l2O per man per annum, or about
£6O less than would be paid him in wages for the same
period ; and as the average yield of gold per ton was loz.
12dwt. 21gr., it would tend to show that in the future the
Westralian goldfields will not be above the average of
other fields in their output. Any superiority that they
-may have will be due to the large number of mines which
may be worked on the immense areas within their boun-
daries.

“A Disgusted Shareholder” sends along the
alleged history of the Ernest Giles prospecting syndicate
to show why Sydney people are slow to plunge into
mining. If The Bulletin were worth a million it might,
publish the letter in full. The subscribed capital, £6OOO,
has gone, some of it in firearms and other equipment,
camels, stores. &c., some in salaries—even the secretary
got £lO a week. And some of it is missing. Three or
four leaders followed Giles, several mines were found, in-
cluding the Nellie Farren, which was supposed to have
been sold for £7OOO on a £5OO deposit. The deposit can’t
be found if ever paid. The beautiful prospecting and ex-
ploring equipment was recently sold in Coolgardie for
£l5O the lot. Several meetings, too, have been held in
Sydney, at which the management has had nasty things
said of it, and motions for the adoption of reports and
balance-sheets have been negatived. And in its early
career the show was regularly tipped every Sabbath as
one of the good things.

N.S.W. “ Lawyer’s Clerk ” to Bulletin :
Plucked 1 That’s what you call it. But I call it a rob-
bing scheme. I stand for admission as articled law clerk.
Between us we pay in fees over £2OO. The examiners get
these fees. As the exam, is too stiff we’re plucked by the
score and must go back in a couple of months with more
fees. The examiners get these fees, too, and so on. Is
payment by fees the best for such exams. ?
Malvern, the one-horse Melb. suburb,
where ex-Premier Jimmy Munro hides his dimi-
nished head, seems to be suffering from a virtuous
son-in-law of that busted Rechabite. His son-in-
law, by name Balding, lately fetched a policeman
along to join with him in a crimson blush at
certain “indecent” pictures displayed by a local
tobacconist. They were just ordinary cigarette
advt. pictures, and nobody knew they were bad
for public morals until Balding and the “ bobby ”
brought them before the Malvern jaypays. More-
over, the Bench, which must have drifted into
wickedness since the decline and fall of Munro and
family, threw out the prosecution with profound
(ontempt, so Balding’s blush stands where it did.
“ WHAT PLICE ME?"
Passed through Port Augusta (S.A.) the
other day a man and his wife, with a two-horse
team, bound from Coolgardie to Bourke. They
put the journey down at 4| months, nearly three
of which have been consumed. Two goats formed
part of the caravan ; these and the horses were in
good condition.

S.A. Northern farmers are imploring
Govt, to forego Crown rents and give other assis-
tance ; and they send down a list of what they
call sample cases. Two of them : (1) “In Jan.,
I had 6000 sheep and have now 79 left. I
have lost 8 cattle out of 13, and 8 horses out of 12.
I have paid the Govt. .£lOO for shifting stock, and
have bought ohaff to the value of <£9o. I have
sown 120 acres two years in succession and reaped
nothing.” (2) “ I had no wheat in 1894, five bags
in 1895, nothing in 1896. I have lost 26 horses,
being all I had, and 32 out of 34 cattle. ” The
Govt, promises to consider the cases submitted in-
dividually. Altogether, if the reports really don’t
do violence to the facts, it looks like another case
of an industry beaten by climate. The same
thing has occurred in some states of America ;
a writer in the N.A. Review said that, owing to
insufficient rainfall, Kansas had lost 250,000
people.
There are Polynesian Sugar Acts in Q.
under which Kanakas are forbidden to work at
anything but “ field work ” in connection with
sugar-plantations, and cannot legally take employ-
ment in any service whatever outside of sugar-
farms. The Q. Govt, doesn’t care a rap for these
Acts. Kanakas are employed indiscriminately at
all sorts of work in S.Q. Every Polynesian in-
spector around the infeoted districts has chronio
myopia, and trusts to earn his promotion faith-
fully. The Kanakas, even if they barracked for
the white man’s wages, which they don’t, would
be preferred to white men. They are a curse to
the white wage-seeker, who seldom gets a chance
to be a wage-earner. If the Govt, didn’t know be-
fore —though it is clear that they must have known
—theyknownow how numerously bullock-waggons
are driven through the Polynesian Acts; for
Medland Mitchell, the man who succeeded in
getting a Minister of the Crown, a leading sassiety
lady, and several choice citizens fined for illegally
employing Kanakas, has given the whole pano-
rama away to the Brisbane press. “ Hundreds of
Kanakas,” he says, “ are employed without agree-
ments at all kinds of work.” within coo-ey, so to
speak, of Brisbane. The slave-loving Govt,
doesn’t move a finger, however, but leaves prose-
cutions to be undertaken by “ informers.” May
the Lord send along the “ informers,” and “ many
on ’em.”

BETHNAL GREEN, LONDON.
“A prominent Vic. church is much worrited
just now by the squabbling of its agents on a
foreign mission - field. Somehow, none of t e
women, brought up in this particular denomination
on porridge and the Shorter Catechism, fret them-
selves much over the doings of the heathen in ms
blindness. Failing the home - trained article,
damsels have to be got in from other sects, whic
leads to trouble. No one accuses the hirelings or
rescuing the perishing with the wrong or
doctrine, but, in their innocence, they taught tne
converts that smoking and salvation never went
together. The riot began when the he-missionary
arrived from the auld countree to superintend tne
mission wi’ a short black cutty in his mouth, and
no more objection than most of his brethren to a
wee drappie, ’ as a precaution against fever.
“From that moment, it was war to the knife.
The maidens told such blood - curd-ling tales or
their superior that a long-headed ex-bank-director
was sent from Melb. to see the combatants through
their trouble. He oame back strong on the side
of his brother man, but the ladies’ committees
here sniff at his evidence, side with their own sex,
and weep scalding tears over the budgets which
arrive by every mail treating of the Male Monster
--his bottles, his Godless company, his dear
dead wife, and goodness knows what else.
“Meantime, Melbournians interested in perish-
ing pagans hold back their subscriptions, on the
grounds that they never promised to support
‘fighting-cocks,’ as they rudely term the dis-
putants. Another section surmises that peace
might have been restored long ago if a wealthy
Victorian spinster, at the beginning of the row,
had not set off to the battle-field to be a sister to
those oppressed girl-missionaries.
“It is about time that the rather spitefully
paragraphed wife of a certain Australian Premier
got credit for her consistent self-respeot. The
snobs who held aloof from her when she was
nobody in particular found the worthy woman
quite indifferent to their advances when she
became somebody of importance, whereas her
less toney acquaintances of long ago are warmly
invited to remember that friendship improves by
keeping. There is no offence in the demonstrative
patronage of one who is too proud to be patronised
and too honest to feign forgetfulness of an affront.
“A development of the looal cycling craze is a
tandem ridden (in Collins-st.) by two bloomerod
ladies, followed by two ferocious-looking dogs.
“ Most people are surprised at Marshall Hall’s
condescension in consenting to conduct a mere
Liedertafel. Probably he regards himself, in his
r6le of a musical Messiah, as called upon to shed
his radiance on the dark places of harmonio
Philistinism whenever opportunity offers.
“Talkingof the Ormond Professor’s disapproval
of Hambourg methods, the worst of these
academic musicians is that their students would
need to be Methuselahs to give the system a fair
trial. On the other hand, the Prof.’s strong point
is the enthusiasm and energy he awakes in
his pupils. Many of them lock themselves up alone
with the piano, six times per day for 20 minutes at
a time, and sit agape with flattened tongues,
uttering strange guttural sounds like clucking
hens, or nasal notes suggestive of snarling dogs,
by way of exercising the whole gamut of the vocal
apparatus. At other times they practise yawn-
ing, or contort their mouths in efforts to use
the palate as a sounding-board, vide Svengali.
After all, unless you’ve got ‘ a fiddle in your kist ’
(as Scots say of people vocally gifted), a mountain
of work only results in the voice of a mouse.
Professor Marshall Hall, sad to say, iB still in
poor health.
“In street-crushes, policemen generally scruff
the smallest boy available, and tramp heavily on
the toes of disobedient old women. On the same
principle, perhaps, with all grades of criminals to
take an interest in, Melb. detectives lately com-
bined their talents to sheet her sins home to a
poor little palmist in the Eastern
Market. After letting her openly advertise her
silly, transparent little trade for ever so long,
offended Law fell suddenly upon the unsuspecting
Sibyl, and after gaoling her from Monday till
Thursday, fined her 405., in default seven days
imprisonment. It seems hard on the tearful cul-
prit when one considers that at most charity fairs,
and at nine out of ten church bazaars, a professional
palmist is hired to beguile the faithful of their
shekels. For “afternoons” and receptions, too,
hostesses retain fortune-tellers to amuse the guests
by reading their hands at so much per dozen.
Most law-breakers of this type are lone, lorn
females, ludicrously innocent and transparent in
their magic-methods. One particularly plump,
fresh-colored Melb. benedict, who went recently
to dip into the secrets of destiny, found the inter-
view embarrassing because the dark-eyed diviner
sat without speaking, glaring not at his hand but
his face through a large magnifying-glass. At last
she said he was a bachelor. He supposed she
came to this conclusion because she could discover
no traces of scratches, past or present; but his
wife, who disapproved of the proceeding when she
heard of it, thought the sallow soothsayer must
have wanted to spy out the secret of hubby’s
peachy complexion.
“ British provincials are less versed in the
catchwords and fads of the hour than their Colo-
nial compeers. At Jubilee time a party of Aus-
tralian lads touring in Dorsetshire were freshening
up a musty railway-compartment with eucalyptus
extract. An old lady fellow-passenger interested
in the unfamiliar perfume, sniffed so enthusiasti-
cally that one of the boys volunteered the inf or-
mation that the stuff was ‘ grand for microbes.
‘ Just what I’ve wanted all my life,’ exclaimed the
dame; ‘ insects are that bad in our part, and a
flga-bite on me is worse’n a wop’s-sting to most! ’
“ Conventionists and their womenkind are
hardly likely to have such a good time in Mel-
bourne, in January, as they had in Sydney.
Premier Turner is hardly disposed to spoil his
anticipated surplus by spending five or six thou-
sand pounds on junketting, and he would be horri-
fied at the idea of throwing away £lOOO on such a
disgusting orgy as the Hawkesbury River feed-
fight. Women had better stay away from Mel-
bourne in January, and leave the Conventionists
to work with as little indigestion as possible.
“ Miss Ethel Levien, Melb.’s prettiest brunette,
is at present in London working hard for the stage.
She is studying under Herman Vezin, who has a
high opinion of her ability. London people are
much struck with Miss Levien’s likeness to dainty
May Yohe.
“ I fear the photo.-zinco process is another
medium whereby man administers to the unholy
vanity of woman. Nowadays city photographers
are constantly on the look-out for that daughter
of Eve amongst their customers who will make &
‘ picture,’ and when she is discovered the fair
young thing is overwhelmed with a request to sit
in the interests of Art, and for the benefit of
humanity, and, of course, she sits, in draped
goods, holding a oalico lily or a stuffed canary,
and eventually wakes to find her ‘ picture ’ illumi-
nating the dull weeklies and labelled ‘Sweet
Dreams,’ or ‘Reverie,’ or ‘Will he Come?’ or
‘Summer,’or anything equally irrelevant, and
she is convinced at once ahd for ever that her
beauty is a danger to the peace of mind of men,
and a source of envy, hatred, and all uncharitable-
ness to women. Really the beauty of the pic-
tures (when it exists) is largely due to the re-
toucher —a negative virtue, so to speak—and the
block from the camera man’s point of view is
merely intended to lend verisimilitude to the bald
little advt. in the oorner: ‘ Photo, by Albumen
and Co. ’
“ Bereaved relatives forwarding some dear de-
parted, via steamship to the old folks at home,
nave to enter the consignment on the manifest
as ‘statuary,’ or ‘natural history specimens,’ to
spare the nerves of the crew. ‘ Ada Cambridge/
in one of her Australian stories, when the colossal,
ginger-headed hero, who crops up in all her books,
is accidentally shot, varies matters by exporting
him to his ancestral vault in a leaden coffin encased
in a huge packing case labelled ‘ Grand Piano ! ’
Once upon a time, a Queenslander, importing
a consignment of heavy books ( received his
packet in a bad state of repair and with a
statement written in pencil on its wooden
cover, signed by a number of stevedores to
the effeot that they believed the contents to be
‘ cold meat ’ and had ill-treated it in retaliation for
the supposed deception. Now that man always
packs weighty volumes in casks, which are not
likely to call up ideas of corpses, and, more-
over, can be rolled, if the lumpers don’t relish
lifting them.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed in,
and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

CURRENT “HOPS."


A young lady named Annie Looney, who
recently ran amok with a revolver at Feilding
(M.L.)i appears to have made a holy show of the
local police. One cop says that he got within 10
yards of Annie, but she threatened to shoot him,
so he went away to get his horse and his revolver
with a view to “riding her down”—he intended
overwhelming the girl with a charge of cavalry.
The other policeman says he saw Miss Looney
blazing away promiscuously at the universe and
intended capturing her, only she got behind a heap
of bricks. Under these difficult circumstances
both policemen stood off while Annie fired at
sundry school-children, the local doctor and the
baker’s horse, and plugged the population gene-
rally. Two bystanders ultimately captured this
gentle cow-girl, and, during her trial, she got out
of the dock and smote a barrister once on each ear,
and also banged him across the back with a chair.
By the time she was quite done assaulting the
barrister, the long, slow arm of justice hauled her
off and put her back in the dock again. Maori-
land is commonly credited with having the most
gradual and deep-thinking police-force in Austra-
lasia, and when it isn’t fast enough to prevent a
prisoner spanking a barrister in court it does seem
rather gradual.
Italy, the country from which Consul-
(General Corte wants Australia to import the
peaceful agricultural laborer in large quantities,
seems to keep the whole world supplied with
assassins. It was an Italian who murdered Presi-
dent Carnot, of France ; another Italian butchered
the Spanish Premier, Canovas del Castillo ; and
the latest news is that a man from sunny Italie
has been laying for President Diaz, of Mexico.
Diaz is about the most picturesque indi-
vidual in modern politics. Of the little
band — Juarez, Diaz, Gonzalez, Ortega, Comon-
fort, and a few more—who started, in 1861, the
hopeless-looking war of Liberty, and who, after
four years’ hard fighting, upset the old clerical
tyranny, and confiscated the vast church property,
and mashed up the great Holy Roman domina-
tion, Diaz was nearly one-half and Juarez
was the other. Gonzalez, Ortega and Comon-
fort were mainly fierce, bull-headed fighters and
nothing more. When the great French invasion
followed, Diaz was the man who licked the
first French army, and he was in at the
death by breaking-up the last hopes of the Im-
perialists in the Five Days’ battle outside Puebla.
In the three years which lay between these two
events, he fought with grim persistence against all
manner of odds ; and when Puebla was at its last
gasp, and the walls crumbled down and the garri-
son lost heart and went and hid itself in a cellar,
Diaz stood in the breach alone with one big gun,
and faced a storming-party single-handed. As
President he covered Mexico with roads and rail-
ways, created a system of universal compulsory
education, abolished brigandage, gave absolute
freedom to the press, paid up arrears of the public
debt, and generally resurrected the sleepy land of
Anahuac. Among modern rulers Diaz looks like
the one who should be safest from assassination,
but the average Italian seems to plug everybody
promiscuously.
Gov. Buxton appears to be the worst-paid
pro-consul in Australia. Nominally, he gets
■£1000 more than Ger.vd Smith, but the latter
potentate’s private sec., A.D.C., and other
hangers-on are provided with salaries by the
State, and several other contingencies, totalling
much over <£1000, are treated as extras. Buxton
gets .£5000, but no extras. Lamington draws
.£5000, but Queensland pays his private secretary,
A.D.C., and orderlies, gives him .£500 extra when
touring, £500 for uniforms, and £300 for the rent
of a country residence. Victoria pays £7000 and
no extras to the massive Brassey ; while Hampden
manages to put away £7000 plus salary of private
secretary, and £1580 for general odds and ends.

POLITICAL
POINTS
They call it steal-rails.
Toorak electorate is by no means exclu-
lively composed of the haw-haw crowd its name
would imply.
N.S.W. Labor-party entered Parliament
waving the banner with the strange device, “ One-
man-one-billet.” Where is that banner now?
Puffy believes that his scheme to fetch
Parkes into Victorian politics was frustrated
through Parkes blurting to Sydney newspaper-
men.
At a recent Melb. suburban election meet-
ing when two of the “ gentler ” sex came to fisti-
cuffs on the platform, betting on the event was
out short by trie turning off of the gas.
MORE THAN SUFFICIENT.
MR. O'BEESE : “What first drove yer ter drink,
Battler? ”
BATTLER : “ My wife wuzer temp’ranee orator."
“ These wretched Australian colonies will
be independent in a few years. They are a mill-
rtone round our neck.”—Letter by Disraeli, 1858,
quoted in Lord Malmesbury’s “Recollections.”
Q. Labor-member M‘Donald: “I don’t
■ee the hangman on the estimates ? ” Boss Tozer:
Out of consideration for the feelings of the hon.
member, I had it put among the incidentals. ”
Premier Seddon denies that he is after the
M.L. Agent-Generalship, and says he will not
leave his party “till he can leave it with honor.’’
Well, what would he leave it with if he left it now ?
A.N.A. President Toucher has resigned
his billet in Vic. Income-Tax Office, and will con-
test Ararat electorate against that veteran nuis-
ance, M‘Lellan. Toucher has strong democratic
leanings.
Staughton, M.P. (Vic.), is the squatter
and funny-man of the Opposition corner. He be-
lieves in Freetrade for everything except explo-
sives, one of his constituents being in the gun-
powder business.
“ After all, high wages for the workers too
often mean only opportunity for more drink.”
Teetotal utterance in Melbourne, where electors
are asked to vote for teetotal candidates inde-
pendent of every other issue.
Vic. Trades Hall is mightily offended with
Deakin, and one of its ex-presidents (Bishop) is in
the field against him. This contest will embarrass
many a voter, for, singularly enough, Deakin has
a reputation as favorable as that of Gillies is un-
favorable, though they were both concerned in
the same boomeries.
According to a Brisbane wire, Higgs, of
the Worker, wants the local unemployed to go
straight to the Government and demand “ Bread
or Blood.” But the Anglo-Saxon is a patient,
law-abiding individual, and no one takes him
seriously when he demands Blood. The one
decent chance of Blood that the Queenslanders
had was in the great shearers’ strike. They were
strong enough then, if they had had a good old
revolutionist of the Peruvian filibustering order to
lead them, to sack Brisbane and hang the Govern-
ment on a sour apple-tree, and they didn’t even
hit one policeman a bat on the nose. The law-
abiding habit was too strong for them.
The Age has three labels for candidates
this time—“ Ministerial,” “Freetrade,” “Oppor-
tunists. "
The veteran Longmore (Yic.) has been
politically much threatened of late ; sometimes
threatened men live long.” A paper in his con-
stituency recently chronicled 26 candidates for his
seat.
Very properly the new Q. Land Bill pro-
vides restrictions against the employe of a lessee
being allowed to select on his boss’s run. That is
the flimsiest form of dummyism known to Aus-
tralian land-grabbing science.
N. : “Hypocrisy underlies the whole of
the Vic. Bible-in-Stato-Schools business. This
M’sP. do not believe in
the Bible. Ihey are forced to pretend they do,
and so the whole position is false.”
Bananaland grows painfully sober. Pro-
duction of proof spirits for the last year was down
to 102,190 gals., a decrease of 3286 gals, on pre-
vious year. Wine has declined to 170,733 gals, as
against 238,208 gals, in the year preceding.
In Q. Assembly, in committee on the
Estimates: “ Mishur Shairm’n—hie! I shay
zhere’s too —hie—many pleesh mashtrates, too—
hie—many, many, many—hie ! An’ zhay orterbe
—hie—cut down, cuddown, cuddown!— hic-ic!”
The tick vanguard advances slowly and
irresistibly southward while the rearguard dis-
appears from the north or, at least, the north-west.
Very shortly the North will find it necessary to
quarantine the South—thus combining protection
and vengeance.
Premier Turner says nothing about re-
ducing the revenue tariff on tea, rice, eto.,
which is only a tax on consumption, a tax that
falls on the poor man in his poverty. But the In-
come Tax, whose only incidence is wealth, must
in mercy be reduced.
F: “Ip the Australian Federal Consti-
tution, compromise on ephemeral matters like the
adjustment of finance can be justified, but com-
E remise in the essential principles that will be
anded down to posterity is only comparable to
compromise with honesty.”
How the Q. Fat-Man bursts through the
Factory Act. The Act allows overtime to be
worked 52 days in the year. Fat-Man doses his
hands with all their legalised overtime right off
the reel, and sacks them ; gets fresh hands and
gives them the full dose right off ; sacks these and
gets a third lot, and so on until six times, which
works out the year.
An eccentric medico has been canvassing
Korong (Vic.) electorate on shanks’ pony. He
carries a gaudy rug across his shoulders, likewise
his best clothes in a parcel, and when, in the
course of his pilgrimage, he has to camp far away
from the haunts of men the fiery politician de-
livers spirited addresses to an audience of ghostly
gums. He is a supporter of the Turner Govt.,
but favors the abolition of the duty on axle-boxes.’
The Australian swashbuckler of every
nationality recalls, when he girds up his sword
and coughs, the posse of comic-opera troopers—
“ The gallant troopers, here we come !
The guardians of your house and home 1
But by a most unlucky fate,
We always come too late—too late ! ”
The Soudan heroes too late ! The Fiaschi
Italians too late ! The Maniaehi Greeks—too
late!
Miles': “ I saw the last detachment of Eng-
lish soldiers leave Melb. per ship Corona. The
momentousness of the occasion was
impressed on me. I thought ‘ Eng-
land is a fool.’ Do you think—well,
you don’t that any hanky-panky
interchange of troops, between the
bellicose Chamberlain and the feroci-
ous Turner can bridge the hiatus ? ”
The first section of the Natal
Alien Act—that which excludes im-
migrants who cannot write out and
sign an application in some one of
several languages—is merely a
change on the old fraudulent
literary test to which Austra-
lian policemen were subjected
in the early days. The would-
be bobby learned to write
his application and name
as a drawing-exercise, and
not till the ranks were full
of such impostors did the
authorities drop down.
The Labor Exchange
movement (a late American pill
to remove obstructions to the
millennium) is beginning to make
its appearance in Victoria and S.A.
An Adelaide Labor paper is publishing
articles on the subject; and in Vic. the
PiOle of the cult, a loosely-constructed
but thoughtful book called “The Trials and Tri-
umphs of Labor,” is being discussed in Labor and
Socialistic coteries. The basis of the notion
appears to be a paper currency based on barter, a
sort of legal tender “ warehouseman’s certificate.”
Right to the end of the Convention Han-
sard failed to credit Glynn with being a
member of S.A. Parliament. S.A. members
of Assembly, by the way, call themselves
and are called Ms. P.—not Ms.L.A. N.S.W.
Hansard apparently regards the M.P. affix
as the exclusive right of members of the
English Commons, and so described them, along
with the Tasmanians, as Ms.H.A., giving to
others the ordinary M.L.A. title.

There is fair promise that the new session
of Maoriland Parliament will be mostly ear-chew-
ing and Donnybrook fair. Big John M'Kenzie,
who officially put his feet in it all the time of Sed-
don’s absence, has been asked several dozen times
already to explain the curious Bushy Park job, and
he has merely roared and breathed an indignant
spirituous breath on his questioners. The validity
of the insolvent Ward’s re-election is also to be
questioned, and the rumor that that agile specu-
lator will be again offered the Treasurersnip means
more trouble. But the principal row centres
round Lands Minister M‘Kenzie. That elephan-
tine public lout is the evil genius of the Seddon
Government, and in his own capacious Scotch
manner he has done more damage during Sed-
don’s absence than is ever likely to be repaired.
THE GREEN-EYED MONSTER,
MR. SOFTHEAD : “Here's sixpence, Bobbie. Now,
tell me why your sister wouldn't see me when
I called yesterday?”
BOBBIE : “ 'Cause there was a feller up there cuttin’
her corns.”
It is announced that in determining the
question whether Govt, should go outside N S W
to seek a successor to the late Chief Railway
Commissioner Eddy, “ some consideration will be
given to the feelings of members of Parliament.”
iie who hesitates is lost. Premier Reid is hesi-
tating.
Premier Reid announces that the N.S. W.
Local Government Bill will be introduced before
the present Parliament .expires. He also alleges
that his last Local Govt. Bill would have been
passed “only the Opposition killed it.” The
official Hansard reported that Reid himself re-
fused to go on with the measure because the Labor-
party carried an amendment somewhat restricting
the municipal plural property-vote. But probably
Hansard had been drinking, or else Lyne suborned
it to put the matter in a false light.
M.L. Government’s promises for the new
session include an eight-hour law for factories, the
referendum, a system of State fireunsurance, an
extension of the law for the acquisition of big
estates for closer settlement, encouragement of the
industry, and, “ if finances permit,” an
Old Age Pension scheme. The Seddon Govt, may
ma, ny faults, including the irrepressible John
M'Kenzie and the desire to invite out the Yorks,
but, all the same, this is the straigh test democratic
programme presented by any Australasian Govt,
this year.
The Turner Government’s programme in-
cludes one-man-one-vote, extension of mallee
railways, act to prevent the sale of bad or adul-
terated food and drink, an industrial conciliation
law, a loan conversion scheme, a measure to pro-
tect homes from seizure under mortgage, a per-
petual leasing system, the resumption of large
estates for closer settlement, a coal-mines bill, and
a number of other items. There is nothing large
or sensational in it, but it covers a multitude
of small, dry, tough problems which have been
long neglected.
Consider the hopelessness of the agricul-
tural Sandgroper. Between 1885 and ’95 the popu-
lation of W.A. increased threefold, prices were
high, and the demand for all kinds of produce
enormous. And ;n that period the area under
wheat declined from 29,000 to 21,000 acres ; under
barley, from 6000 to 2000 acres ; under maize, from
1 , acres to 54; oats remained stationary, and
only potatoes and hay showed any increase. The
whole area cropped by the tired Sandgroper in
1894 was actually less than that in 1886, before the
boom started and brought 120,000 new customers to
his door.
Mclnerney, who is opposing Graves for
Delatite (Vic.), announced himself as a Federal
Parliament candidate. “ Don’t you think the
bist min should choke their modesty and come
forward ? ” he asked a Melb. journalist. “Of
course/’ was the reply. “ I think so too,” said
ir C iV T? therefore Oi am goi ng to announce my-
S iu * s an A.N.A., but preserves the ances-
tral brogue. Once there was a rumpus as to his
being Australian-born—but he produced an affi-
davit from his mother stating that he was born
at Eaglehawk. It was reckoned that she ought to
know, and the matter dropped.
So far every Freetrade candidate at the
Victorian elections, bar Murray Smith, has ex-
plained that he is prepared to leave the fiscal
question to the Federal Parliament, or that he
recognises the time is inopportune, or that he
regards the tariff as an evil, but realises that it is
required for revenue purposes and so is prepared
to let it alone, or that he is prepared to sink his
private opinion in deference to the will of the
majority, or that he isn’t a Freetrader at all, or
else he has dropped the fiscal issue out of his speech
altogether. The lonesome Smith seems to be the
one willing fighter of the crowd.

Writes Mr. G. F. Carlton, Secretary
Nannine (W.A.) Hospital Committee
Ed. Bulletin, —Be Nannine Hospital, your correspon-
dent of Xlth Sept, misrepresents matters. The Hospital
Committee were not consulted re the site, that matter
being attended to by the local Board of Health, who had
the site altered from the one chosen by the Govt, archi-
tect. The Committee, who at that date had no locus
standi, fought hard, but. could only compromise with the
local Smell-men so as to shift it a few chains farther from
the graveyard. Neither battery is nearer than J-mile—
one S.E. and the other S.W. from the building—and as
the prevalent winds are from the east, the sound, if
heard at all, will only be a humming noise, not at all
suggestive of the undertaker.— Geo. F. Carlton, Secre-
tary Hospital Committee, Nannine, Sept. 20, 1897.

Dear Bcllktis,—That Tasmanian barrister who burst
forth as a Dickens recitalist at Melb. Athenaeum Hall, a
while was a rather awful instance of misplaced
energy. With no real sense of humor or vocal ability to
interpret the characters he tried to represent, the
neglected stranger had seemingly been drawn into mono-
logue entertainment” by his “wonderful memory.”
remaps he was so unlucky as to hear the old original
Grossmith give a Dickens recital in the long ago. One
good professional monologuist makes a number of bad
amateurs. Given the very ordinary power to photograph
words upon the mind, any man who hears a successful
reading of Dickens from memory is tempted to make an
effort in the same direction. Being tempted, he is bound
to tall, unless the faculty of self-criticism steps forward to
save him—the faculty of realising his failure as an artist,
lhats what keeps millions of would-be actors from taking
to the stage. Bobbie Burns talked of the gift to see our-
selves as others see us as though it were a rare endow-
ment, whereas it is common enough up to a certain point
m human nature. The average person can't be per-
suaded to run the risk of appearing ridiculous on a public
platform.— Collins Street.

F. R. Woodhouse, a Sydney tenor, dilating at Brisbane
on the wonderful advantages of “correct breathing” as
being the key to singing : “ Any man who can speak can
sing, if he once learns how to breathe, and the physical
benefits are enormous. Why, when I first started to
learn how to breathe, I gained five inches in chest-
measureipent in a few months ”
