Snazelle, in his “ Tales of a Traveller,” testifies to the
excellence of the Firm’s comic opera shows. “Their
scenery was always superb, and was recommended by the
names of such artists as Gordon, Brunton, Goatcher, and
bpong The artists imported were always of the best,
and the grand and long training in such a school has
brought many a fine singer to the front. The climate is
so tine, the atmosphere so bright and clean, that the
voices are nearly always in good order.” Which reminds
Ihe Bulletin that plump Courtiee Pounds used to snort
indignantly at the lack of Australian admiration for
chorus-singing, which would get double encores from a
London audience.
“The Captive Soul" cantata, by Miss Woolley, of
Sydney, has been published by Novello. The Musical
News, in recommending the work to the attention of
choral societies, sheds tears of joy over the brilliant effort
received from over the sea. The music is adjudged melo-
dious and stirring, unconventional oftentimes in character,
and not lacking in dramatic intensity.

Not one man in a hundred survives after
having both legs amputated—so say the surgeons
Mr. Alt, railway station-master at Colo, N.S.W.
who was run over by a train and suffered double
amputation, has been discharged from Bowral
Hospital “cured.” By the way, Parisian savant
Hr. Keclus advocates abolition of amputation ex-
cept in most hopeless cases. Whatever the extent
or gravity of the damage, he never, under any
circumstances, amputates the injured limb, but
merely wraps it in antiseptic substances and
swathes it like a mummy, leaving nature to
separate the dead from the living tissues. But
before doing this, he shaves and cleanses with
ether the surface round the damaged part, and
irrigates the injury freely and thoroughly with
hot water at 140 deg. F. He tells the French
Congress of Surgery that by so doing he has a far
lower death-rate than where amputation is prac-
j.se .•. Hot water is antiseptic, is an excellent
dimimsher of bleeding, helps to compensate for
the loss of body heat due to the loss of blood, and
is a powerful aid against traumatic shock. Lastly,
this process preserves far more of a limb, and pro-
vides a more workable and useful extremity than
an amputation stump can ever be.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The first act of Melbourne racing carnival, due
at Caulfield on Saturday, ought to clear away a good deal
of the smoke that is hanging around the big handicaps at
present. The Caulfield Stakes has a number of popular
fancies among the entries (including the Derby horse
Amberite), and the Stakes, although only a £2OO event’
is sometimes a wonderfully straight guide to punters. On
the other hand, it. is sometimes a delusion and a snare.
If Amberite be a really first-class three-year-old, he can
win this w.f.a. race, and, if it is to produce another great
surprise, perhaps Sam, Cook will furnish the surpriser in
Hypnotist.
n The ' v >% r thinks that Aurum can win the
fi? i Gjujneas (it is not a very reckless prediction),
i tlainault is sufficiently big and ripe to capture
the Debutant Stakes under a penalty. But guesswork re
the chances of a crowd of interested two-vear-olds is
dangerous. Adelaide judges count on Staffa taking the
loorak Handicap, whereas the writer would sooner make
a book on that exciting mile jostle, and lay agin’ allofem.
As for Caulfield Cup chances, the market, at
shows nothing clearer than 100 to 7 on
the field and 100 to 3 against outsiders, which amounts to
a warning that the public will do well to viiit a week
before investing their hard-earned beans in any direction,
lrent seems to he the one undeniably genuine candidate
thiisfar. The writer expects that a number of reasonable
fancies will presently form in favor of Trent, Rosella,
Devon (perhaps), Foliage, Mischief, The Hypnotist, Vir-
tue, Luster, Amberite, and one from W. R. Wilson’s little
lot. The first day at Caulfield should certainly give a
turf-adviser ” something solid to go upon, although it
wiU not alter the fact that Caulfield Cup “tips” are
almost invariably knocked into contempt by the result.
Auraria, the heroine of two years ago, is certain
to show up for the weight-for-age races at Fleniington.
Ihe mare, improving rapidly, may be her old self again
in three weeks time. And, by the wav, Burrabari, who
ran third to Auraria and Hova for a Melb. Cup, is said to
be in a fair way to recover his lost “ form.”

The Sydney galloway, Watercolor (Niagara—
Cerise and Blue) surprised Melbournians last Saturday by
making an easy win at the Hunt Club races, Flemington,
of the Melton Handicap Flat-race, starting at 15 to 1, and
beating a field including Zero, Malto, Devoted, and Paul
1 ry. At same meeting the First Sapling Stakes was won
by Vigorous, followed home by Cocos. Both colts arc by
Abercorn, and were bred at Kirkham.

Admirers of Wait-a-Bit are beginning to doubt
whether the son of Malua is a brilliant finisher. In the
Newmarket Handicap, and again in the September Stakes
at Flemington, the other Saturday, the Ballarat candi-
date ran like one that can’t raise an extra sprint when
called on in the last 20yds. Perhaps Anwin has made a
mistake m taking Wait-a-Bit too early to the fore and
trusting to the horse s stamina to keep him there, instead
of holding him back for the “ Malua rush.” But has he
inherited the paternal rush ?

A “contingency” sale and a moral. When
Curzon ran second for the English Derby, Tom Cannon
sold him to Baron Hirsch for a bigf cash price and a con-
tingency of £5OO to boot the first time he won. Curzon
proved a duffer, and, after the baron’s death was sold for
almost a song. Two years later he won a little selling-
race, and was auctioned for 115 guineas. Promptly Can-
non swooped down on the baron’s estate for his £6OO con-
tingency, and got it too.

Much disappointment was felt in Sydney swim-
nung circles when the news was cabled that Jack Hellings
had been beaten in the 100yds. amateur championship,
swum at London. Hellings was clear at 44yds., covered
m 25sec,, and at 76yds. was a yard to the good from
Derbyshire, who led Tyers by a yard and a-half. Hellings
then seems to have mulled the final turn, and Tyers,
making his effort, won by six inches, Hellings and Derby-
shire a dead heat. Time, Imin. 3 3-5sec.

The weather for the first day of the Australasian
Championship Sports meeting, held on Sydney Cricket
Uround last Saturday, did not promise fast times, but
Stanley Rowley managed to rattle off 100yds. in lOsecs.,
equalling VV. T. Macpherson’s N.S.W. amateur record.
Jn the 220yds., on the second day, in perfect weather,
Rowley again equalled Macpherson’s record—of 22 2-5secs.
JN.b.W. won the championship with six first places ; M.L.
second, four ; Victoria three, and Queensland two. The
attendance was small on both days, but it must be con-
fessed that, except to enthusiasts, a sports-meeting is not
lively enough to compete with the many other Sydney
public-holiday recreations.

Mr. Sam Hordern left an order in England for

Towns and Wray, the two Australian scullers
now in England, have been haggling for a match, but as
only a second in the start to be allowed was the bone of
contention, it does not look as if the two mean business.
People who ought to know reckon that Towns is a very
cunning individual who, so far, has rowed his races with
the object of more work ahead. As to the respective
qualifications of Towns and Wray, watersiders who should
know aver that Towns is far and awav the better man ;
some reckon him another Stanburv. * Barry, who was
beaten by Towns, has now challenged Wray for £100.

L, Vic. W. Brassard of £5 a week falls to A. C.
Forbes, the sturdy M.L. plugfjer, whose facial resemblance
to the tfreab and only Bilmartin caused the crowd to hail
him as “ Little Bill.” Unplaced in the mile event, won
by one of Joe Melon's usual outside swoops, Forbes
carried off both the ten and the five miles, leaving all the
Australian cracks standing at the sprint. Bananalander
Walne, hero of last year's cycling, ran nowhere in any
event, and the foreign contingent—Martin, Green, and
Reynolds—didn’t consider the prize of a fiver a week
worth the trouble.
With this trio and a coterie of cycling’ press-
men was Platt-Betts, the English crack, who had
arrived from Ome that morning. Bstts is even more of.
a “ take down ” than the great Zimmerman. About
5ft. 4in. high with a smooth cheek, the holder of all the
world’s records up to 30 miles looks like a youngster of
15 or so, and when he gravely informs you that he has
brought his wife with him you stand aghast. Mrs. P.-B
is a pretty little woman—very delicate-looking and in
strong contrast to her youthful hubby who is at present
hog-fat and obviously out of condition.
It was just a few days before he left England
that the little man reduced the mile to lmin. 37 3-5sec
and, with the speediest man extant on their own tracks’
Australians will at last be able to guage their abilities
with those of the dearoldmotherland.
With Betts came Ainsworth, five-mile amateur
champion of England, who lias given amateurism best in
view of t he big cash prizes of this glorious empire beneath
the Southern Cross.
After being practically tested by the L. V. W.
Biassard charity-meeting on Saturday, the Melb. Friendly
Societies’ cycle-track proved itself almost perfect. The
saving clause is necessary, because the exposed position
of the place makes every breath of wind felt with full
force ; otherwise, the track is a cycling heaven. The
surroundings, however, are as yet very primitive.
Despite Saturday’s wretched weather, the ground held ali
it could, and the reserve could have taken few more
The grand-stand is an open structure 1
About time the cycling authorities exercised
better supervision over the crowd of “battlers” who
rush the arena on the Press-nod. On Saturday, there
were a score of alleged journalists whom nobody knew
and no less than three leery-looking individuals were
lording it as Bulletin representatives, and promising
everybody “pars.”

* * • *
Re amateur cyclists, and bikes as trophies. The
amateur governing bodies of Vic. and Queensland have
forbidden the thing. They, however, have a still more
pernicious system, viz., the open-order one, which enables
amateurs to obtain money in lieu of prizes. For instance
A. wins an open order value £5 5s. He is supposed to
select a trophy to the value, which is duly inspected by
an official and paid for. A. happens to have a friend in
the jewellery line, selects something from his stock, and,
after inspection, the selection is returned to stock and
cash handed over to A.—less a small discount for the
trouble. A sample of every-day shamateurism.
Phil Fargher, one of the successful Kolapore
Cup Bisley team, has a safe seat on the council which
controls rifle-shooting in Vic. The meeting at which he
was elected had a proposal before it that all competitors
should appear at rifle-matches in uniform. This idea of
the shoddy militaires on the council was denounced by
Fargher as a dodge to introduce the military koo-too.
Every man in uniform would have to salute “every
whipper-snapper of an officer” —and salutes would take
up the time meant for shooting. The meeting went fran-
tic with delight at this championship of the mere rifle-
man, and Fargher was hoisted to the council —much to
the disgust of the imported persons upon it.
* * • *
Pd. Bulletin, — Re the Corbett and Fitz-
simmons Vitascope. Of course, the uglier man won.
Beauty means balance, proportion, all-round develop-
ment, and general capacity. Success means specialisa-
tion, development along one line, lopsidedness, and
deformity. Elegants, like Brockwell and Donnan, are
less successful in cricket because they bat partly on gene-
ral principles, not devoting themselves wholly to the
special needs of each ball. Cramped players, like Darling
and the English Brown, get there at the pinch. The
graceful man is often the second-best man never the very
best. So much movement has to be made to secure grace-
fulness, which the direct-action man eeon anises. Same
in polities, same in science, same in philosopl,\. Compare
the “ efficiency of the highly-finished Gladstone with
the crude Abe Lincoln, of the mellifluous Drummond
with the crabbed Darwin, of Edwin Arimld with Herbert
Spencer. Keats said that “ First in beauty should be first
in might.” But biology has developed much since Keats’
time.- Compton.
* * * *
A pathetic bicycle story from Vic. Western dis-
trict. Brown hung up his machine to a log trough, in
front of a pub. on the edge of a wilderness, and went in
to take a drink. While he was drinking, a teamster,
watering his horses, shifted the machine to the verandah.
Presently the cyclist came forth, scattered one startled
glance, found his machine missing, anil a strange rider
slipping over the crown of the hill 100yds. down the road.
The bicyclist suspects every man. Brown sprang upon
the bike on the verandah, and started after the thief at a
Bill Martin gait. The other rider, looking back, saw a
scorcher charging down upon him, and. scenting a chal-
lenge, humped his back and set sail for the setting sun.
But Brown was the better man, and came up with the
other on a down-grade, and, filled with blind liny,
charged the petty larcenist like a hull. Two men and
two bicycles mingled in the dust of the road, and pre-
sently the riders rose up out of the ruins and the dirt,
and began a wild fight, in which both were battered
beyond recognition, and which only terminated when the
teamster arrived, and sat on Brown’s head whilst he con-
vinced him that he had been riding bis own mad ine all
the time. Bikes removed to nearest hospital ; bikers
still in lint.
* * * *
The disqualification of Vaughan, who finished
first in the recent Scott-Morton road-race, should have a
good effect on such long-distance events. Last year’s
“ towing was so glaring that the present race was run
“unpaced”; yet some competitors had friends on tan-
dems awaiting them in certain spots, as well as outside
bikers who “for fun” measured pace with the racer.
Vaughan’s excuse is ignorance. By taking the track he
did he got ahead of the leading bunch as they were going
round by the proper course; not knowing' that anyone
was ahead of them, they fell into the loafing tactics of the
average cycle-racer until they made the final sprint and
the desperate finish up-hill between Jackson and G Car-
penter was a terrible sell for the former when he found
another man had come in 7min. previously.
The Jackson awarded the race is “ Newhaven ”
Jackson, who ran away with his Austral heat last year,
and who has been consistently winning races in’ the
countiy ever since. Jackson’s time was 9hr. 51min. 9sec.,
last year’s with pacing being 8hr. 40min. 15see.

Some recent remarks of The Bulletin on old-
time peds have received strange confirmation by a late
mail. Bacon, England's champion ped., lately tried to
beat Jack White’s record for five miles, and, though
paced by bikes and foot-runners, failed hadlv. He trained
specially for the distance, whereas Whitens record was
accomplished in the course of a much longer race.
Indeed, it is certain that had the referee not told him to
stop as the race was won, White would that day have
altered all records up to 10 miles, which distance he must
have accomplished well within 50min

Dr. Gresswell lately told the members of Mel-
bourne Board of Health that chewing - gum con-
tained 15 per cent, of “utterly indigestible” paraffin
wax, and that it ruined the health of its consumers. The
Board decided to request the City Council to prosecute
all vendors of this seductive poison. If the Council com-
P"« \ vltl J, request, sly chewing-gum shops will pro-
bably be Melbourne’s next commercial development.

Ch. Towers produces perhaps the most ferocious
footballers on earth. Huge, hulking fellows most of
them, with about as much science as a bull-elephant,
they usually win their game by disabling or mortally
wounding their adversaries.

Sailorman Ball, wno made such a ludicrous ex-
hibition with Peter Felix in Sydney, has been giving the
Yanks a similar experience in ’Frisco. He was pitted
at." one Baker, and made just such another show as
with Felix until the referee ordered him out of the ring.
Jack Valentine, erstwhile Sydney amateur boxer,
has turned professional, and is to meet Billy Edwards in
South Africa. Edwards beat Mick Dunn, a well-known
Sydneyite, a clever boxer, but one who, when in Australia,
always had sense enough to know when he had sufficient
of a good thing.
* * * *
Vic. rowing will receive fresh stimulus from the
recent Yarra improvements, whereby the river has been
transformed from an evil-smelling, meanderim' creek
materially narrowed by the frequent carcases of dead
animals, to a straight, broad expanse of water capable of
accommodating five or six competing crews " Where
Brander’s ferry was, the stream now is fully double its old
width, and the sharp old “ Baths corner ” has been sup-
planted by a gradual sweeping curve. There will still be
two slight turns in a mile course, but three-quarters of
the distance is a straight line whereon oarsmen may
spit chips under the very eye of Gov. Brassey on his
verandah.

Brisbane’s newest social institution is the
Queensland Sporting and Athletic Club, organised bv
Theodore Wood, who has been nearly all things in his
time—from Imperial bureaucrat to editor of reform
t 3 \ lordly squatter to insurance-drummer.
An, what a time there was at Waitemata plantation
Maryborough, in the good old days when Theodore W
was a bloated sugar-planter ! Already most of Brisbane’s
most influential flesh-and-blood human beings (The Bul-
letin isn’t talking of saints) have signed Wood’s list and
Dowridge s athletic hall has been leased. “ The Black
Diamond ” will be general manager.

The champion glutton for punishment among
Austraban pugs is Jim (“ Ironbark ”) Burge, who invari-
ably won his battles by allowing the other man to punch
bis cast-iron head until his hands were useles- Once
being trained for a fight in Melb. by Peter Newton, Jim
was attacked by a violent headache, and, alter several
curative efforts, he suggested that Newton should “ deal
it out ” to him and make his nose bleed. This the trainer
conscientiously did until a soreness of the knuckles
warned him to desist. In desperation he lifted on hieh
an Indian club of several pounds weight, while Bur"-e
held his nose in position. Just as the weapon was about
to fall, the iron-man changed his mind. “ No Peter ” he
cried, “ ye’d better not. The club ain’t mine.”

Found in S.W. Queensland that the most
effective of all rabbit-poisons is pollard mixed with
livers of rabbits which have died of chicken-
cholera. Also, the rabbits desert the burrows
wherein bunnies dead of chicken-cholera have
been placed.

Somk cycles are cominsr down in price—that is, the
kind in opposition to RED BIRDS. The refined con-
struction of Red Birds is not found in other cycles,
notably their Adjustin'? Alignment Bottom Bracket
and their little Oil Bath Bearings, which make the
Red Bird the fastest wheel on earth.
\VM. MARTIN’S celebrated mile in lmin. 19 3-sseo.
is a sample of their easy winning qualities.
One of the great features of the new Red Bird is
its bottom bracket. It is a triumph of modern skill
in cycle-building, and is a feature as far ahead of
bottom brackets in other cycles as the pneumatic tyre
is ahead of the solid. The two cups which form the
bearings are turned from the finest grade of steel pos-
sible to manufacture. They contain ten 5-16 balls.
Each cup is threaded to a steel sleeve or barrel which
adjusts inside the bottom bracket proper, and is held
in place by two bolts. With the bearings it is only
necessary to oil same about twice a year, as they are
thoroughly dust-proof and oil-retaining, the balls
running in a continuous oil bath. With OTHER
CYCLES it is almost impossible to get a perfectly
true chain alignment, whereas the chain and the
bearings, with the aid of this most important im-
provement, can be adjusted to 1-Iouoth part of an
inch.

He was doing the purse-trick in a second-class
railway-carriage, i.e., putting three sovs. in a purse and
selling the lot for AT. Somebody laid a well-advertised
detective on to catch him. The D. said he thought he
could as he was a strange spieler and would not know
him. He sauntered into the carriage just in time to see a
farmer taken down. The D. handed the artist a sovereign
and took the purse ; opening same he found three sovs.
“Gentlemen, I can assure you the game is quite fair;
good day.” Then the D. whistled himself into a first-
class carriage.

Several Vic. professional cyclists are rejoining
the amateur body. The general reason given for apply-

“Father” Abrahams, the Melb. Rabbi, has
been celebrating the Jewish New Year by a series of
special sermons, the first being termed ‘The Moral
Ledger. 1 The intelligent printer on one of the
dailies turned this into the Moral ‘ Lodger, 1 but
little harm was done. The public knows that the
age of miracles is past.
That Melb. dame who appeared as respond-
ent twice in the Divorce Court in two years took
good care that the public should miss none of the
factsof thecase.andaccordinglyadvertisedboth her
marriages in the dailies on the date of hearing of
the last petition. She poetically instructed the
second husband, who had been the co-respondent
in the prior case, that “she had served him as she
served the first ; he could go to and do his
worst.” In these days of dissimulation, a littl e
candor like this is refreshing.

Q. Govt, is shutting down on comprehen
sive endowments to hospitals. The rate has been
£2 for every „£1 subscribed anyhow. In future,
the same duplication will only be paid to “ bona
fide contributions given by individuals 11 ; no sums
raised by balls, entertainments, automatic medical
batteries, etc., will be subsidised. Boss Tozer
gives this out as something new. Why, up at
Thursday Island, every pearling -and
most of him is black, or brown, or yellow—is com-
pelled by law to pay Is. a month —the Customs
department take it from the wages due, getting
first bite, so to speak—to the local hospital, and
Govt, some time ago stopped its subsidy to that
levy.
The Fijian is dying out. The epidemics
of civilisation-cwm-Christianity are the cause of his
extinction. So at least says S.M. Herald, who
with the next breath observes that, from the mis-
sionary point of view, Fiji is the greatest triumph
of the century ! Cadmean sort of triumph ! Eh ?
Some eighteen hundred years ago a carpenter
with the gift of prophecy explained in vigorous
speech how the missionary would act; also how
said missionary’s work would pan out —sea and
land compassed to make a proselyte and the pro-
selyte once made rendered twofold more the child
of hell than the missionary. Yes, than the mis-
sionary ! Irreverent carpenter !

| What Queensland M.P. dares to move a
, clause in the Land Bill to debar M’sP. from acting
I as agents for persons before the Land Court ?
Was that a laugh ’ Is the game, then, too profit-
able to be readily relinquished ? On this, and a
nearly analogous matter, a Bulletin correspondent
writes:—
Should not M’s.P. also he prohibited from acting as
counsel in law-courts? Perhaps the cases are not even
distinctly alike. But, look you, what a glorious purifica-
tion of Parliament it might lead to ! The democracy might
say “No, we will not be so unjust as to disable lawyers
from taking seats in Parliament,’’ but the repute of our
Parliamentary representatives must be so far above
suspicion that we enact that no M.P. shall be allowed to
act as advocate in any court called upon to administer the
laws which Parliament makes! How many six-and-
eightpenny men, or how many variously guinea-ed barris-
ters would try for Parliamentary honors under such a
system ? A beautiful way to expurgate Parliaments !

A TASMANIAN MIRACLE.
PARTIAL PARALYSIS AND WHOLE
PROSTRATION ENTIRELY CURED.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS CREATE A
SENSATION IN HOBART.
(From the Hobart Clipper.)
Henry Dixon Clarke, a native of Hobart, Tas-
mania, tells a story of a cure effected by the use
of the now famous Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, which in its marvellous details proves
the adage that truth is stranger than fiction.
Twelve years ago he fell from the scaffolding of
a building in course of erection in Collins-street,
Melbourne, and was taken to the hospital in-
sensible. He recovered and was sent home. The
next morning he was too stiff to go to work, and
resolved on resting awhile. The next day he was
worse, and in addition to feeling feverish and
unwell, he was seized with twitching pains in the
back whenoverTie attempted to straighten himself.
These increased in virulence, and were accom-
panied with intense pains in the head. He lost
the use of his left arm, and partially that of his
left leg, as well as feeling a numbness all down the
side, extending from the shoulder to the region of
the abdomen. Electricity was tried amongst
other things, but without relief. So much was ho
insensible to outward agencies that he could bear
the application of the battery to any degree with-
out feeling it. To use his own expression, “ You
could jab a needle into my arm and I could not
feel it.” He was given up as a hopeless paralytic
by the medical fraternity of Melbourne after he
had spent his last shilling in trying to get better.
He then resolved on coming over to Tasmania at
the request of his relatives in the faint hope that
the climate might perhaps give him relief. But
years dragged on, and his sufferings were without
end. “ I thought,” said he, “ frequently of
COMMITTING SUICIDE
rather than continue to be a burden upon my
friends,, for my miserable plight was exceedingly
depressing, as well as painful. I found relief in a
sense of the word by sitting on the verandah in
the sun ; but when I saw my friends go past to
work, it only made me the more miserable, for I
was always a very active man. There are not a
few who remember the pale and wretched object I
was, seated in the verandah of my mother’s house
in Macquarie-street, and many can testify to the
marvellous cure which took pl'ace. I got hold of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and took
four boxes of them. One morning I found I could
lift my arm quite easily. It all came on in the
night, and then I began to feel better right off.
Tn about a week from when the change set in the
pains had left me altogether, and I cannot describe
to you the relief I felt both in body and mind. A
fortnight later, and I could walk about and eat
well. I had another sensation then, which was
new to me —the feeling of hope. Only those who
have passed an ordeal like mine can tell what that
actually means.”
“ What doctors did you have to attend you ? ”
“ I would tell you, but perhaps they wouldn’t
care to ha.ve their names mentioned in a case like
mine, which they turned up as hopeless. But I
spent in all .£l7O, and being then a beggar, came
home to die. Now there is no fear of my being
reduced to beggary again, and as for dying, I’m
not going to throw a seven yet for a long while, I
can assure you. lam going to work next week
round at the West Coast —a job a friend got for
me—so you see what Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People have done for me. I say—and I say
it advisedly—and all who know me say so too, that
it is the most marvellous thing of the kind that is
going. You are welcome to make what use you
like of this. I can tell you lam heartily thankful
for my recovery—more than I can describe to you.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People havo
cured paralysis, locomotor ataxy, rheumatism, and
sciatica ; also all diseases arising from impoverish-
ment of the blood, scrofula, rickets, chronic ery-
sipelas, consumption of the bowels and lungs,
anaemia, pale and sallow complexion, general mus-
cular weakness, loss of appetite, palpitations,
pains in the back, nervous headache, early decay,
all forms of female weakness, and hysteria. These
pills are a tonic, not a purgative. They are genuine
only with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People, and are sold bv chemists and
storekeepers generally, or the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine'Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, will forward, on
receipt of stamps or post order, one box for 2s. Ltd.

Lake George South Mines
(To be registered as a No-Liability Company under the
Companies Act 1890.)
CAPITAL: £lOO,OOO, in 100,000 Shares of One Pound each,
of which 50,000 Shares are now offered to the publio at
4s. per Share ; 30,000 Shares, paid up to 45., are retained
by the vendors, and also 20,000 Shares fully paid-up.
£5OOO will be placed to the credit of the Company, free
and clear of all expenses whatsoever.
Provisional Directors : Reginald A. F. Murray, F. 0.5.,
London, late Government Geologist, and President Vic-
torian Chamber of Mines; Alex. Oliver, Ti e Grange,
Domain-road, South Yarra ; Wm. Bell Jackson, Mer-
chant, The Rialto, Collins-street, Melbourne ; G. Geo.
Crespin, J.P., Merchant, Queerest., Melbourne. Legai.
Manager; (To be appointed by the Board of Directors
when constituted.) Bankers : The Union Bank of Aus-
tralia Limited, Melbourne. Solicitors ; Fink, Best and
Hall, The Rialto, Collins-street, Melbourne. Brokers :
A. Oliver, 100 Queen-street, Melbourne; Clark and
Robinson, Adelaide.
This company is formed for the purpose of ac-
quiring those sections contained in mineral lease
applications 85 and 86, comprising an area of 120
acres or thereabouts, situate Captain’s Flat, New
South Wales, and to the south of the Lake George
Mines, as shown on plan. These sections comprise
the southern* extension of the high metalliferous
deposit or lode known as the “ Champion,” now
being worked by the Lake George Mines Limited,
the outcrop of which can be traced through this
property from end to end.
The accompanying plan of locality indicates
approximately, pending official survey, the trend
of the lode, with its northern and southern exten-
sions, as per reports attached.
The ore bodies, as exposed and available for
stoping in the parent company’s ground, are for
magnitude unequalled in any part of Australia
(vide the Argus special report on Lake George
Mines Limited).
From the accompanying reports it will be seen
that the “Champion” lode traverses this com-
pany’s ground from end to end, and this same
lode has been proved in the Lake George Mines
Limited for a depth of 500 ft., for a width of 42ft.
—its metallic contents being gold, silver, and cop-
per in highly payable quantities.
The district is particularly favored in other
respects. There is plenty of available timber,
water, and the necessary materials for fluxing—
matters of grave economic importance in mining.
Attention is drawn to the special reports of ex-
perts on this property, and to the proximity of
these sections to the Lake George Company’s
ground; and possessing, as it does, the “Cham-
pion ” lode, its value cannot be estimated. The
£1 shares in the parent company are now worth
725. per share, representing a capital value of over
half a million sterling.
REPORTS.
James S. Hawkes, Esq., constructional engineer
to the Lake George Mines Limited, reports, under
date 22nd September, 1897, as follows :
“ I have examined the properties held by you
south of the Lake George Mines Limited, and beg
to report that they are, in my opinion, on the
direct line of the ‘Champion lode’ of the Lake
George Mines Limited, and that the said lode tra-
verses the property from end to end. There is a
massive and well-defined outcrop, in some places
over 20ft. wide, and there can be no reasonable
doubt that this will develop into one of the most
valuable properties in the district. The foreground
furnishes convenient sites for the machinery and
working plant, and though I have not had the op-
portunity of taking levels I am sure that, from the
general lay of the country, water can easily be
brought on to the ground from Ballinafad Creek,
a short distance away.—Yours faithfully, James
S. Hawkes, C.E., Constructional Engineer Lake
George Mines Limited.”
The Argus special correspondent, reporting on
the Lake George Mines Limited (vide issue 20th
July, 1897), writes ;
“ At the Lake George Mines the pyritic lode
(the ‘ Champion ’) has been proved for a depth of
500 ft. and for a width of 42ft. Three parallel
lodes traverse the ground with a north ana south
course. As the true foundation of a rnine’B pro-
sperity is the extent and value of the ore to be
dealt with, these sections must be looked upon as
of the utmost importance. I never saw so much
ore opened up in a new mine before, and 300 men
could be put on to stope to-morrow. The venture
is certain to be a success, its probabilities are very
great, and its possibilities stupendous. A close
inspection was made of the northern and central
sections of the ‘ Champion ’ lode.”
The lode referred to by the Argus correspon-
dent is identical with that traversing the sections
which this company now proposes to acquire.
Captain John Batty, late manager of the Koh-i-
-nor Gold, Copper, and Silver Company (now Lake
George Mines Limited), reports as follows :
“Lake George Mines, 23rd September, 1897.
“At your request, I have visited and inspected
the south blocks, and find them to be in a very
pood position and in mineralised country. There
is a large outcrop traversing north and south
throughout these blocks; and, from all appear-
ances, I can confidently recommend this property
as a very valuable one, if properly prospected.
(Signed) John Batty.”
Hugh M'Neill, one of the original prospectors of
the district, and late manager of One Better Com-
pany, Swamp Oaks, reports as follows :
“ Captain’s Flat, 22nd September, 1897.
“I beg to furnish you with a report of the pro-
perty held by you at Captain’s Flat. The lode is
outcropping from one end of the property to the
other, bearing almost due north and south, and I
have no hesitation in saying that it is the con-
tinuation of the ‘ Champion ’ lode of the Lake
George Mines Limited, and I am also of opinion
that the ‘ Copper Creek ’ lode junctions with the
main lode on your property. In this latter
opinion, I am supported by most of the practical
men in the district. I consider the property a
very valuable one, and this is more than confirmed
by the fact that large numbers of blocks are being
taken up south of your property, and on the same
line of lode. —Yours faithfully, Hugh M ‘Neill,
late Mine Manager, One Better Company, N.S.W.”
Shares will be allotted according to priority of
application, and early application is necessary, as
a large number of shares are already applied for.
Plans, prospectuses, and all information may be
obtained from the brokers to the company.
ALEX. OLIVER, Queen-street, Melbourne.
CLARK and ROBINSON, Adelaide.

Remarkable how station - properties in
Western N.S.W. are being federated, as the result
of rabbits, bad seasons, bank smashes, mortgages
secured to purchase freeholds, and other things.
Almost a majority of stations consist each of two
or more holdings ; and, where stations are still
nominally separate, they are often worked under
one super., from the same city office. The latest
amalgamation is that of Mundi Mundi and Poola-
macea, both of some historical interest.
In a Richmond (Vic.) court, a few days
back, a witness acknowledged that he “ didn’t
believe there was a God.” Thereupon that learned
theological authority, the clerk of the court,
stopped short with the microbe-infested volume
in his hand and told him that he “ was like the
brute beast.” This sort of phrase is a favourite
one with the mild and gentle pietist, but when
one comes to think of it, does anyone really know
for certain what the brute beast believes or disbe-
lieves ? Judging from history, a great many sin-
cere believers have been beasts. Also, if a man
is to be judged by the company he keeps, is it
better to be an unbeliever like the harmless
necessary cow, or,a believer like the Borgia fa?nily
and the lately-hanged Sheehan? That Richmond
witness missed a beautiful point. “You are an
unbeliever like the brute beast,” said the official
snuffier. Obvious answer: ‘ ‘ And you are a be-
liever like the brute Butler.”
This knocks even the visiting-card of Frank
Farnell, M.P. of N.S.W., into a cocked hat. From
the Business Directory of the Port Douglas and
Mossman Gazette (Queensland) we learn that Mr.
Connolly, P.M., is also—
Water Poliee Magistrate, Sub-Collector of Customs,
Land Commissioner, Land Agent, Gold Warden, Mining
Registrar, Mineral Lands Commissioner, District Court
Registrar, Petty Debts Registrar, High Bailiff, Deputy
Marshal, Deputy Sheriff, Electoral Registrar, Clerk of
Petty Sessions, Harbor Master, Shipping Master, Shipping
Inspector, Inspector of Fisheries, Inspector of Stamps,
Inspector of Distilleries, Inspector S.S. Islanders, Savings
Banks Officer 5.5.1., Registrar Births, Deaths, and Mar-
riages, District Receiver in Insolvency, Agent Curator In-
testacy, Agent Curator Insanity, immigration Agent,
Paymaster to Treasury, Receiver of Wrecks.
Also he figures in the paper as President of the
District Hospital and as a member of the com-
mittee of the School of Arts. If he were only a
doctor and an undertaker he would be a township
in himself.

in his “Sphinx of Eaglehawk,” this is the sort of
evidence he allows to be given for the prosecution
on a murder-trial—the prisoners being defended
by the best criminal lawyer in Australia. One
of the Crown witnesses, examined by the Crown-
Prosecutor, says :
The night before, the deceased had been playingeuchre
with the three prisoners. . . . He didn't know that
two of the prisoners had been tried for murder before,
and the other had served a long sentence for robbery
with violence. If he had it mightn’t have made much
difference. People didn’t mind these things on a
diggings. . . . They wanted him to stop . . . but
he wouldn’t. Something he saw the night before fright-
ened him, I expect.
After a start like that, the author, in his descrip-
tion of the remainder of the trial, should not have
relapsed into mere fiction. What really happened
was that the prisoners were put in the box by the
Crown-Prosecutor, and severely cross-examined.
The Judge having summed-up, His Honor’s de-
liverance was then subjected to a fearless and
scathing criticism by counsel for the defence in his
address to the jury, who thereupon, without leav-
ing the box, by a majority of 9 to 3, found a ver-
dict of guilty against the before-mentioned witness
for the prosecution. The prisoners having been
discharged with a caution, the guilty man was, in
accordance with law, at once deli vered into their
hands and summarily executed.
A Boon to Sufferers.— A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*

picture of the ballet-girl on The Bulletin back
cover. Suddenly she exclaimed “Oh, mother,
how disgusting!” and then, after a thoughtful
pause, “but how stylish!” The ruling spirit
showing early.

in the quality of the commercial pepsine (some of which, under the most favorable circum-

“The Last of

Phi* Post. la. 3(1.
A Zealot in Labor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The creek was hacked and mangled out of all
semblance to a sylvan rivulet.
The ruin effected looked like the work of many
men. The muddy, yellow stream had been
diyerted from its course several times within half-
a-mile, and all along the banks were torn down,
great cuttings made, piles of gravel heaped up,
dams built, and races dug. But the ravisher was
there— a lone man gouging his way into a bank at
the head of the flat where it met the hill, looking
a mere midge amongst the destruction he had
wrought with his two good hands.
“ Humpy ” Bannon was puny and weazened and
old ; he had a hump between his shoulders, and
no intelligence to speak of, but he had the spirit
of a little red ant, magnified to suit his size. He
loved labor, and he had chosen Grim Creek as his
vineyard. From a miner’s point of view, Bannon
was the discoverer of Grim Creek.. He it was who
prospected it and found gold in it, and he
was exceedingly proud of his field, although it
was a starvation hole at the best, and rewarded
him for his tremendous labors —digging, shovel-
ling, puddling, cradling, wading in water, and
grubbing in sludge—with a few wretched weights
where ounces would have been poor pay. But
Humpy never thought of leaving. Wet days and
fine found him smeared with clays of many colors,
struggling in a wet shaft or delving at the banks,
full of enthusiasm, without resource, without
horse-sense, but all grit.
“ Leave the creek ? ” he would say in answer to
the advice of casual visitors. “ Why, where’d I
go ter?”
“ Well, there’s some good gold gettin’ at Black
Cap, an’ I hear about somethin’ worth prospectin’
ten miles out by Double-TJ-Hell.”
“Bah! Stringer! You don’t catch me leavin’
the Creek for a hole like Black Cap. Why,
some’r them city minin’ men’d snap it up soon’s
my back was turned.”
It was a favorite superstition of Old Bannon’s
that some one from the city was always prowling
about in the hope of securing his latest pitch on
the Creek.
“ Jumpt/m show, Humpy ! Why, there is not
gold enough in a mile of it to yield a peanut.”
Bannon couldn’t restrain his temper when the
creek —his creek —was disparaged, and at this
point always became incoherent between extrava-
gant predictions as to the fabulous richness of the
wash he was going to cut presently and insulting
reflections upon the intelligence of the maligner,
and he would fall to working again more fiercely
than ever, jigging his old head the while, and
chummering bitterly.
How he did graft! Little, and skinny, and
aged, and ill-fed as he was, he cheerfully faced
mountains of labor, and wore them down by sheer
pertinacity—shifted them by faith and works.
What wonders of toil can one determined man
perform in a year ! To know you must see the
man struggling amongst the evidences of it, with
the works of his hands piled up about him, and
the man’s sole master must be a belief, sane or
otherwise.
Humpy’s faith in Grim Creek was transcendent;
that the creek gave him no justification mattered
not a scrap. He lived in a little bark hut, com-
fortless as a mia-mia, on nothing in particular;
he dressed at work in a worn shirt, patched ex-
travagantly, and deplorable trousers and boots, and
he wound lengths of sugee about his shins. His
hat, a battered boxer, a gift from a sympathetic
selector, had a big hole fore and aft—driven to
extremes, he had once run a handle through it,
and used it for a ladle when cradling—and the
whole costume was cemented and frescoed with
the grit and clay of the unspeakable creek.
The old man never had a mate, he never wanted
one. He designed all sorts of hare-brained, un-
workable contrivances in the shape of dumb-
waiters and cranks, and feed-pipes, and sluices, to
overcome the difficulties that hamper a lone hand,
but through disappointments and dangers and
endless tribulation he struggled on, and turned up
regularly every Saturday afternoon at the. log
store on the Piper-road with his pathetic little
packet of gold and his long, familiar story of the
good day that was coming for Grim Creek and
the surrounding district when he finally “ got on
to it.” . , ~
A few of the farmers and selectors m the dis-
trict, thinking that possibly, by reason of an
unlooked-for contingency, Humpy might some
day “ get on to it ” and boom the place a bit,
helped with gifts of food and old clothes that to
him were as good as new, One or two, from pure,
wooden-headed good nature, visited him at times,
especially on Sundays, and sat with him in the
sun or in his smoky hut, and let him talk to. them
by the hour about his creek. Next to grafting in
the creek like a tiger, nothing pleased Humpy
better than to prattle about his work, and invent,
and lie, and rhapsodise to a sympathetic audience.
Tom Hughes was the old digger’s best friend.
He had secured a selection in the locality of Grim
Creek within the last six months, built a hut upon
it, and settled down to take life as easily as a
selector can who observes the covenants. Hughes
was a hatter—a big, hairy man, physically slow,
mentally alert, with a golden faculty of extracting
amusement out of anything and everything, from
the capers of his waddling terrior pup or the soli-
citude of a motherly hen to the foibles of his
fellows. Hughes enjoyed Humpy Bannon enor-
mously. He cultivated him. . He would sit and
study him by the hour, ponderous and apparently
as grave as a fat frog between meals, but with a
soul full of laughter. “Humpy ” reminded him
of an ant that he had once seen attempt to shift
Mount Macedon. The ant thought the mount
obstructed its view, or felt that it had a call; any-
how, Tom kept track of the insect fora week, and
neglected his duties to watch progress, and when
he left the ant was still going strongly. Now,
here was this other midge ripping up the face of
nature and tearing at the bowels of the earth
after something he didn’t really want and wouldn’t
know how to appreciate. Wifeless, childless,
without a taste superior to mutton and bread or
an aspiration above the puddling-tub, and with
very few years of life before him, he worked from
daylight to dusk, moving mountains, and grew
radiant describing the treasure he must win some
day. Yet ten shillings a week would have satisfied
his needs, twelve would have embarrassed him
with riches.
Walking along the creek one day, Hughes came
upon the old man clambering out of a prospecting-
hole on a rise. He was dripping wet, and coated
with mud ; day was in his hair and his ears, and
the dirty water ran from him as he stood. _ Humpy
was too busy for conversation ; he seized the
windlass-handle, and began hauling with terrific
energy. There were two buckets on the rope
one a kerosene-tin, the other an ordinary water-
bucket. Humpy landed and emptied these, and
then, lowering the rope into the shaft again,
began to fish about. Presently he hooked another
bucket, and brought it to the surface. After fish-
ing once more he landed a nail-keg. Then he
proceeded to let himself down again, sliding on
the rope.
“What’s the little game, old man?” asked
Hughes as the dripping head disappeared.
“After a bit o’ wash here. Tremenjis rich, 1
think,” answered Bannon up the shaft.
“But it’s too wet; you'll never be able to
bottom, workin’ her alone.”
“ But I will, though ! ” .
Further comment was deferred by the pit-pit of
the old man’s pick in the wet hole. Tom Hughes
hooked the nail-keg, and put in an hour or so at
the windlass, and was rewarded later with
Humpy’s confidence. As usual, the little, man
was on the eve of a discovery that was going to
revolutionise the district, and bring a big town
humming about their ears on Grim Creek in less
than no time. Hughes was a better miner than
old Bannon, and knew the latter was hustling
after a vain thing, but he offered no advice, un-
derstanding that it would be wasted, and remem-
bering that it was Humpy’s policy to go and find
out for himself at whatever cost of sweat and
patience.
Humpy did bottom that hole, and scraped up a
prospect that promised about ten weights to the
load to a sanguine man, but the water, was up
within three feet of the surface next morning,, and
eight hours’ vigorous baling had no appreciable
effect. The claim could not be worked without a
diving-suit and apparatus.
So Humpy went apart and thought. He wasted
little time in speculation, and presently took a
bee-line from his shaft to the foot of the rise, 250
yards off, and commenced an open cutting. His
idea was to carry this narrow cutting into the hill
on a level as long as he could throw the dirt, and
then, when the sides became too high, to tunnel to
the shaft, and so drain the ground he wished to
work. This represented about a year’s labor to an
average man working decent hours and in modera-
tion. It was an utterly fatuous and foolhardy un-
dertaking ; as far as it was possible to judge, the
ground would not pay for the working, let alone
compensate for this gigantic “dead horse ” ; but
Bannon did not calculate—he worked.
On the occasion of Hughes’s next visit, he found
Humpy pegging away industriously, in his cutting.
He had covered a good distance in the shallow
ground.
“ Well, old party, what’re yeh coursin now ?
asked Tom.
Humpy explained between blows.
“ (?efi-ruBalem but you do lick ’ell an all! ”
Tom proceeded to explain the difficulties of the
job and the ridiculousness of it, but the digger’s
under-hand pick was going busily all the time,
and at last Hughes seated himself upon a log, and
overlooked the toiler in silent enjoyment of his
wonderful courage, his dunder-headedness, and
the comical little ape-like figure and quaint tricks
and turns of the man.
Humpy persisted, and as the weeks wore by his
cutting extended and deepened, and at length he
was forced to take on another contract. It was
necessary to get the water away. He felled trees,
and split palings, and laid down a bo;: drain all
along the cutting—a wonderful drain, represent-
ing much time and trouble. He timbered his job
where timber was needed, and continued as before
eating his way into the hill, and as he progressed
his pride in his work increased. The cutting was
trim and true ; Humpy bestowed the most loving
care upon it, and Tom Hughes brought all the
strangers he came across to inspect and ad-
mire it as the one spectacle of Grim Creek, and to
gaze upon Humpy and wonder over him. And
whilst Tom stood aloft eulogising the digger with
something of the air of a showman, and amiably
explaining his humors and eccentricities for the
pleasure of these strangers, Humpy hammered
away eagerly on the job below.
“He ain’t got common sense about minin’,”
Hughes would say; “ have you, old man ? ”
Humpy, with his pick driven to the eye in the
wall before him, would turn up his puckered,
tanned, hairy face with the aspect of a venerable
mandril, and damn his friend—hide, bones, and
soul —as the selector went on :
“ But in a tunnel or a drive he’d work any man
I ever knew stone-blind inside a week. Wouldn’t
yeh, Humpy?”
More profanity from below.
“See, he’s built for it. Them shoulders was
built fer pokin’ round in low black drives an’
muddy tunnels, but he’s wasted fer want’ve horse
sense. He’s a blessed steam-engine whirling away
like blazes, but doin’ nothin’ that matters a hang.
Look at him ! He’s the only man in Australia that
likes work —he’d rather be workin’ than drinkin’ —
an’ he’s only happy when he’s clayed up to the
ears and sweatin’ quarts.”
Sometimes a visitor dropped Humpy a half-
crown or a shilling, and often a settler or farmer
gave him help ; but for all that tie was compelled
to leave his cutting now and again and go fossick-
ing in the creek for a pennyweight or two, and
then he was given over to a great discontent.
Whilst he was working in the cutting it preserved
its spick-and-span appearance ; when he was away
dead leaves accumulated in it, and Monaghan’s
sheep sometimes destroyed the symmetry of its
edges, and that affected Humpy as dirt and litter
about a room irritate a good housewife.
But as time passed the great work progressed,
and at length the tunnel had been opened out, and
was being driven towards the shaft. It was the
most elegant of tunnels, with a beautiful entrance,
and carefully squared throughout, and it went in
and in until at length, when Humpy was within a
week of his goal, there came jangling up the
creek one day a mounted policeman. The officer
of the law examined Bannon’s hut carefully, and
tossed things about and turned the place upside-
down with the placid insolence power confers
upon most men; and then he rode to Humpy’s
cutting, called the little man into the light of day,
handcuffed him, and led him off.
The charge was sheep-stealing. There was no
doubt of Bannon’s guilt: one skin, with the brand
on it as bold as brass, was found doing service as
a rug on his bunk, another, quite fresh, was
tacked up in his shed ; and the best part of a fine
lamb was rescued from his pickling-tub, and pro-
duced in court. The spirit of the early squatter
still survives in the particular and express abomin*


“ From the moment a young man lands in
Maoriland, his features begin to alter, and his
nose especially tends to become broad and aqui-
line.”—Dr. Louis Robinson, in Blackwood’s
Magazine.

him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-

a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Ngvvman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Tom Hughes tried hard to preserve Humpy’s
cutting from destruction, and kept a careful eye
on his hut, but, walking down the creek one day,
twenty months later, he came upon the little hid
digger standing surveying the ruins of his gr -at
work. The sides of the cutting had tumbled in,
the tunnel was down, and the drained ground was
worked out. Humpy was smaller-looking and
more shrunken, and ten years seemed to have been
added to his age.; he was bent nearly double,
and vvas bleached a deadly dough-color ; his limbs
trembled as he stood, and he snivelled miserably,
like a,boy. No greeting passed between the two
men.'
“’T" as three fellers from Melbourne done it,”
said Hughes, indicating the cuttings.
“Damn ’em ! ” snapped Bannon.
“ 1 tried hard to stop ’em,” continued the
selector. “ I explained it was your job ; 1 argued,
an’ pleaded, an' preached, but ’twasn’t no good.”
, Tom had also fought the intruders, singly and
in a bunch, and had been severely manhandled for
his kindness ard consideration, but he did not ex-
plain this.
“ Hows’mever, ’twasn’t worth a cuss,” he added
eagerly. “They skursly knocked out tucker, an’
only hung on jest from pure villainy.”
This was a lie : the young men had done fairly
well out of Humpy’s claim, and had taken to town
with them when they left sufficient gold to run a
month-long bender of the most virulent and
dazzling .description.
“Damn ’em ! ” said Humpy again.
“ Better track up to yer hut, old man,” Hughes
said. “You'll find it in order. You can spell-oh
till you pick up a bit, an’ then you can get down
to graft. You’ll be all right, you know.”
“ Yes, yes,” grasped Bannon with a feeble re-
turn of his old fire, “there’s somethin’ above the
fork I’m goin’ after. I’ll have to turn the creek.
B’lieve there’s some ten-ounce stuff there.”
Hughes had to lead him to his hut, and attend
to him for a few days, but presently Humpy was
out and about again, with pick and shovel, pot-
tering weakly here and there. Once Tom found
him struggling to clean out the old cutting. By-
and-bye he started making great raids upon the
hills, digging aimless holes, and throwing up
heaps of dirt anywhere. Two or three times he
was discovered lying helplessly by his work. At
length the same policeman came trotting up the
gully again, and once more Humpy Bannon was
led away. This time he did not come back. He
finished his days performing extraordinary feats
of labor with a little wooden shovel at Yarra
Bend, destroyed in mind and body by 18 months
of comparative peace and rest and comfort in Her
Majesty’s gaol.
EDWARD DYSON.
The Price of Life.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is nothing in life that is worth a wish but is
bought with the price of blood,
Be it cru-hf d from the heart or distilled from the
mind, in the marble courts or the mud.
There is never a slave who can bind his so il to
the social creed and its ban
Who is worth, in the time of a strong resolve, the
curse or the claim of a man.
For we know that the world is a bitter world, and
we live in the way we know,
And gather the strength of the strongest so d for
the weak to the wall must go ;
And few there are whose philanthropy believes in
that same old wall,
And, but for the praise of the sons of men. that
few were as none at all.
For the strength of life is in bitterness, and weak-
ness lies in love;
And the world weeps well for the kindly souls
with its eye on the few who shove :
For the scornful mind in its scorn is strong though
that strength may bring self-hate,
And the weakness won by its over-strength arrive
at the last too late.
Were it not for the scorn and the satire deep, and
the world's keen irony,
A doctor’s den of disease and death and a weak-
kneed race were we;
And the fight for Self, though it bears its curse,
has a strength which defies defeat,
While the tardy thoughts of benevolence are
drowned in its own dream-sweet.
’Tis the thoughts for Self, and the fears for Self,
and the deeds of the selfish throng,
With the hand of steel, and the heart of steel, and
the voice that is stern and strong
That have made the world, and the world is made,
and for why should we complain,
For it well may be were it once unmade it were
worse remade again;
And the coward mind is of all things mean, for ’tis
such that will bring disgrace,
And decline to death in a pallid woe of an erst-
while lusty race.
’Mid the doubts of men, and the tears of men,
keen-visaged Intensity
With a deep-souled eye, and a firm-set mouth,
saith—“ Such [ say .shall be ! ”
Till the thoughts that she thinks with a silent
strength, and the acts that the world ne’er
saw,
And the deeds that she doth in the midst of death
are the Universal Law !

A Night Alarm.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was after a heavy circuit-dinner at . The
hotel was more than crowded at this particular
time, and a number of the leaders of the Bar and
a few of their clerks had to content themselves
with such accommodation as the “long room
upstairs ” could afford.
In this huge apartment some lay on the table,
some under the table, some on couches, improvised
from a row of chairs, others again on the carpet —
where they might. All were more or less ready
for bed ; a few, in fact, quite overcome with sleep,
for it had been a hard day, and, so far, a wet
night . . .
Convenient enough as a “shelter-shed,” the
room was absolutely minus wash-stands, chests
of drawers, dressing-tables, and clothes-pegs. But,
so fagged was the general crowd that its members
were only too happy to kick off their boots, roll
up top-coats for pillows, “ prick for the softest
plank ” (as “ Jack ” hath it), and so, to sleep—
But, there was one dignitary among them, not
a judge, yet he travelled with a secretary ; and,
like Knight and Squire after the battle, they lay
beside each other on the cold ground. The Knight
was round and portly, the Squire lean and bony,
thus exactly reversing the conditions of Don
Quixote and Sancho Panza.
Late ! and the breathings of the “ Bar ” were
almost apoplectic ; stentorian snores woke all the
echoes of the heavily-corniced ceiling ; but there
was, for all this, one who found sleep impossible.
This was our Knight.
He was in positive, corporal pain. . . The
colic of Gil Bias on that eventful night in the
robbers’ cave was a fool to it—for Gil Bias’ colic
was a “put-up thing,” and with the ulterior
motive of escaping from the place where he was
confined ; whereas the Knight’s pain was in
earnest.
The Knight was a lineal descendant of a long
line of Irish kings, including Malachi that “wore
the collar of gold that he tuk from the proud
invader.’’
“ Marjoram ! ” cried the Knight to the Squire,
“ Marjoram ! Mr. Marjoram ! For Heaven’s sake,
quick! Have yez such a thing about yez as
matchuss ? ”
An interrupted snore, far more aggravating
than the completed article by reason of the catch
in the waist of it . . .
“ Are yez a week, Mister Marjoram ? ”
“ Ye-ye-yes, sir! What—ever —is—the—matter
—sir ? ”
“ 00-00-00-ach ! Oh ! my dear Marjoram, tis a
doyin man Oi am ! Oi didn’t loike to distorb yez
at first—ach !ah ! O’m round an’ round this dtvil
of a colly-sayum building, an’ the jooce a door can
I foind—nothing but winddws, winddws every-
where, an’ stumbling over dead corpses. Oi must
have a docther, look-at, at wannst —Oi’m swelled
to a balloon.”
“ No, sir ; can’t And my things in the dark.”
“Pfwhat! Not aiven matchuss! Why don’t
yez kape the matchuss in under yer peelow ; yer
coat Oi mane. Ah, wirra, wirra ! the saints be
merciful to us, ann the divil a match !

Melbourne is to be the head-quarters of
the Belgian South Polar Expedition, and the Bel-
giea, which sailed for the south at the end of last
month, comes on to Hobson’s Bay. She should
be leaving for the Pole just after the Yic. elec-
tions, when frozen-out politicians will be looking
out eagerly for a warmer climate.

ence Cardinal Vaughan, will make a pilgrimage in a
tody to Canterbury in September next. To free the

“During the past summer the extremely hot weather made me seriously
01. I lost all appetite, could not sleep, was incapacitated for work, and
suffered from severe pains in the head. I tried several tonics, but they did
me no good whatever. A friend, who had been similarly affected, told me
to take Ayer s Sarsaparilla, as he had done. I followed his advice, and
soon began to regain my strength, and after using three or four bottles my
health was completely restored.”
Very many people suffer from the effects of a prolonged season of warm
weather in the same way as did Mr Gough. Their blood becomes thin, food
distasteful, and life is positively burdensome.
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
makes new, pure blood; restores strength and vitality, and has been the
salvation of thousands who, prior to its use, were, as they expressed it,
both tired out and worn out.”
